iv 
ABSTRACT 
This	  master	  thesis	  investigates	  a	  strategy	  to	  standardise	  working	  processes	  
that	  support	  consistency	  in	  information	  exchange	  from	  a	  BIM	  perspective	  
for	  lighting	  designers	  in	  the	  Danish	  AEC	  industry.	  
	  
Through	  a	  state	  of	  the	  art	  review	  and	  survey	  of	  four	  industry	  experts	  from	  
three	  major	  Danish	  companies	  involved	  in	  lighting	  design,	  it	  was	  possible	  to	  
map	  down	  current	  working	  processes	  and	  necessary	  information	  needed	  to	  
perform	  certain	  lighting	  design	  activities.	  Based	  on	  the	  responses,	  which	  
revealed	  a	  general	  lack	  of	  communication	  and	  consistency	  in	  information	  
exchange;	  it	  was	  possible	  to	  develop	  two	  Information	  Delivery	  Manuals	  
(IDMs)	  according	  to	  the	  buildingSMART	  guidelines.	  These	  packages	  
describe	  architectural	  lighting	  design	  processes	  in	  the	  outline	  conceptual	  
design	  stage	  followed	  by	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage.	  Each	  IDM	  package	  
mainly	  consists	  of	  a	  Process	  Map	  displaying	  the	  current	  business	  process	  by	  
using	  Business	  Process	  Model	  and	  Notation,	  and	  defined	  Exchange	  
Requirements	  including	  Input	  Requirement	  Models	  describing	  information	  
needed	  by	  actors	  when	  initiating	  an	  activity,	  and	  Output	  Requirement	  
Models	  describing	  information	  resulting	  from	  an	  activity.	  	  
	  
Based	  on	  the	  IDM	  developed,	  it	  was	  possible	  to	  critically	  assess	  and	  analyse	  
both	  working	  processes	  as	  well	  as	  information	  exchanges	  in	  order	  to	  
suggest	  points	  of	  potential	  optimisation,	  or	  efficiency	  improvements.	  This	  
analysis	  revealed	  information	  that	  was	  often	  difficult	  to	  obtain	  or	  subject	  to	  
inaccuracies	  and	  activities	  with	  inefficient	  communication	  occurring.	  
Consequently,	  the	  authors	  of	  this	  study	  proposed	  and	  developed	  three	  tools	  
that	  can	  potentially	  streamline	  information	  exchange.	  These	  tools	  include	  
an	  illuminance	  requirement	  tool	  based	  on	  CCS	  classification;	  a	  reflectance	  
database	  with	  common	  building	  materials;	  and	  a	  proposal	  for	  a	  standard	  
light	  fixture	  datasheet	  using	  IFC	  format	  including	  suggestions	  for	  new	  IFC	  
specifications.	  
	  
Both	  the	  IDM	  and	  the	  three	  developed	  tools	  were	  tested	  on	  a	  real	  life	  case	  
study	  in	  collaboration	  with	  Ramboll.	  This	  study	  revealed	  that	  the	  IDMs	  were	  
executable	  and	  proved	  to	  be	  efficient	  with	  the	  use	  of	  the	  tools.	  By	  
identifying	  and	  testing	  the	  mapped	  workflow	  it	  is	  safe	  to	  say	  that	  this	  can	  be	  
even	  further	  optimised	  and	  effective	  if	  BIM	  working	  methods	  are	  more	  
widely	  used.	  	  
	  
The	  IDM	  packages	  developed	  in	  this	  study	  are	  valuable	  tools	  to	  ensure	  
working	  process	  standardisation	  and	  information	  exchange	  consistency	  
when	  implementing	  BIM	  working	  methods	  in	  lighting	  design.	  	  
	  
	  
Keywords:	  
Building	  Information	  Modelling	  (BIM),	  Information	  Delivery	  Manual	  (IDM),	  
Lighting	  Design,	  Process	  Map,	  Exchange	  Requirements,	  buildingSMART,	  
Industry	  Foundation	  Classes	  (IFC).

vi 
RESUME 
Dette	  speciale	  undersøger	  en	  strategi	  for	  standardisering	  af	  arbejds-­‐
processer,	  der	  understøtter	  ensartethed	  i	  informationsudveksling	  fra	  et	  BIM	  
perspektiv,	  blandt	  lysdesignere	  i	  den	  danske	  byggebranche.	  	  
	  
Gennem	  et	  litteraturstudie	  samt	  interviews	  af	  fire	  brancheeksperter	  fra	  tre	  
af	  de	  førende	  danske	  virksomheder,	  som	  er	  involveret	  i	  lysdesign,	  var	  det	  
muligt	  at	  kortlægge	  nuværende	  arbejdsprocesser	  og	  den	  nødvendige	  
informationsudveksling	  der	  skal	  til	  for	  at	  udføre	  visse	  aktiviteter	  inden	  for	  
belysningsdesign.	  På	  baggrund	  af	  eksperternes	  tilbagemeldinger,	  der	  
afslørede	  en	  generel	  mangel	  på	  kommunikation	  og	  ensartethed	  i	  
informationsudveksling,	  var	  det	  muligt	  at	  udvikle	  to	  ’Information	  Delivery	  
Manuals’	  (IDMs)	  i	  henhold	  til	  buildingSMARTs	  retningslinjer.	  Disse	  IDM	  
pakker	  beskriver	  processer	  inden	  for	  lysdesign	  i	  projektforslaget	  og	  
hovedprojektet.	  Hver	  IDM	  pakke	  består	  hovedsageligt	  af	  en	  proces-­‐
beskrivelse,	  der	  viser	  den	  nuværende	  arbejdsproces	  ved	  hjælp	  af	  BPMN,	  og	  
som	  definerer	  krav	  til	  informationsudveksling,	  herunder	  krav	  til	  ’Input	  
Requirement	  Model’	  og	  ’Output	  Requirement	  Model’,	  der	  henholdsvis	  
beskriver	  nødvendig	  information,	  der	  skal	  leveres	  for	  at	  igangsætte	  en	  
aktivitet,	  eller	  leveres	  som	  resultat	  af	  en	  aktivitet.	  	  
	  
Med	  udgangspunkt	  i	  de	  udviklede	  IDM	  pakker,	  var	  det	  muligt	  at	  vurdere	  og	  
analysere	  nuværende	  arbejdsprocesser,	  samt	  informationsudvekslinger	  
med	  henblik	  på	  at	  kortlægge	  optimeringspotentiale	  eller	  effektivisering.	  
Dette	  blotlagde	  informationer	  der	  ofte	  var	  vanskelige	  at	  fremskaffe	  eller	  var	  
upræcise	  samt	  aktiviteter,	  hvor	  der	  ofte	  opstod	  ineffektiv	  kommunikation.	  
På	  baggrund	  heraf,	  har	  forfatterne	  af	  dette	  speciale	  udviklet	  tre	  
hjælpeværktøjer,	  der	  potentielt	  kan	  strømline	  informationsudvekslingen.	  
Disse	  værktøjer	  omfatter	  en	  forbindelse	  mellem	  CCS-­‐klassifikation	  og	  
nationale	  belysningsstandarder,	  en	  database	  med	  typiske	  byggematerialer	  
og	  deres	  reflektantser	  samt	  et	  forslag	  til	  et	  standard	  lysarmaturdatablad	  
med	  brug	  af	  IFC-­‐formatet	  herunder	  forslag	  til	  nye	  IFC	  specifikationer.	  
	  
IDM-­‐pakkerne	  og	  de	  tre	  udviklede	  hjælpeværktøjer	  blev	  testet	  på	  et	  
konkret	  byggeprojekt	  i	  samarbejde	  med	  Rambøll.	  Dette	  studie	  viste,	  at	  IDM-­‐	  
pakkerne	  var	  funktionsdygtige	  og	  effektive	  ved	  brug	  af	  hjælpeværktøjerne.	  
Ved	  at	  identificere	  og	  teste	  den	  definerede	  arbejdsgang,	  konkluderes	  det,	  at	  
lysdesignprocesserne	  kan	  optimeres	  ved	  at	  standardisere	  tilgangen	  til	  
information	  og	  ved	  at	  udnytte	  potentialet	  i	  BIM-­‐arbejdsmetoder.	  	  	  
	  
IDM	  pakkerne	  som	  blev	  udviklet	  i	  dette	  speciale,	  er	  et	  værdifuldt	  redskab	  til	  
sikring	  af	  standardiserede	  arbejdsprocesser	  og	  ensartethed	  i	  informations-­‐
udveksling,	  når	  BIM	  arbejdsmetoder	  bliver	  benyttet	  i	  lysdesign.	  
	  
	  
Nøgleord:	  
Bygnings	  Informations	  Modellering	  (BIM),	  Information	  Delivery	  Manual	  
(IDM),	  Lysdesign,	  Informationsudveksling,	  buildingSMART,	  Industry	  
Foundation	  Classes	  (IFC).	  
==PAGE:8==
	  
	  vii 
==PAGE:9==
	  
	  
viii 
PREFACE 
Following	  report	  is	  carried	  out	  as	  the	  final	  part	  of	  the	  Master	  of	  Science	  
programme	  in	  Architectural	  Engineering	  at	  the	  Technical	  University	  of	  
Denmark.	  The	  thesis	  counts	  30	  ECTS	  points	  for	  each	  of	  the	  authors	  and	  
started	  in	  September	  2014.	  	  
	  
The	  two	  authors	  of	  this	  report	  are	  MSc	  Eng.	  students	  Jonathan	  Madsen	  
(s080183)	  and	  Stefan	  Pavic	  (s093350).	  
	  
Development	  of	  Information	  Delivery	  Manual	  packages	  to	  use	  in	  lighting	  
design	  disciplines	  in	  the	  architecture,	  engineering	  and	  construction	  
industry	  is	  the	  main	  focus	  of	  this	  thesis.	  The	  project	  has	  been	  part	  of	  an	  
industrial	  Ph.D.	  between	  Ramboll	  and	  DTU	  that	  has	  defined	  the	  overall	  
framework	  and	  provided	  guidelines	  for	  this	  project.	  This	  project’s	  
development	  of	  two	  IDM	  packages	  will	  therefore	  be	  utilised	  in	  the	  Ph.D.	  
study’s	  further	  work.	  	  
	  
Acknowledgements	  go	  to	  all	  persons	  involved	  in	  this	  project.	  A	  large	  credit	  
is	  in	  place	  to	  our	  supervisor,	  Associate	  Professor	  and	  Head	  of	  Section	  Jan	  
Karlshøj	  for	  his	  comprehensive	  knowledge	  on	  Building	  Information	  
Modelling	  and	  maintaining	  interoperability	  interests	  when	  developing	  the	  
Information	  Delivery	  Manual	  packages.	  Also	  the	  two	  co-­‐supervisors	  
Associate	  Professor	  Flemming	  Vestergaard	  and	  Industrial	  Ph.D.-­‐student	  
Niels	  Treldal	  has	  provided	  thorough	  guidance	  and	  helped	  set	  boundaries	  to	  
secure	  applicability	  of	  the	  Information	  Delivery	  Manuals	  for	  further	  work	  in	  
Niels’	  Ph.D.	  study.	  
	  
Furthermore	  acknowledgements	  go	  to	  all	  industry	  experts	  involved	  in	  this	  
project.	  Without	  their	  knowledge	  and	  sharing	  of	  personal	  working	  
experience	  it	  would	  not	  have	  been	  possible	  to	  complete	  this	  study.	  The	  
industry	  experts	  include	  Jørgen	  Kjer	  from	  COWI;	  Vladan	  Paunovic	  and	  
Martin	  Sømberg	  from	  Ramboll;	  and	  Kasper	  Hammer	  from	  Louis	  Poulsen.	  
Lighting	  designer	  Lisa	  Trapp	  from	  COWI	  has	  performed	  peer	  review	  on	  this	  
study	  and	  deserves	  our	  acknowledgements.	  	  	  
The	  last	  credits	  goes	  to	  the	  persons	  allocating	  valuable	  time	  for	  reviewing	  
this	  study	  and	  providing	  us	  with	  constructive	  feedback.	  These	  persons	  are:	  
architect	  Lars	  Madsen,	  B.Eng.	  Lasse	  Hamborg	  Nielsen	  and	  cand.design	  Lisa	  
Trapp.

(110	  normal	  pages)

Containing	  digital	  appendices	    
Printing	  
It	  is	  the	  purpose	  of	  this	  report	  to	  be	  printed	  in	  duplex	  
and	  colours.	  Colours	  are	  mainly	  used	  where	  necessary	  
so	  the	  report	  can	  occasionally	  work	  in	  black/white.	  
Blank	  pages	  are	  inserted	  various	  places	  to	  favour	  
duplex	  printing.	  	    
References	  
APA	  style	  is	  used	  to	  when	  other	  works	  are	  cited.	  See	  
bibliography	  in	  the	  back	  of	  this	  report	  for	  an	  alphabetic	  
list	  of	  all	  references.	  Citations	  use	  APA	  style	  as	  well.	  	  
Footnotes	  are	  occasionally	  used	  to	  support	  unique	  
statements	  from	  surveyed	  industry	  experts.	  	    
Work	  distribution	  
	  
Both	  authors	  have	  contributed	  equally	  to	  this	  study.	  	    
Figures	  
The	  authors	  have	  developed	  all	  figures	  unless	  
otherwise	  stated.

INTRODUCTION // 1 
	  
2
1. 
INRODUCTION 
The	  Architecture,	  Engineering	  and	  Construction	  (AEC)	  industry	  is	  currently	  
facing	  several	  difficulties	  when	  implementing	  Building	  Information	  
Modelling	  (BIM)	  based	  working	  processes.	  A	  lot	  of	  man-­‐hours	  are	  wasted	  
during	  a	  project	  due	  to	  working	  processes	  and	  information	  exchange,	  which	  
are	  not	  yet	  standardised.	  At	  current	  state	  it	  is	  not	  unlikely	  that	  information	  
flow	  and	  deliverables	  are	  re-­‐defined	  every	  time	  a	  new	  project	  starts	  up	  and	  
activities	  are	  mapped	  all	  over	  again	  in	  order	  to	  manage	  the	  project	  
accordingly.	  	  
	  
In	  the	  future,	  it	  is	  of	  high	  priority	  to	  standardise	  and	  ensure	  consistency	  in	  
information	  exchanged	  between	  project	  disciplines	  and	  map	  the	  overall	  
information	  needed	  to	  perform	  their	  individual	  activities.	  Interdisciplinary	  
communication	  and	  interoperability	  is	  crucial	  to	  implement	  BIM	  tools	  in	  
AEC	  projects.	  Information	  Delivery	  Manuals	  (IDM)	  developed	  by	  
buildingSMART	  is	  a	  valuable	  method	  to	  map	  down	  AEC	  project	  discipline	  
processes	  and	  thereby	  standardise	  workflow	  and	  information	  exchange	  
between	  related	  actors.	  	  
	  
IDM	  packages	  describe	  specific	  disciplines	  at	  certain	  AEC	  project	  life	  cycle	  
stages.	  One	  complete	  AEC	  project	  can	  therefore	  be	  described	  by	  breaking	  
the	  whole	  project	  down	  to	  a	  cluster	  of	  packages	  each	  representing	  one	  
working	  process.	  On	  a	  project	  management	  level	  this	  ensures	  transparency	  
and	  overview	  of	  the	  processes	  needed	  at	  specific	  life	  cycle	  stages	  and	  where	  
to	  allocate	  resources.	  From	  a	  project	  team	  member	  perspective,	  building	  up	  
the	  project	  using	  IDM	  packages	  could	  clarify	  what	  information	  should	  be	  
delivered	  to	  perform	  an	  activity	  and	  what	  kind	  of	  deliverables	  are	  expected.	  	  
	  
The	  concept	  of	  IDM	  packages	  are	  still	  sparsely	  implemented	  in	  the	  Danish	  
building	  sector,	  even	  though	  buildingSMART	  has	  a	  wide	  selection	  of	  
packages	  available.	  An	  industrial	  Ph.D.	  study	  carried	  out	  by	  Niels	  Treldal	  in	  
collaboration	  between	  Ramboll	  and	  the	  Technical	  University	  of	  Denmark	  
(DTU),	  research	  on	  the	  possibilities	  to	  create	  whole	  projects	  based	  on	  IDM	  
packages	  describing	  each	  project	  discipline	  involved.	  In	  order	  to	  map	  down	  
a	  project	  like	  that,	  it	  is	  therefore	  necessary	  to	  develop	  a	  whole	  range	  of	  IDM	  
packages	  describing	  different	  processes.	  This	  is	  what	  this	  project	  aims	  to	  
provide	  a	  small	  part	  of	  by	  describing	  processes	  in	  the	  ‘Lighting	  Design’	  
discipline	  at	  various	  life	  cycle	  stages.	  	  	  	  
	  
Lighting	  design	  is	  a	  growing	  discipline	  in	  the	  AEC	  industry.	  It	  has	  long	  been	  
a	  natural	  part	  of	  early-­‐stage	  building	  design	  processes	  in	  Denmark’s	  
surrounding	  countries	  and	  is	  gradually	  becoming	  more	  and	  more	  used	  in	  
Denmark.	  Due	  to	  a	  larger	  focus	  on	  energy	  optimising	  other	  disciplines	  such	  
as	  Heating,	  Ventilation	  and	  Air-­‐conditioning	  (HVAC),	  building	  geometry	  or	  
façade	  design,	  lighting	  design	  and	  lighting	  simulations	  currently	  tends	  to	  be	  
neglected	  and	  usually	  just	  used	  for	  documentation	  after	  the	  building	  design	  
is	  complete.	  National	  standards	  are	  evolving	  in	  one	  direction:	  more	  lighting	  
required	  for	  less	  energy	  consumed.	  Correct	  light	  planning	  can	  therefore	  
==PAGE:20==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  3
reduce	  the	  overall	  energy	  consumption	  in	  buildings	  and	  lighting	  designers	  
will	  therefore	  be	  needed	  in	  early-­‐stage	  design	  phases	  and	  help	  performing	  
decision-­‐supporting	  simulations	  alongside	  with	  other	  energy	  optimising	  
disciplines.	  Building	  performance	  simulations	  could	  potentially	  deliver	  
better	  results	  if	  the	  lighting	  design	  were	  optimised	  earlier	  in	  the	  project.	  	  
	  
As	  the	  need	  for	  lighting	  designers	  in	  the	  future	  cannot	  be	  overlooked	  a	  great	  
focus	  on	  securing	  lighting	  designer’s	  integration	  and	  collaboration	  with	  
other	  disciplines	  is	  therefore	  necessary.	  One	  way	  to	  initiate	  collaboration	  
and	  the	  use	  of	  lighting	  simulations	  earlier	  in	  the	  project	  is	  to	  use	  IDMs	  to	  
define	  what	  information	  is	  required	  to	  start	  the	  processes	  and	  how	  they	  can	  
interact	  with	  the	  other	  disciplines.	  It	  is	  believed,	  that	  by	  mapping	  the	  
lighting	  process	  and	  information	  needed	  to	  perform	  lighting	  designs,	  an	  
IDM	  will	  help	  communicate	  the	  potential	  benefit	  of	  having	  simultaneous	  
lighting	  simulations	  throughout	  the	  project.	  Simultaneous	  work	  alongside	  
with	  other	  disciplines	  and	  collaborating	  throughout	  the	  project	  all	  calls	  for	  
BIM	  implementation.	  Therefore,	  IDM	  development	  might	  be	  one	  way	  to	  
initiate	  the	  use	  of	  BIM	  working	  methods	  among	  lighting	  designers.	  	  
1.1. PROBLEM DEFINITION 
Founded	  in	  the	  BIM	  cluster	  project	  carried	  out	  in	  collaboration	  with	  
Ramboll	  and	  DTU	  Department	  of	  Civil	  Engineering	  during	  the	  autumn	  2014,	  
this	  master	  thesis	  will	  develop	  two	  consecutive	  IDM	  packages	  needed	  to	  
perform	  an	  architectural	  lighting	  process	  resulting	  in	  streamlined	  
information	  exchange.	  
	  
This	  project	  aims	  to	  answer	  the	  following	  problem	  formulation:	  
	  
How	  can	  architectural	  lighting	  design	  discipline	  in	  outline	  conceptual	  and	  
coordinated	  design	  stages	  benefit	  from	  using	  IDM	  packages	  to	  streamline	  and	  
simplify	  working	  processes	  and	  information	  exchange?	  	  
By	  developing	  IDM	  packages,	  is	  it	  possible	  to	  optimise	  current	  workflows	  and	  
support	  implementation	  of	  BIM?	  
	  
By	  ‘architectural	  lighting	  design’	  means	  the	  design	  of	  artificial	  lighting	  
including	  selection	  and	  placement	  of	  artificial	  lighting	  fixtures,	  qualitative	  
and	  quantitative	  design	  parameters,	  and	  definition	  of	  lighting	  controls	  
directly	  affiliated	  with	  the	  fixtures	  to	  obtain	  required	  light	  levels.	  This	  
project	  does	  not	  cover	  other	  engineering	  disciplines	  or	  affiliated	  activities	  
such	  as	  daylight	  design,	  electrical	  engineering	  and	  architectural	  design.	  
When	  designing	  IDM	  packages	  they	  will	  be	  limited	  to	  developing	  a	  Use	  Case,	  
Process	  Map	  and	  Exchange	  Requirements	  consisting	  Input	  Requirement	  
Models	  and	  Output	  Requirement	  Models.	  Further	  work	  with	  the	  IDM	  
packages	  such	  as	  developing	  a	  Model	  View	  Definition	  (MVD)	  is	  not	  covered	  
in	  this	  project.	  	  
==PAGE:21==
INTRODUCTION // 1 
	  
4
1.2. PROJECT APPROACH 
When	  fulfilling	  the	  problem	  formulation	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  go	  through	  five	  
steps	  before	  making	  any	  conclusions.	  The	  five	  steps	  covered	  in	  this	  project	  
are	  illustrated	  in	  Figure	  1.	  
	  
	  
Figure	  1	  -­‐	  Diagram	  showing	  the	  five	  study	  constituents	  
The	  first	  part	  of	  the	  project	  will	  undergo	  a	  thorough	  state-­‐of-­‐the-­‐art	  review	  
focused	  on	  existing	  knowledge	  within	  the	  use	  of	  BIM	  in	  lighting	  design;	  
existing	  methods	  and	  formats	  to	  support	  interoperability	  and	  consistency;	  
then	  a	  thorough	  IDM	  description	  of	  the	  IDM	  framework;	  and	  basic	  theory	  
about	  lighting	  design	  and	  current	  working	  processes.	  The	  review	  aims	  to	  
identify	  current	  approaches	  and	  to	  ask	  critical	  questions.	  Existing	  literature	  
will	  be	  selected	  based	  on	  its	  relevance	  to	  IDM	  development	  and	  lighting	  
design	  processes	  in	  AEC	  projects.	  	  
	  
Consecutive	  to	  the	  literature	  review	  it	  is	  essential	  to	  interview	  leading	  
professionals	  within	  the	  lighting	  industry	  in	  Denmark	  to	  indicate	  current	  
working	  processes	  and	  compare	  with	  theoretical	  knowledge.	  Development	  
of	  IDM	  packages	  will	  be	  based	  on	  current	  approaches	  why	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  
map	  the	  professional’s	  current	  approach	  and	  their	  view	  on	  using	  BIM	  in	  
lighting	  design.	  	  	  
	  
After	  existing	  working	  processes	  and	  information	  exchanges	  are	  mapped	  
the	  IDMs	  can	  be	  developed.	  The	  IDMs	  will	  be	  based	  on	  the	  IDM	  framework	  
methodology	  in	  (Mondrup,	  Treldal,	  Karlshøj,	  &	  Vestergaard,	  2014a).	  Two	  
IDM	  packages	  will	  be	  created	  representing	  architectural	  lighting	  design	  
processes	  in	  two	  consecutive	  life	  cycle	  stages.	  	  
	  
Mapping	  down	  the	  processes	  will	  eventually	  reveal	  current	  workflows,	  
which	  might	  be	  subject	  for	  optimisation.	  Fourth	  step	  in	  this	  study	  will	  
therefore	  suggest	  points	  of	  improvements	  and	  develop	  tools	  that	  can	  secure	  
a	  more	  streamlined	  information	  exchange	  and	  effective	  workflow.	  	  
	  
Finally,	  the	  packages	  will	  be	  tested	  in	  a	  case	  study	  of	  a	  real	  world	  AEC	  
project	  provided	  by	  Ramboll.	  This	  case	  study	  will	  test	  the	  adaptability	  of	  
IDM	  packages	  and	  exemplify	  information	  exchange	  with	  the	  use	  of	  the	  
previous	  developed	  tools	  or	  optimisation	  methods.	  
	  
Enjoy	  reading	  the	  study!

FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  5

STATE OF THE ART
REVIEW

==PAGE:25==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
6
2. 
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW 
The	  following	  chapter	  aims	  to	  provide	  the	  reader	  with	  an	  in-­‐depth	  
presentation	  of	  various	  topics	  necessary	  to	  understand	  the	  scope	  of	  this	  
study.	  A	  state	  of	  the	  art	  review	  is	  a	  large	  part	  of	  fulfilling	  the	  problem	  
definition,	  as	  gaining	  the	  most	  updated	  knowledge	  is	  mandatory	  to	  critically	  
assess	  any	  problems	  within	  the	  intersection	  of	  Information	  Delivery	  
Manuals	  (IDM)	  and	  lighting	  design.	  Even	  though	  the	  state	  of	  the	  art	  review	  
aims	  to	  provide	  general	  knowledge,	  it	  has	  been	  narrowed	  down	  to	  only	  
include	  relevant	  topics	  for	  this	  study.	  It	  is	  therefore	  prerequisite	  for	  the	  
reader	  to	  have	  general	  background	  knowledge	  about	  Building	  Information	  
Modelling	  (BIM)	  and	  a	  general	  understanding	  on	  how	  the	  building	  sector	  
works.	  
2.1. BUILDING INFORMATION MODELLING IN DENMARK 
During	  the	  2000’s,	  many	  things	  happened	  with	  the	  otherwise	  very	  
conservative	  building	  sector	  in	  Denmark.	  Radical	  changes	  has	  been	  
attempted	  in	  order	  to	  make	  the	  industry	  more	  efficient	  and	  innovative.	  Cut	  
down	  costs	  or	  ‘make	  the	  most	  of	  money	  spent’	  has	  been	  the	  core	  intentions	  
for	  changing	  working	  methods.	  Especially	  the	  recession	  in	  2008	  pushed	  
companies	  all	  over	  the	  industry	  to	  look	  inwards	  and	  improve	  own	  working	  
methods.	  Many	  actors	  in	  the	  Architecture,	  Engineering	  and	  Construction	  
(AEC)	  industry	  were	  affected	  badly	  and	  had	  to	  declare	  bankruptcy.	  One	  of	  
the	  methods	  that	  could	  prove	  as	  a	  potential	  solution	  is	  according	  to	  (Azhar,	  
2011)	  to	  reduce	  costs	  and	  increase	  productivity	  and	  quality	  was	  the	  
Building	  Information	  Modelling	  (BIM)	  method.	  	  
	  
BIM	  was	  already	  implemented	  in	  the	  Danish	  AEC	  industry	  in	  2003	  with	  the	  
project	  ‘Det	  Digitale	  Byggeri’	  (bips,	  2014).	  The	  Danish	  government	  initiated	  
this	  project	  by	  looking	  into	  other	  countries’	  experiences	  with	  digitalisation	  
of	  the	  building	  sector.	  The	  hypothesis	  was,	  that	  through	  digitalisation	  the	  
AEC	  industry	  could	  experience	  fewer	  errors,	  cost	  overruns	  and	  delays.	  	  
	  
Main	  purpose	  of	  ‘Det	  Digitale	  Byggeri’	  was	  to	  define	  a	  number	  of	  
requirements	  for	  consultants	  and	  contractors	  through	  the	  use	  of	  
Information	  and	  Communication	  Technology	  (ICT).	  These	  requirements	  
should	  be	  followed	  only	  if	  the	  building	  owner	  made	  that	  prerequisite.	  Later	  
on,	  in	  2007,	  the	  government	  demanded	  all	  public	  projects	  to	  follow	  
requirements	  according	  to	  the	  ICT.	  That	  transition	  made	  it	  compulsory	  for	  
consultants	  in	  the	  AEC	  industry	  to	  use	  some	  kind	  of	  digital	  and/or	  3D	  tool	  
to	  be	  able	  to	  bid	  on	  public	  projects	  (Ussing	  &	  Larsen,	  2014).	  This	  helped	  
encourage	  the	  adaption	  of	  digitalised	  working	  methods	  and	  presumably	  
increased	  efficiency	  and	  quality	  of	  AEC	  projects	  in	  Denmark,	  by	  providing	  
actors	  a	  framework	  on	  how	  to	  structure	  their	  digital	  projects,	  information	  
exchange	  and	  deliverables,	  among	  others.	  	  	  
2.1.1. SCOPE OF IMPLEMENTATION 
The	  Danish	  building	  sector	  is	  known	  to	  be	  very	  tradition-­‐bound	  and	  over	  
time	  there	  have	  been	  few	  radical	  developments	  in	  the	  industry.	  The	  largest	  
==PAGE:26==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  7
change	  in	  newer	  times	  was	  the	  transition	  to	  and	  implementation	  of	  
Computer	  Aided	  Design	  (CAD)	  in	  the	  1990’s.	  Since	  BIM	  requires	  brand	  new	  
working	  methods	  and	  potentially	  new	  responsibilities,	  it	  has	  been	  a	  difficult	  
task	  to	  implement	  BIM	  thoroughly	  in	  Denmark.	  On	  a	  strategic	  level	  it	  has	  
been	  a	  general	  conviction	  that	  all	  stakeholders	  in	  the	  sector	  would	  benefit	  
from	  BIM	  to	  gain	  both	  better	  economy	  and	  sustainable	  construction	  
projects.	  However,	  still	  there	  are	  some	  misbelievers	  (Hermund,	  2009).	  	  
	  
The	  AEC	  industry	  is	  facing	  a	  general	  challenge:	  most	  projects	  are	  unique	  
and	  carried	  out	  once.	  Processes	  are	  difficult	  to	  streamline,	  as	  each	  building	  
is	  unique.	  Compared	  to	  other	  industries	  where	  3D	  projecting	  is	  widely	  used,	  
such	  as	  aerospace	  or	  automotive	  industries,	  it	  is	  difficult	  to	  perform	  a	  
standardised	  assembly	  line	  production	  in	  AEC	  projects.	  Because	  of	  the	  
projects	  being	  unique	  the	  contractual	  issues	  are	  usually	  different	  from	  
project	  to	  project,	  hence	  it	  is	  a	  challenge	  to	  standardise	  the	  processes	  
according	  to	  ICT	  (Ussing	  &	  Larsen,	  2014).	  
2.1.2. KEY ACTORS 
Main	  stakeholders/actors	  involved	  in	  BIM	  working	  methods	  in	  the	  Danish	  
building	  sector	  are	  developers/building	  owners,	  engineers,	  architects,	  
contractors	  and	  suppliers.	  All	  the	  stakeholders	  have	  an	  individual	  approach	  
to	  the	  BIM	  method	  since	  BIM	  provides	  different	  values	  depending	  on	  the	  
point	  of	  responsibility.	  	  
Building owner 
A	  primary	  pioneer	  of	  implementing	  BIM	  in	  the	  Danish	  building	  sector	  is	  the	  
building	  owner.	  A	  building	  owner	  is	  defining	  the	  requirements	  and	  
demands	  of	  the	  building	  project,	  hence	  also	  the	  execution	  of	  it.	  If	  they	  see	  
potential	  in	  cost	  reduction	  or	  time	  optimisation,	  building	  owners	  can	  be	  
encouraged	  to	  demand	  the	  use	  of	  BIM	  to	  comply	  with	  ICT	  requirements	  in	  
their	  projects.	  Building	  owners	  are	  the	  stakeholders	  with	  the	  largest	  
benefits	  and	  influence	  of	  BIM	  implementation	  in	  Denmark.	  The	  Danish	  
government,	  which	  is	  the	  largest	  building	  owner	  in	  Denmark,	  has	  
demanded	  the	  use	  of	  ICT	  in	  all	  public	  projects	  since	  2007	  (Ussing	  &	  Larsen,	  
2014)	  (Danish	  Ministry	  of	  Climate,	  Energy	  and	  Building,	  2014).	  	  
Architects 
According	  to	  (Hermund,	  2009),	  architects	  are	  the	  stakeholders	  who	  are	  
currently	  not	  convinced	  about	  using	  BIM	  working	  methods	  even	  though	  
BIM	  implementation	  relies	  on	  them	  to	  use	  it	  properly.	  They	  do	  not	  see	  BIM	  
being	  beneficial	  for	  their	  work	  and	  it	  is	  generally	  believed	  that	  their	  design	  
freedom	  in	  their	  work	  become	  more	  locked	  if	  using	  BIM.	  Using	  BIM	  
methods	  in	  projects	  requires	  more	  work	  from	  the	  architects	  and	  usually	  the	  
architect	  will	  be	  responsible	  for	  both	  delivering	  excessive	  information	  to	  
other	  disciplines	  and	  be	  more	  accountant	  for	  drawing	  details.	  	  	  
Engineers 
Engineers,	  however,	  are	  stakeholders	  that	  look	  more	  positively	  on	  the	  BIM	  
method.	  As	  BIM	  ensures	  consistency	  in	  a	  shared	  3D	  model,	  engineers	  could	  
benefit	  from	  extracting	  and	  providing	  information	  to	  a	  central	  architectural	  
==PAGE:27==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
8
model	  that	  will	  make	  the	  simulation	  and	  calculating	  work	  of	  engineers	  
much	  more	  efficient.	  But	  it	  seems	  like	  engineers	  are	  not	  interested	  in	  
‘loosing’	  hours	  of	  consultancy,	  why	  these	  hours	  has	  to	  be	  allocated	  to,	  for	  
example	  optimisation	  of	  the	  solution	  or	  quality	  assurance	  and	  verification	  
of	  the	  input	  data	  (Azhar,	  2011).	  
Contractors 
Costs	  overruns	  and	  construction	  delays	  can	  significantly	  affect	  the	  quality	  of	  
the	  finished	  design.	  This	  is	  often	  due	  to	  insufficient	  information	  provided	  by	  
the	  building	  owner	  to	  the	  contractor.	  Insufficient	  information	  makes	  it	  
difficult	  for	  contractors	  to	  make	  correct	  cost	  estimates	  or	  make	  time	  
schedules.	  Contractors	  begin	  to	  see	  the	  value	  of	  BIM	  by	  having	  streamlined	  
information	  exchange	  with	  the	  developers	  and	  consultants	  (MT	  Højgaard,	  
2014).	  BIM	  does	  not	  only	  benefit	  contractors	  in	  the	  bidding	  phase	  but	  also	  
in	  the	  execution	  and	  construction.	  Clash	  tests	  are	  much	  easier	  and	  less	  
costly	  to	  initiate	  in	  a	  virtual	  3D	  environment	  rather	  than	  on	  site.	  In	  theory,	  
it	  will	  be	  the	  contractors	  that	  could	  benefit	  the	  most	  from	  BIM	  (bips,	  2007).	  	  
Suppliers 
Suppliers	  may	  also	  benefit	  from	  BIM,	  however	  it	  is	  difficult	  for	  small	  
businesses	  to	  join	  the	  BIM	  movement	  because	  of	  relatively	  high	  initial	  
finance	  or	  time	  resources	  required	  (Ussing	  &	  Larsen,	  2014).	  It	  is	  necessary	  
for	  suppliers	  to	  develop	  and	  maintain	  a	  BIM-­‐based	  database	  containing	  
relevant	  information	  about	  their	  products.	  It	  seems	  to	  be	  a	  growing	  
tendency	  for	  the	  larger	  suppliers	  that	  BIM	  is	  an	  integrated	  part	  of	  the	  
business	  model.	  	  
2.1.3. EXPERIENCES WITH IMPLEMENTING BIM 
Theoretically	  the	  BIM	  method	  is	  a	  great	  opportunity	  to	  innovate	  the	  AEC	  
industry	  and	  to	  make	  it	  more	  efficient	  and	  minimise	  faults	  and	  errors.	  An	  
industrial	  Ph.D.	  study	  in	  2014	  (MT	  Højgaard,	  2014)	  studied	  design	  material	  
of	  100	  Danish	  construction	  projects	  to	  assess	  to	  which	  extent	  the	  use	  of	  BIM	  
and	  ICT	  affects	  the	  quality	  of	  design.	  The	  following	  are	  some	  key	  points	  
observed	  in	  this	  study.	  	  
	  
The	  study	  evaluated	  the	  quality	  of	  design	  proposals	  and	  divided	  it	  into	  
three	  subcategories:	  low,	  medium	  and	  high	  quality.	  The	  survey	  shows	  that	  
63	  %	  of	  the	  selected	  projects	  were	  classified	  as	  low	  or	  medium	  quality	  and	  
had	  faults	  in	  their	  design	  material	  so	  that	  it	  needed	  to	  be	  partially	  or	  
completely	  re-­‐designed.	  These	  design	  faults	  typically	  result	  in	  ineffective	  or	  
economically	  vulnerable	  projects.	  Lack	  of	  information	  in	  the	  design	  
material	  was	  the	  key	  reason	  for	  the	  bad	  design	  quality.	  	  
	  
BIM	  is	  anticipated	  to	  be	  a	  solution	  for	  the	  lack	  of	  information	  content.	  
Around	  half	  of	  the	  surveyed	  100	  projects	  used	  BIM-­‐supported	  tools	  in	  the	  
design	  material.	  Of	  all	  the	  projects	  with	  the	  highest	  quality	  classification	  
BIM-­‐supported	  tools	  were	  used	  in	  85	  %	  of	  those	  projects	  (MT	  Højgaard,	  
2014).	  This	  support	  the	  hypothesis	  that	  BIM	  are	  a	  useful	  way	  to	  ensure	  
proper	  design	  coordination	  and	  information	  exchange.	  Contrary	  to	  this,	  
==PAGE:28==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  9
non-­‐BIM	  2D	  material	  was	  the	  basis	  of	  79	  %	  of	  all	  the	  projects	  that	  was	  
classified	  as	  low	  quality	  (MT	  Højgaard,	  2014).	  	  
	  
Even	  though	  BIM	  models	  are	  widely	  used,	  the	  consultants	  tend	  to	  keep	  the	  
models	  for	  themselves.	  In	  39	  %	  of	  the	  surveyed	  projects	  no	  BIM	  model	  
were	  delivered	  to	  the	  contractors	  to	  be	  used	  as	  construction	  drawings	  even	  
though,	  almost	  74	  %	  of	  the	  2D	  drawings	  delivered	  were	  created	  based	  on	  a	  
prior	  BIM	  model.	  One	  could	  ask	  the	  question:	  “why	  is	  the	  BIM	  model	  with	  
all	  its	  relevant	  information	  not	  surpassed	  and	  utilised	  even	  further?”	  
Entrepreneurs	  and	  contractors	  are	  subject	  to	  work	  in	  a	  3D	  environment	  to	  
get	  insight	  in	  the	  project	  and	  to	  influence	  design	  decisions.	  It	  is	  a	  very	  
common	  practice	  that	  2D	  drawings	  are	  still	  the	  foundation	  in	  consultancy	  
deliverables,	  even	  though	  a	  BIM	  model	  has	  been	  created	  or	  not	  (MT	  
Højgaard,	  2014).	  	  
	  
The	  ICT	  agreement	  is	  the	  foundation	  of	  the	  digitalised	  construction	  sector	  
in	  Denmark.	  ICT	  defines	  common	  requirements	  to	  the	  execution	  and	  
deliverables	  related	  to	  a	  digitalised	  BIM	  model	  in	  a	  project.	  ICT	  is	  not	  
mandatory	  to	  use	  (only	  on	  public	  projects	  over	  20	  million	  DKK)	  and	  
therefore	  only	  57	  %	  of	  the	  surveyed	  projects	  with	  BIM	  models	  currently	  use	  
ICT	  (MT	  Højgaard,	  2014).	  ICT	  requires	  deliverables	  using	  a	  common	  file	  
format,	  hence	  ensuring	  interoperability	  between	  actors	  and	  software.	  77	  %	  
of	  all	  projects	  that	  used	  ICT	  got	  a	  high	  design	  quality	  classification	  (MT	  
Højgaard,	  2014).	  	  
	  
Based	  on	  the	  survey	  it	  is	  hard	  to	  conclude	  whether	  BIM	  implementation	  in	  
Denmark	  is	  successful.	  It	  is	  definitely	  present,	  both	  3D	  modelling	  and	  use	  of	  
ICT	  agreements.	  But	  still	  the	  design	  quality	  is	  not	  good	  enough	  which	  could	  
be	  related	  to	  the	  extensive	  use	  of	  2D	  design	  material	  and	  lack	  of	  
coordination	  between	  design	  disciplines.	  3D	  modelling	  has	  a	  direct	  positive	  
impact	  on	  the	  quality	  of	  design	  material	  but	  there	  is	  room	  to	  improve	  by	  
making	  ICT	  requirements	  more	  mandatory	  (MT	  Højgaard,	  2014).	  In	  
general,	  the	  potential	  of	  BIM	  and	  high	  quality	  of	  design	  information	  is	  not	  
utilised	  enough.	  Still	  large	  efforts	  have	  to	  be	  made	  by	  both	  consultants	  and	  
contractors	  across	  disciplines	  to	  ensure	  quality	  checks	  and	  validation	  of	  
their	  own	  models.	  
2.1.4. KEY CHALLENGES  
The	  idea	  of	  working	  from	  a	  common	  BIM	  model	  all	  actors	  involved	  in	  an	  
AEC	  project	  work	  from,	  have	  many	  good	  qualities.	  This	  requires	  
reorganization	  and	  clear	  definition	  of	  responsibilities.	  Engineers	  especially	  
benefit	  from	  a	  central	  BIM	  model,	  since	  much	  of	  the	  design	  and	  simulation	  
work	  is	  performed	  based	  on	  or	  extracted	  from	  a	  central	  BIM	  model.	  
Activities	  that	  especially	  benefit	  from	  a	  central	  BIM	  model	  are	  structural	  
analysis,	  installations	  coordination	  and	  energy	  simulations.	  	  
	  
Even	  though	  productivity	  and	  economic	  benefits	  of	  using	  BIM	  is	  clear	  to	  the	  
majority	  of	  the	  Danish	  AEC	  industry	  there	  are	  still	  challenges	  to	  be	  faced	  in	  
==PAGE:29==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
10
order	  to	  implement	  it	  fully.	  According	  to	  (Azhar,	  2011)	  there	  are	  generally	  
two	  main	  challenges	  to	  be	  faced:	  technical	  and	  managerial	  challenges	  	  
	  
Technical	  issues	  include	  difficulties	  with	  software	  packages	  and	  technical	  
interoperability.	  Data	  interoperability	  is	  a	  problem	  that	  has	  to	  be	  solved.	  
Many	  BIM-­‐supporting	  software	  packages	  are	  available	  on	  the	  market,	  which	  
ensures	  open	  competition	  and	  market-­‐driven	  pricing.	  But	  the	  different	  
software	  packages	  have	  to	  work	  together	  to	  ensure	  seamless	  cooperation	  
across	  companies.	  This	  is	  where	  data	  interoperability	  becomes	  a	  solution	  
but	  still	  a	  challenge.	  Industry	  Foundation	  Classes	  (IFC)	  file	  format	  is	  a	  way	  
to	  solve	  this	  problem.	  This	  will	  be	  further	  covered	  later	  in	  sub-­‐section	  2.1.6.	  	  
	  
Managerial	  challenges	  are	  related	  to	  the	  implementation	  of	  BIM	  on	  strategic	  
levels.	  Something	  that	  is	  quite	  unclear	  in	  the	  industry	  is	  how	  to	  use	  BIM	  and	  
how	  to	  change	  working	  methods	  according	  to	  BIM	  (Azhar,	  2011).	  ICT	  
agreements	  are	  one	  way	  to	  solve	  standardisation	  of	  deliverables	  and	  
information	  exchange,	  but	  when	  there	  is	  no	  requirement	  to	  follow	  ICT	  on	  all	  
projects,	  it	  is	  difficult	  to	  fully	  implement	  it.	  Legal	  issues	  could	  also	  be	  
problems	  on	  managerial	  level.	  Ownership	  and	  legal	  responsibility	  of	  
elements	  in	  a	  common	  BIM	  model	  seems	  unclear,	  as	  everyone	  is	  able	  to	  
change,	  add	  and	  delete.	  This	  leaves	  much	  more	  responsibility	  on	  an	  overall	  
BIM	  manager	  or	  project	  manager	  to	  have	  the	  full	  overview	  of	  all	  involved	  
actors	  (Ussing	  &	  Larsen,	  2014).	  	  
	  
Another	  large	  key	  challenge	  with	  BIM	  is	  that	  the	  whole	  industry	  is	  not	  
convinced	  about	  using	  BIM	  yet.	  They	  state	  in	  (MT	  Højgaard,	  2014)	  that	  a	  
common	  agreement	  has	  to	  be	  taken	  to	  switch	  to	  BIM	  working	  methods	  in	  
order	  to	  fully	  utilise	  the	  potential.	  In	  particular,	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  convince	  
architects	  and	  contractors	  of	  BIM,	  as	  those	  actors	  see	  BIM	  as	  an	  expense	  for	  
their	  work.	  BIM	  can	  complicate	  many	  of	  their	  usual	  working	  routines	  
(Hermund,	  2009).	  Architects	  are	  with	  the	  BIM	  method	  required	  to	  provide	  
more	  detailed	  architectural	  models	  earlier	  in	  the	  project,	  which	  requires	  
much	  more	  resources.	  According	  to	  (Hermund,	  2009)	  a	  part	  of	  detailing	  the	  
architectural	  model	  also	  means	  that	  architects	  gets	  responsibility	  to	  assign	  
properties	  for	  a	  number	  of	  building	  elements	  in	  the	  model,	  for	  instance	  
properties	  of	  space,	  structures,	  facades,	  windows,	  etc.	  	  
	  
If	  architects	  do	  not	  see	  any	  particular	  benefits	  from	  using	  BIM	  it	  is	  a	  great	  
challenge	  to	  implement	  BIM	  in	  the	  industry.	  Architects	  are	  an	  important	  
key	  actor	  in	  BIM	  method	  implementation	  but	  they	  believe	  they	  are	  not	  
getting	  credited	  enough	  with	  the	  work	  they	  put	  into	  it	  (Andersen,	  2011).	  
	  
Most	  of	  all,	  it	  is	  necessary	  that	  all	  parties	  in	  the	  AEC	  industry,	  including	  
contractors,	  verify	  the	  quality	  of	  design	  information	  they	  use	  in	  the	  same	  
way	  as	  physical	  products	  delivered	  to	  construction	  sites	  are	  quality	  
assured.	  A	  collection	  about	  the	  results	  of	  good	  design	  information	  quality	  
will	  eventually	  increase	  productivity	  for	  the	  entire	  construction	  industry	  
(MT	  Højgaard,	  2014).	  
==PAGE:30==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  11 
2.1.5. BARRIERS WHEN IMPLEMENTING BIM 
There	  are	  some	  barriers	  to	  the	  adoption	  of	  BIM.	  First	  and	  foremost	  changes	  
has	  to	  be	  made	  on	  how	  and	  when	  information	  within	  an	  AEC	  project	  should	  
be	  delivered.	  To	  this,	  a	  barrier	  in	  the	  economy	  can	  be	  added,	  as	  more	  
detailed	  work	  usually	  requires	  more	  man-­‐hours.	  	  	  
	  
Looking	  at	  the	  economy,	  there	  is	  a	  big	  cost	  for	  the	  companies	  to	  invest	  in	  
new	  software	  that	  can	  handle	  BIM	  models.	  This	  is	  especially	  significant	  for	  
small	  companies	  (Azhar,	  2011).	  There	  may	  be	  expenses	  related	  to	  schooling	  
employees	  from	  working	  with	  2D	  drawings	  to	  BIM.	  Schooling	  employees	  
also	  means	  reduced	  productivity	  for	  other	  profitable	  tasks	  in	  those	  periods	  
resulting	  in	  profit	  loss.	  
	  
It	  is	  a	  barrier	  when	  changing	  standardised	  working	  methods	  for	  the	  sake	  of	  
new	  working	  methods	  such	  as	  BIM.	  An	  argument	  often	  heard	  is	  that	  it	  is	  
difficult	  to	  move	  from	  2D	  drawings	  to	  BIM,	  which	  requires	  a	  lot	  of	  effort,	  
especially	  in	  the	  older	  generations	  within	  the	  industry	  (Ussing	  &	  Larsen,	  
2014).	  New	  changes	  can	  be	  deemed	  as	  ‘unsafe’	  and	  employees	  can	  lose	  
motivation	  while	  transitioning.	  BIM	  models	  also	  tend	  to	  include	  much	  more	  
information	  than	  its	  predecessor,	  why	  people	  are	  tending	  to	  feel	  more	  
responsible	  and	  accountable	  for	  information	  provided	  into	  the	  model.	  For	  
instance,	  engineers	  have	  to	  design	  and	  specify	  technical	  installations	  down	  
to	  the	  smallest	  objects	  –	  something	  that	  used	  to	  be	  taken	  care	  of	  by	  
contractors	  or	  craftsmen	  on	  site.	  	  
	  
Finally,	  there	  are	  the	  issues	  with	  software	  that	  support	  businesses	  to	  
manage	  BIM,	  which	  is	  still	  under	  development.	  Many	  available	  programs	  
support	  the	  modelling	  of	  BIM.	  Only	  recently,	  there	  has	  been	  an	  
acknowledgement	  that	  these	  programs	  should	  support	  the	  data	  exchange	  
between	  different	  applications	  as	  well.	  This	  way,	  the	  whole	  industry	  is	  not	  
reliant	  on	  a	  single	  software	  supplier	  (Ussing	  &	  Larsen,	  2014).	  	  
	  
BIM	  models	  of	  buildings	  offer	  a	  technological	  opportunity	  to	  improve	  the	  
quality	  of	  the	  contractor's	  work	  basis.	  But	  the	  potential	  of	  better	  design	  
quality	  of	  information	  is	  still	  largely	  untapped	  in	  the	  Danish	  construction	  
industry	  (MT	  Højgaard,	  2014).	  As	  Figure	  2	  shows,	  the	  learning	  curve	  
towards	  utilising	  the	  full	  potential	  of	  BIM	  is	  long	  and	  not	  straightforward.	  
The	  ‘expected’	  curve	  on	  Figure	  2	  is	  what	  most	  organisations	  expect	  to	  take.	  
The	  ‘Actual’	  curve	  is	  the	  unavoidable	  learning	  curve	  happening	  for	  most	  
organisations.	  The	  ‘Optimal’	  learning	  curve	  is	  when	  proper	  change	  
management	  and	  implementation	  among	  employees	  are	  present.	  The	  
‘inexpedient’	  curve	  happens	  if	  implementation	  is	  unsuccessful	  and	  the	  
capabilities	  are	  reduced	  as	  a	  reaction	  of	  this.	  Expectations	  are	  always	  that	  
implementation	  will	  happen	  quick	  and	  effortless	  to	  reach	  the	  full	  BIM	  
capability	  but	  the	  actual	  learning	  curve	  shows,	  that	  the	  company	  will	  soon	  
after	  implementation	  reach	  a	  ‘valley’	  filled	  with	  challenges	  and	  stress	  on	  the	  
organisation	  (Oakley,	  2012).	  This	  is	  according	  to	  (Oakley,	  2012)	  why	  a	  slow	  
implementation	  of	  BIM	  focused	  on	  single	  projects	  and/or	  chosen	  employees	  
is	  essential.	  More	  efforts	  has	  to	  be	  taken	  to	  motivate	  and	  educate	  
==PAGE:31==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
12
employees,	  change	  working	  methods	  and	  be	  very	  clear	  about	  the	  roles	  and	  
responsibilities	  of	  all	  actors	  involved	  in	  a	  BIM	  project	  (Mondrup,	  Karlshøj,	  &	  
Vestergaard,	  2013).	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  2	  –	  Learning	  curve	  of	  BIM	  implementation.	  [Inspired	  by	  (Oakley,	  2012)	  and	  (Mondrup, Karlshøj, 
& Vestergaard, 2014b)]	  
2.1.6. INTEROPERABILITY IN BIM 
As	  previously	  mentioned,	  lack	  of	  information	  exchange	  standards	  is	  one	  
major	  issue	  and	  barrier	  towards	  fully	  utilised	  BIM	  working	  methods.	  BIM	  
offers	  a	  massive	  collection	  of	  data	  within	  AEC	  projects,	  which	  in	  theory	  
could	  be	  utilised	  more	  by	  project	  team	  members	  through	  more	  consistent	  
communication	  and	  interoperability	  between	  software	  platforms	  (Karlshøj	  
&	  Berard,	  2011).	  	  
	  
In	  Denmark’s	  nearest	  neighbour,	  Sweden,	  a	  study	  was	  completed	  in	  2012	  
(Hooper	  &	  Ekholm,	  2012)	  that	  identified	  the	  technical	  barrier	  
(interoperability)	  and	  the	  non-­‐technical	  barrier	  (organisational	  and	  team	  
communication)	  for	  being	  the	  two	  main	  barriers	  that	  are	  the	  core	  reasons	  
for	  lack	  of	  full	  utilisation	  of	  BIM	  information.	  	  
	  
One	  solution	  to	  solve	  technical	  barriers	  and	  interoperability	  issues	  is	  
through	  a	  standardised	  common	  framework	  for	  data	  exchange	  and	  file	  
formats.	  If	  several	  actors	  working	  together	  on	  a	  project	  share	  one	  central	  
BIM	  model,	  it	  is	  essential	  that	  data	  exchanged	  to/from	  this	  model	  is	  
independent	  of	  software	  platform.	  It	  does	  not	  serve	  any	  good	  to	  have	  a	  large	  
number	  of	  software	  applications	  that	  cannot	  interact	  or	  use	  information	  
across	  platforms.	  	  
	  
Industry	  Foundation	  Classes	  (IFC)	  is	  an	  open	  and	  neutral	  file	  format	  that	  
aims	  to	  solve	  issues	  with	  technical	  operability.	  It	  is	  initially	  developed	  by	  
the	  International	  Alliance	  for	  Interoperability	  but	  nowadays	  managed	  by	  
buildingSMART	  and	  later	  on	  acclaimed	  by	  ISO	  (ISO,	  2013).	  It	  serves	  as	  being	  
the	  primary	  neutral	  file	  format	  used	  in	  the	  AEC	  industry	  to	  provide	  a	  
universal	  basis	  for	  process	  improvement	  and	  information	  exchange.	  	  
==PAGE:32==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  13 
The	  use	  of	  IFC	  in	  Denmark	  is	  widely	  acknowledged	  primarily	  because	  of	  the	  
requirements	  to	  use	  IFC	  file	  format	  on	  all	  AEC	  projects	  using	  ICT.	  IFC	  is	  
object-­‐based,	  meaning	  that	  IFC	  translates	  all	  information	  relevant	  to	  an	  
object	  into	  this	  neutral	  language.	  IFC	  is	  based	  on	  an	  EXPRESS	  data	  
specification	  language	  that	  the	  major	  BIM	  software	  packages	  can	  read	  and	  
interpret.	  	  	  
	  
In	  general,	  IFC	  has	  been	  positively	  met	  by	  the	  Danish	  AEC	  industry.	  
Architects	  see	  IFC	  as	  an	  inevitable	  way	  of	  communicating	  data	  to	  engineers.	  
The	  export	  functions	  of	  architectural	  models	  to	  IFC	  are	  simple	  and	  inclusive.	  
However,	  the	  level	  of	  details	  can	  be	  reduced	  when	  importing	  IFC	  models	  
into	  other	  tools	  like	  AutoCAD	  for	  instance	  (bips,	  2007).	  A	  common	  
workaround	  of	  this	  is	  for	  the	  architects	  to	  provide	  additional	  2D	  drawings	  
to	  the	  engineers.	  	  
	  
Also	  some	  contractors	  see	  the	  benefits	  of	  using	  IFC	  as	  a	  central	  IFC	  model	  
with	  embedded	  information	  can	  be	  used	  to	  calculate	  prices	  more	  accurately	  
than	  conventional	  2D	  drawings	  (bips,	  2007).	  
	  
Challenges	  with	  IFC	  relates	  to	  IFC’s	  relative	  complexity.	  Large	  BIM	  models	  
with	  a	  lot	  of	  objects	  can	  be	  difficult	  to	  export	  properly	  –	  and	  it	  is	  difficult	  to	  
identify	  missing	  objects	  in	  the	  resulting	  IFC	  model.	  Exporting	  a	  complex	  
model	  is	  also	  time-­‐consuming	  and	  has	  to	  be	  taken	  into	  account.	  These	  
issues	  indicate	  that	  IFC	  should	  primarily	  be	  used	  as	  a	  deliverable	  format	  
when	  the	  project	  is	  finished	  and	  not	  as	  a	  primary	  exchange	  file	  format	  (bips,	  
2013a).	  The	  effort	  needed	  to	  educate	  users	  of	  IFC	  is	  also	  significant.	  It	  
seems	  as	  small	  companies	  are	  having	  difficulties	  with	  finding	  resources	  to	  
make	  IFC	  deliverables,	  hence	  not	  being	  able	  to	  work	  on	  public	  projects	  
(bips,	  2013a).	  IFC	  are	  being	  updated	  regularly	  why	  all	  actors	  are	  
responsible	  to	  obtain	  the	  newest	  knowledge.	  An	  IFC-­‐certification	  of	  
employees	  could	  be	  a	  possibility	  to	  ensure	  streamlined	  quality.	  
2.2. INFORMATION DELIVERY MANUAL (IDM) 
The	  following	  section	  will	  present	  a	  general	  introduction	  to	  the	  IDM	  method	  
and	  hereafter	  introduce	  the	  framework	  of	  how	  to	  design	  IDM	  packages	  later	  
in	  this	  study.	  	  
2.2.1. DEFINITION OF IDM 
Technical	  barriers	  in	  BIM	  implementation	  are	  partly	  broken	  down	  by	  
seamless	  digital	  information	  exchange	  ensured	  by	  implementation	  of	  IFC	  in	  
BIM	  software.	  However,	  there	  seems	  to	  have	  been	  a	  general	  lack	  of	  
standardised	  methodology	  to	  exchange	  information	  between	  project	  team	  
members	  in	  the	  AEC	  industry	  (Mondrup,	  Treldal,	  Karlshøj,	  &	  Vestergaard,	  
2014a).	  	  
	  
IFC	  does	  not	  account	  how	  information	  is	  created	  and	  how	  it	  is	  -­‐	  or	  should	  be	  
-­‐	  shared	  between	  practitioners	  (Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  2010)	  (Sacks,	  Kaner,	  
Eastman,	  &	  Jeong,	  2010).	  In	  order	  to	  gain	  the	  full	  potential	  of	  IFC,	  the	  
industry	  needs	  to	  have	  more	  structured	  information	  exchange	  (Karlshøj,	  
==PAGE:33==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
14
2012).	  The	  quality	  of	  communication	  between	  project	  participants	  needs	  to	  
be	  improved	  and	  information	  exchange	  has	  to	  be	  displayed	  properly	  for	  
everyone	  to	  have	  clear	  definitions	  of	  each	  participant’s	  role	  (Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  
2010).	  Every	  participant	  need	  to	  know	  what	  information	  is	  required	  to	  
perform	  and	  what	  information	  is	  required	  to	  deliver	  (Hooper	  &	  Ekholm,	  
2012).	  
	  
A	  2010	  document	  by	  buildingSMART	  described	  a	  new	  methodology	  to	  
standardise	  the	  process	  workflow	  and	  information	  exchange	  between	  
processes	  within	  an	  AEC	  project	  (Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  2010).	  This	  method	  is	  
called	  the	  Information	  Delivery	  Manual	  (IDM)	  and	  aims	  to	  support	  
information	  exchange	  standardisation	  alongside	  with	  the	  IFC	  standard.	  This	  
methodology	  is	  further	  supported	  in	  the	  industry	  by	  being	  adapted	  as	  an	  
ISO	  standard	  in	  2010	  (ISO,	  2010).	  The	  buildingSMART	  document	  states	  the	  
following:	  
	  
“The	  IDM	  aims	  to	  provide	  the	  integrated	  reference	  for	  process	  and	  data	  
required	  by	  BIM	  by	  identifying	  the	  discrete	  processes	  undertaken	  within	  
building	  construction,	  the	  information	  required	  for	  their	  execution	  and	  the	  
results	  of	  that	  activity”	  (Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  2010,	  p.	  7).	  
	  
The	  IFC	  standard	  delivers	  data	  to	  support	  all	  business	  requirements	  on	  all	  
project	  stages	  within	  an	  AEC	  project.	  What	  IDM	  aims	  to	  deliver,	  is	  a	  
description	  of	  the	  single	  process	  at	  a	  certain	  project	  stage	  –	  hence	  detailing	  
the	  information	  exchange	  to	  support	  the	  framework	  of	  IFC.	  According	  to	  
buildingSMART,	  three	  constituents	  can	  describe	  the	  BIM	  data	  exchange	  
model	  as	  illustrated	  on	  Figure	  3.	  Some	  of	  the	  goals	  of	  using	  IDMs	  are	  to	  
“define	  the	  processes	  within	  the	  AEC/FM	  project	  lifecycle	  for	  which	  users	  
require	  information	  exchange”	  and	  “specify	  the	  IFC	  capabilities	  required	  to	  
support	  these	  processes”	  (Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  2010,	  p.	  16).	  
	  
Figure	  3	  –	  The	  three	  elementary	  parts	  of	  sharing	  structured	  data	  according	  to	  openBIM	  definition.	  
[Source:	  (buildingSMART,	  n.d.)]	  
IDM	  is	  a	  useful	  tool	  when	  mapping	  business	  processes	  and	  defining	  needs	  
and	  requirements	  to	  carry	  out	  these	  processes.	  The	  main	  idea	  is,	  that	  if	  the	  
methods	  of	  exchanging	  information	  were	  formalised	  the	  productivity	  of	  the	  
==PAGE:34==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  15 
processes	  would	  be	  improved	  (Karlshøj,	  2012).	  Potentially,	  this	  would	  also	  
lead	  to	  more	  automatic	  execution	  of	  certain	  processes	  where	  wasted	  time	  
could	  be	  reduced.	  Also	  potential	  over-­‐production	  of	  information	  material	  by	  
the	  actors	  could	  be	  trimmed	  to	  only	  include	  the	  most	  relevant	  and	  precise	  
information.	  	  
2.2.2. IDM FRAMEWORK OVERVIEW 
IDMs	  describe	  various	  processes	  related	  to	  AEC	  projects.	  These	  processes	  
will	  be	  covered	  by	  different	  disciplines	  at	  different	  life	  cycle	  stages	  in	  the	  
project.	  In	  each	  IDM	  package,	  all	  relevant	  activities	  performed	  in	  that	  
respective	  process	  will	  be	  mapped	  in	  a	  sequential	  order.	  Next,	  the	  IDM	  will	  
describe	  the	  actors	  involved	  in	  the	  process.	  Key	  information	  is	  who	  creates,	  
consumes	  and	  benefits	  from	  the	  information	  exchanged	  between	  
actors/processes.	  After	  that	  follows	  a	  more	  detailed	  description	  of	  the	  
exchanged	  information	  so	  all	  actors	  are	  certain	  what	  information	  needs	  to	  
be	  produced	  or	  consumed.	  Lastly,	  an	  IDM	  will	  describe	  the	  information	  
exchanged	  in	  a	  technical	  matter	  as	  how	  it	  should	  be	  supported	  by	  software	  
solutions.	  This	  is	  necessary	  to	  develop	  and	  certificate	  relevant	  software	  
packages	  in	  order	  to	  ensure	  that	  software	  meet	  the	  needs	  of	  the	  users	  (Wix	  
&	  Karlshøj,	  2010)	  
	  
In	  the	  following	  paragraphs	  the	  framework	  of	  creating	  IDMs	  will	  be	  
described	  based	  on	  (Mondrup,	  Karlshøj,	  &	  Vestergaard,	  2014b)	  	  
Life cycle stages 
Life	  cycle	  stages	  are	  used	  to	  identify	  where	  processes	  are	  situated	  within	  an	  
AEC	  project’s	  lifecycle.	  Life	  cycle	  stages	  described	  in	  IDMs	  are	  using	  the	  ISO	  
29481-­‐1	  standard	  Table	  A.1	  (ISO,	  2010),	  which	  is	  illustrated	  on	  Figure	  4.	  As	  
one	  can	  see,	  an	  AEC	  project	  is	  divided	  into	  stages	  from	  A	  to	  K,	  which	  will	  
also	  be	  the	  first	  name	  of	  IDM	  package	  naming	  hence	  describing	  at	  which	  life	  
cycle	  stage	  the	  described	  process	  occurs.	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  4	  -­‐	  ISO	  29481-­‐1	  life	  cycle	  stages	  [Source: (ISO, 2010)]	  
==PAGE:35==

OmniClass disciplines 
Many	  different	  disciplines	  are	  present	  in	  an	  AEC	  project	  and	  it	  is	  important	  
to	  streamline	  terminology	  so	  everyone	  knows	  what	  discipline	  is	  described.	  
In	  order	  to	  secure	  that,	  it	  has	  been	  chosen	  that	  OmniClass	  Construction	  
Classification	  System	  (CCS)	  is	  a	  way	  to	  organise	  this	  information.	  OmniClass	  
is	  widely	  used	  in	  North	  America	  as	  a	  tool	  to	  classify	  information	  throughout	  
the	  AEC	  project	  life	  cycle.	  OmniClass	  counts	  15	  individual	  tables	  all	  
classifying	  different	  kind	  of	  information	  from	  the	  built	  industry.	  	  
	  
The	  table	  used	  to	  describe	  disciplines	  related	  to	  IDM	  packages	  is	  the	  
OmniClass	  Table	  33:	  ‘Disciplines’	  (OmniClass,	  2012).	  This	  describes	  all	  
services	  provided	  throughout	  an	  AEC	  project	  construction	  entity	  by	  using	  
Work	  Breakdown	  Structure	  (WBS).	  WBS	  is	  normally	  associated	  with	  project	  
management,	  where	  gradually	  breaking	  down	  and	  specifying	  tasks	  makes	  it	  
possible	  to	  time	  and	  costs	  estimate	  a	  given	  task	  more	  precisely.	  An	  example	  
from	  OmniClass	  would	  be	  the	  ‘Lighting	  Design’	  discipline	  that	  uses	  
breakdown	  to	  3th	  level	  as	  exemplified	  on	  Table	  1.	  
	  
Table	  1	  -­‐	  Example	  showing	  3	  levels	  of	  design	  discipline	  specification	  using	  WBS	  in	  OmniClass	  [Source: 
(OmniClass, 2012)]	  
LEVEL 1	  
LEVEL 2	  
LEVEL 3	  
33-­‐21	  00	  00	  
Design	  Disciplines	  
	  
	  
	  
33-­‐21	  99	  00	  
Specialty	  Design	  
	  
	  
	  
33-­‐21	  99	  28	  	  
Lighting	  Design	  
	  
The	  numbering	  of	  a	  particular	  OmniClass	  discipline	  indicates	  how	  much	  it	  
has	  been	  broken	  down	  from	  its	  ‘parent’	  discipline.	  The	  Lighting	  Design	  
discipline	  is	  named	  ’33	  –	  21	  99	  28’.	  This	  means,	  that	  this	  discipline	  is	  found	  
in	  the	  OmniClass	  Table	  33,	  and	  is	  broken	  down	  to	  3rd	  level,	  hence	  the	  three	  
sets	  of	  two-­‐digits	  numbers.	  This	  numeration	  is	  used	  when	  naming	  IDM	  
packages	  and	  makes	  it	  possible	  to	  identify	  which	  discipline	  at	  what	  
particular	  life	  cycle	  stage	  that	  IDM	  package	  describes.	  An	  example	  could	  be:	  
A	  33	  –	  21	  99	  28	  which	  says	  it	  is	  Lighting	  Design	  in	  the	  Portfolio	  
requirements	  life	  cycle	  stage.	  	  
IDM project plan 
The	  top	  part	  of	  Figure	  5	  shows	  a	  matrix-­‐structure	  in	  which	  IDM	  packages	  
can	  be	  assembled	  inspired	  by	  (Mondrup et al., 2014b).	  This	  matrix	  contains	  a	  
number	  of	  IDM	  packages	  describing	  various	  processes,	  vertically	  sorted	  in	  
OmniClass	  disciplines	  and	  horizontally	  sorted	  after	  in	  which	  ISO	  life	  cycle	  
stage	  they	  occur.	  Using	  a	  database	  of	  packages	  like	  this	  makes	  it	  possible	  for	  
a	  project	  manager	  to	  choose	  packages	  and	  create	  a	  project-­‐specific	  IDM	  
Project	  Plan	  as	  seen	  on	  the	  bottom	  part	  of	  Figure	  5.	  This	  could	  be	  an	  actual	  
project	  where	  only	  relevant	  packages	  are	  selected	  or	  ‘activated’	  hence	  
knowing	  exactly	  what	  tasks	  should	  be	  performed;	  what	  information	  is	  
needed	  and	  what	  actors	  are	  involved.	  If	  those	  packages	  are	  standardised	  
==PAGE:36==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  17 
they	  will	  be	  independent	  on	  organisation	  or	  company,	  which	  is	  showed	  in	  
the	  left	  column	  in	  the	  IDM	  project	  plan	  in	  Figure	  5.	  	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  5	  –	  IDM	  Framework	  and	  IDM	  Project	  Plan.	  [Inspired	  by	  (Mondrup et al., 2014a):	  	  
Creating	  IDM	  packages	  for	  a	  vast	  number	  of	  processes	  within	  the	  AEC	  
industry	  opens	  for	  endless	  time-­‐	  and	  resource	  management	  possibilities.	  
A	  project	  manager	  could	  have	  a	  large	  database	  of	  pre-­‐defined	  IDM	  packages	  
representing	  processes	  in	  different	  life-­‐cycle	  stages	  that	  he	  can	  choose	  from

mapped	  with	  actors,	  information	  exchange	  and	  potentially	  time	  estimation,	  
it	  will	  be	  easier	  to	  plan	  out	  a	  complete	  project.
