18
2.2.3. IDM CONSTITUENTS 
According	  to	  (Mondrup,	  Treldal,	  Karlshøj,	  &	  Vestergaard,	  2014a)	  an	  IDM	  
package	  consists	  of	  generally	  of	  four	  deliverables	  as	  illustrated	  in	  Figure	  6.	  
As	  an	  extension	  of	  the	  IDM,	  an	  MVD	  (Model	  View	  Definition)	  occurs,	  which	  
translates	  the	  IDM	  to	  specify	  needs	  for	  software	  development	  (Karlshøj	  &	  
Berard,	  2011).	  The	  four	  IDM	  deliverables	  in	  Figure	  6	  will	  be	  explained	  in	  
the	  next	  subsections.	  For	  the	  sake	  of	  simplicity	  the	  input	  requirement	  
model	  and	  output	  requirement	  model	  will	  be	  unified	  into	  ‘exchange	  
requirement	  models’.	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  6	  -­‐	  IDM	  deliverables.	  [Source:	  (Mondrup,	  Treldal,	  Karlshøj,	  &	  Vestergaard,	  2014a)]	  
Use Case (UC) 
An	  IDM	  starts	  with	  a	  brief	  description	  of	  the	  intended	  UC	  in	  terms	  of	  which	  
business	  scenario	  the	  IDM	  is	  intended	  to	  solve.	  The	  description	  should	  be	  
plain	  text	  without	  technical	  details	  so	  readers	  without	  specialist	  knowledge	  
understands	  it.	  A	  UC	  should	  clarify	  what	  the	  intended	  use	  of	  the	  IDM	  is;	  
what	  activities	  are	  included;	  and	  every	  actor	  involved	  and/or	  interested	  in	  
the	  IDM.	  It	  should	  also	  clarify	  the	  information	  exchange	  needed	  for	  the	  
process	  to	  continue,	  who	  wants	  the	  information	  and	  who	  sends	  and	  receive	  
this	  information	  (bips,	  2013b).	  In	  general,	  it	  is	  especially	  useful	  for	  the	  
project	  manager	  to	  get	  an	  overview	  of	  the	  process	  and	  its	  relevance	  to	  a	  
specific	  project.	  The	  UC	  is	  part	  of	  the	  preparation	  and	  is	  prerequisite	  to	  
develop	  an	  IDM.	  	  
Process Map (PM) 
A	  PM	  is	  the	  graphical	  output	  of	  an	  IDM	  and	  usually	  the	  easiest	  to	  
communicate.	  A	  PM	  is	  a	  graphical	  way	  to	  display	  all	  activities	  in	  a	  business	  
process	  and	  their	  individual	  relations	  and	  sequential	  flow	  throughout	  the	  
process.	  It	  is	  also	  used	  to	  get	  an	  overview	  of	  the	  actors	  responsible	  for	  the	  
individual	  activities.	  A	  PM	  is	  notated	  in	  Business	  Process	  Modelling	  
Notation	  (BPMN),	  which	  is	  an	  industry-­‐wide	  accepted	  standard	  of	  
presenting	  business	  processes	  in	  a	  graphical	  representation	  focusing	  on	  
flows	  and	  connections	  between	  activities.	  Hence	  a	  PM	  focuses	  on	  actor	  
interaction	  between	  activities	  rather	  than	  describing	  the	  single	  activity	  in	  
detail	  (bips,	  2013b).	  	  
Even	  though	  activities	  are	  listed	  in	  terms	  of	  their	  individual	  occurrence	  
within	  a	  project,	  they	  are	  not	  time-­‐dependent	  as	  the	  core	  purpose	  of	  a	  PM	  is	  
to	  show	  relations	  and	  sequences	  only.	  But	  a	  PM	  can	  be	  used	  as	  inspiration	  
for	  a	  separate	  time-­‐management	  related	  tool	  such	  as	  a	  Gantt	  chart	  or	  
==PAGE:38==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  19 
location-­‐based	  scheduling	  (LBS).	  A	  diagrammatic	  example	  of	  a	  PM	  is	  shown	  
on	  Figure	  7	  that	  illustrates	  a	  typical	  structure	  of	  a	  process	  map	  with	  
activities	  (the	  boxes)	  connected	  with	  arrows	  in	  a	  sequence	  and	  related	  
exchange	  requirements	  (document	  symbols).	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  7	  –	  Diagrammatic	  presentation	  of	  a	  Process	  map	  showing	  a	  sequence	  of	  activities	  (boxes)	  with	  
related	  data	  objects	  (documents)	  and	  a	  gateway.	  
Exchange Requirement (ER) 
ER	  describes	  the	  required	  information	  exchange	  to	  happen	  in	  order	  to	  
support	  the	  particular	  business	  requirements	  previously	  fixed	  in	  the	  use	  
case	  of	  the	  IDM	  (ISO,	  2010).	  ERs	  are	  created	  based	  on	  the	  activities	  in	  the	  
PM	  and	  the	  definitions	  covered	  in	  the	  UC.	  In	  other	  words,	  ER	  is	  the	  end	  
user’s	  overview	  of	  what	  information	  needs	  to	  be	  delivered	  to	  others	  and	  
what	  information	  other	  actors	  will	  provide	  in	  order	  to	  continue	  a	  
downstream	  development	  throughout	  the	  PM.	  An	  ER	  is	  usually	  non-­‐
technical	  and	  should	  be	  presented	  in	  an	  easy-­‐readable	  schematic	  overview.	  	  
Exchange Requirement Models (ERM) 
An	  ERM	  is	  a	  more	  technical	  description	  than	  ER.	  ERMs	  organize	  the	  ERs	  into	  
complete	  schemas	  with	  technical	  information	  that	  is	  machine-­‐interpretable.	  
ERMs	  are	  the	  connection	  between	  process	  and	  data,	  hence	  inevitable	  for	  
connecting	  IFC	  with	  IDMs.	  An	  ERM	  is	  used	  by	  software	  applications	  to	  
exchange	  information	  at	  a	  very	  specific	  time,	  location	  and/or	  purpose	  in	  the	  
project	  (Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  2010).	  ERMs	  are	  important,	  as	  they	  are	  the	  IDM	  
components	  that	  translate	  the	  outcome	  to	  be	  used	  in	  software	  applications.	  
Also	  the	  ERM	  is	  somewhat	  standardised	  making	  it	  able	  to	  assign	  business	  
rules	  and	  validation	  tests,	  which	  is	  helpful	  to	  quality	  check	  the	  outcome	  of	  
an	  IDM	  in	  an	  automatic	  process	  (ISO,	  2010).	  
==PAGE:39==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
20
ERMs	  consists	  of	  an	  Input	  Requirement	  Model	  (IRM)	  that	  describes	  the	  
specific	  information	  needed	  to	  initiate	  an	  activity,	  and	  Output	  Requirement	  
Model	  (ORM)	  describing	  what	  specific	  information	  should	  be	  delivered	  to	  
other	  actors	  in	  order	  to	  continue	  a	  downstream	  development	  throughout	  
the	  PM.	  	  
	  
IRMs	  and	  ORMs	  are	  separate	  documents	  represented	  in	  the	  PM	  as	  data	  
objects	  every	  time	  they	  occur	  in	  relation	  to	  an	  activity.	  These	  documents	  
need	  to	  be	  constructed	  in	  a	  standardised	  matter	  so	  that	  recipients	  of	  these	  
documents	  can	  identify	  required	  information	  easily	  –	  hence	  strengthening	  
interoperability	  and	  information	  exchange	  that	  is	  the	  core	  purpose	  of	  
creating	  IDMs.	  IRM	  and	  ORMs	  represent	  project-­‐specific	  information	  
required	  and	  can	  be	  further	  supported	  by	  additional	  information	  (ADD),	  
which	  is	  non-­‐project	  specific	  and	  general	  knowledge,	  e.g.	  standards	  and	  
product	  datasheets.	  	  
	  
Information	  needs	  to	  be	  structured	  and	  standardised,	  hence	  well-­‐known	  
common	  terminology	  should	  be	  used.	  Overall	  classification	  of	  building	  
elements,	  spaces	  or	  components,	  can	  be	  described	  using	  Cuneco	  
Classification	  System	  (CCS).	  According	  to	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  2014b)	  geometry	  
and	  properties	  can	  be	  described	  by	  using	  Level	  of	  Development	  (LOD)	  and	  
IFC.	  These	  topics	  will	  be	  further	  covered	  when	  designing	  IDM	  packages	  in	  
chapter	  4.	  	  
2.2.4. STATE OF THE ART USE OF IDM 
In	  theory	  all	  actors	  in	  an	  AEC	  project	  can	  benefit	  from	  implementing	  IDMs	  
and	  utilise	  the	  potential	  of	  IFC	  in	  their	  BIM	  projects.	  For	  BIM	  users,	  IDMs	  
provide	  an	  easy	  to	  understand	  description	  of	  the	  AEC	  processes	  and	  
associate	  activities	  within	  each	  process	  and	  all	  required	  information	  
exchanges	  between	  actors	  in	  order	  to	  complete	  processes.	  Results,	  
deliverables	  and	  outcomes	  of	  each	  process	  is	  made	  visible	  for	  all	  actors,	  
which	  provides	  more	  reliable	  information	  exchange	  and	  could	  potentially	  
improve	  information	  quality,	  decision	  making	  quality	  and	  project	  efficiency	  
(Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  2010).	  IDM	  also	  solves	  an	  issue	  with	  IFC	  being	  too	  general	  
and	  not	  adaptable	  to	  specific	  needs	  in	  different	  business	  cases,	  by	  breaking	  
down	  a	  BIM	  project	  in	  smaller	  processes	  and	  applying	  relevant	  IFC	  data	  to	  
each	  (Sacks,	  Kaner,	  Eastman,	  &	  Jeong,	  2010)	  (Karlshøj,	  2012).	  It	  is	  also	  a	  
large	  benefit	  for	  software	  developers	  to	  identify	  specific	  user	  needs	  through	  
IDMs	  which	  guarantee	  better	  quality	  and	  reusable	  elements	  of	  the	  software	  
since	  it	  is	  process-­‐specific	  rather	  than	  project-­‐specific	  (Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  
2010).	  	  
	  
Every	  AEC	  project	  is	  more	  or	  less	  unique	  so	  it	  is	  difficult	  to	  standardise	  the	  
project	  and	  reuse	  knowledge	  gained	  in	  the	  project.	  By	  breaking	  down	  a	  
project	  into	  specific	  modular	  processes	  and	  IDMs	  makes	  it	  more	  likely	  that	  
these	  IDMs	  will	  be	  reused	  later	  in	  another	  project.	  A	  project	  manager	  can	  
simply	  just	  choose	  packages	  relevant	  for	  his/her	  project	  and	  activate	  them	  
accordingly.	  The	  big	  issue	  here	  is,	  that	  it	  is	  difficult	  to	  create	  a	  standardised	  
framework	  on	  how	  professionals	  work	  and	  even	  more	  difficult	  to	  
==PAGE:40==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  21 
standardise	  how	  they	  work	  across	  companies	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  2014a).	  The	  
Danish	  building	  sector	  is	  very	  tradition-­‐bound	  and	  not	  very	  adaptable	  for	  
revolutionary	  changes.	  It	  is	  tradition	  that	  processes	  and	  information	  flow	  
both	  within	  companies	  and	  across,	  is	  somehow	  undocumented	  and	  usually	  
relies	  on	  personal	  experience	  or	  company	  dictated	  work	  routines	  rather	  
than	  standardised	  routines	  across	  the	  industry	  (Karlshøj,	  2012).	  It	  is	  very	  
common	  for	  professionals	  to	  develop	  his/her	  own	  work	  structure.	  For	  
example	  when	  measuring	  areas,	  this	  can	  be	  done	  in	  a	  various	  number	  of	  
ways,	  making	  it	  very	  difficult	  to	  compare	  and	  reuse	  data	  across	  companies	  
(Karlshøj,	  2012).	  In	  order	  to	  have	  IDM	  to	  work	  properly,	  every	  actor	  needs	  
to	  work	  in	  the	  same	  way	  regardless	  of	  company	  and	  eventually	  regardless	  
of	  country	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  2014a).	  It	  is	  also	  a	  prerequisite	  that	  all	  
information	  is	  well	  documented.	  	  
	  
There	  is	  potential	  in	  using	  IDMs	  but	  it	  is	  rarely	  used	  in	  the	  Danish	  building	  
sector.	  Most	  companies	  structure	  their	  organisations	  on	  project-­‐basis,	  
which	  could	  be	  a	  reason	  for	  low	  adaption	  of	  ICT	  (Karlshøj,	  2012).	  
Implementation	  of	  IDM	  would	  be	  the	  first	  critical	  step.	  IDMs	  are	  commonly	  
criticised	  in	  the	  industry	  of	  being	  too	  big	  making	  them	  complex	  and	  difficult	  
to	  work	  with	  or	  having	  others	  to	  demonstrate	  how	  to	  work	  with	  them	  
(Karlshøj,	  2012)	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  2014a).	  The	  need	  to	  simplify	  the	  IDMs	  and	  
making	  them	  smaller	  and	  more	  precise	  is	  evident,	  and	  one	  method	  to	  do	  
this,	  is	  to	  break	  down	  the	  processes	  even	  more	  using	  a	  typical	  Work-­‐
Breakdown	  Structure	  (WBS)	  approach	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  2014a).	  If	  the	  IDM	  
packages	  were	  smaller	  and	  more	  specific,	  they	  would	  more	  likely	  be	  
adapted	  and	  reused	  across	  projects.	  The	  industry	  should	  also	  agree	  on	  a	  
commonly	  accepted	  framework	  structure	  and	  how	  IDMs	  should	  be	  
modelled.	  According	  to	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  2014a)	  the	  work	  breakdown	  could	  
be	  described	  using	  OmniClass	  disciplines;	  life	  cycle	  stages	  could	  be	  
described	  by	  the	  ISO	  29481-­‐1	  standard;	  and	  data	  exchange	  could	  be	  based	  
on	  IFC	  format,	  which	  is	  also	  introduced	  in	  subsection	  2.2.2.	  	  
	  
Another	  big	  challenge	  in	  IDM	  implementation	  in	  the	  industry	  is	  also	  linked	  
to	  lack	  of	  communication.	  Some	  studies	  in	  Sweden	  has	  shown	  that	  there	  
seems	  to	  be	  a	  lack	  of	  common	  understanding	  about	  BIM	  and	  the	  importance	  
of	  standardising	  the	  work	  routines	  in	  order	  to	  utilise	  full	  potential	  of	  BIM.	  
There	  seems	  to	  be	  a	  lack	  of	  consistent	  quality	  level	  of	  the	  delivered	  
material,	  which	  results	  in	  wasted	  time	  fixing	  errors	  (Hooper	  &	  Ekholm,	  
2012).	  Through	  IDMs	  the	  process	  activities	  and	  information	  exchange	  will	  
be	  described,	  but	  a	  clear	  definition	  of	  the	  quality	  level	  is	  not	  described.	  All	  
project	  team	  members	  need	  to	  be	  encouraged	  to	  work	  with	  BIM	  and	  follow	  
certain	  standardisation	  provided	  by	  IDM	  –	  only	  by	  doing	  that,	  all	  project	  
members	  can	  be	  sure	  what	  to	  deliver	  and	  what	  to	  provide	  others.	  
2.3. USE OF BIM IN LIGHTING DESIGN 
Lighting	  design	  is	  a	  relatively	  new	  discipline	  in	  the	  AEC	  industry	  (Ford,	  
2007).	  It	  is	  a	  field	  that	  combines	  creativity	  with	  technical	  approaches	  and	  
results	  in	  holistic	  solutions	  to	  enhance	  the	  architecture	  through	  lighting.	  
Lighting	  design	  is	  an	  indispensable	  discipline	  if	  building	  society	  aims	  to	  
==PAGE:41==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
22
create	  holistically	  sustainable	  buildings.	  Lighting	  design	  in	  buildings	  has	  
two	  primarily	  goals:	  to	  ensure	  high	  quality	  lighting	  for	  human	  needs	  and	  
comfort;	  and	  to	  reduce	  energy	  consumption	  in	  buildings	  through	  correctly	  
planned	  and	  utilised	  lighting.	  This	  section	  aims	  to	  provide	  the	  reader	  with	  a	  
thorough	  understanding	  of	  the	  lighting	  designer’s	  work	  and	  how	  this	  is,	  or	  
could	  be	  influenced	  by	  BIM,	  which	  is	  the	  scope	  of	  this	  study.	  The	  term	  
‘lighting	  design’	  covered	  in	  the	  following	  chapter	  is	  a	  discipline	  working	  
with	  design	  and	  detailing	  of	  artificial	  lighting	  systems,	  hence	  having	  no	  
primarily	  influence	  on	  the	  daylight	  design	  and	  architectural	  shaping	  
involved	  within.	  	  
2.3.1. LIGHTING DESIGN FOR ENERGY 
Lighting	  is	  important	  in	  several	  aspects.	  It	  is	  a	  major	  source	  to	  energy	  
consumption	  in	  buildings,	  accounting	  for	  up	  to	  40	  %	  of	  the	  total	  electricity	  
consumption	  in	  office	  buildings	  (Halonen,	  Tetri,	  &	  Bhusal,	  2010).	  On	  a	  
global	  scale,	  up	  to	  20	  %	  of	  the	  total	  electricity	  consumption	  is	  due	  to	  
lighting	  (International	  Energy	  Agency,	  n.d.).	  At	  the	  same	  time,	  lighting	  is	  
also	  one	  of	  the	  building	  installations	  that	  has	  the	  largest	  savings	  potential	  
due	  to	  relatively	  small	  actions	  required	  to	  measure	  large	  impacts	  (Halonen	  
et	  al.,	  2010).	  Followed	  by	  recent	  year’s	  development	  in	  lighting	  technology,	  
the	  access	  to	  high-­‐efficiency	  light	  bulbs	  or	  light	  control	  systems	  is	  still	  
getting	  cheaper	  and	  competition	  is	  enlarged.	  Development	  in	  LED-­‐
technology,	  which	  is	  the	  most	  energy	  efficient	  light	  source	  at	  the	  moment,	  
has	  exploded	  since	  the	  mid-­‐2000s	  (Shur	  &	  Žukauskas,	  2005).	  
	  
Despite	  that,	  the	  primary	  light	  source	  used	  in	  residential	  buildings	  is	  still	  
incandescent	  light	  sources,	  even	  though	  production	  terminated	  in	  2010	  
(Halonen	  et	  al.,	  2010).	  In	  commercial	  buildings	  the	  primary	  light	  source	  is	  
compact	  fluorescent	  lamps	  (CFLs)	  due	  to	  more	  energy	  efficient	  properties	  
than	  incandescent	  (Halonen	  et	  al.,	  2010).	  However,	  the	  energy	  consumption	  
for	  CFLs	  is	  2-­‐3	  times	  higher	  than	  LEDs	  why	  there	  is	  a	  large	  savings	  potential	  
in	  those	  areas.	  Development	  of	  LEDs	  is	  the	  main	  reason	  for	  the	  enlarged	  
focus	  on	  savings	  potential	  in	  buildings	  –	  both	  new	  and	  old.	  Standards	  and	  
national	  requirements	  are	  getting	  stricter	  and	  use	  of	  high-­‐efficient	  
luminaires	  in	  new	  built	  buildings	  in	  Denmark	  is	  mandatory	  (Danish	  Energy	  
Agency,	  2014).	  Use	  of	  low-­‐energy	  consuming	  luminaires	  such	  as	  LED	  is	  also	  
a	  crucial	  method	  to	  obtain	  the	  nationally	  acclaimed	  maximum	  energy	  
consumption	  for	  lighting,	  which	  at	  the	  moment	  is	  5	  kWh/m2/year	  (Thau,	  
2005).	  	  
	  
A	  typical	  situation	  for	  a	  lighting	  designer,	  who	  designs	  lighting	  for	  energy,	  is	  
to	  evaluate	  and	  analyse	  the	  given	  architecture	  to	  determine	  where	  light	  is	  
needed	  and	  how	  much.	  A	  conventional	  office	  lighting	  design	  where	  light	  is	  
distributed	  uniformly	  and	  independent	  on	  the	  actual	  plan	  layout	  is	  long	  
gone	  and	  substituted	  with	  a	  more	  efficient	  scheme.	  Nowadays,	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  distributes	  the	  lighting	  in	  zones	  to	  make	  advantage	  of	  the	  core	  fact,	  
that	  the	  main	  work	  performed	  is	  based	  on	  vertical	  planes	  rather	  than	  
horizontal	  –	  hence	  our	  computer	  screens	  being	  a	  source	  of	  illumination	  
itself	  (Boyce,	  2003).	  So	  the	  energy	  consumption	  of	  the	  general	  lighting	  
==PAGE:42==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  23 
system	  can	  be	  remarkably	  reduced	  by	  planning	  the	  light	  more	  intelligent	  
and	  distribute	  lighting	  where	  it	  is	  actually	  needed.	  	  
	  
Selecting	  appropriate	  light	  fixtures	  to	  support	  the	  energy	  reduction	  strategy	  
is	  a	  large	  part	  of	  the	  lighting	  designer’s	  job.	  Lighting	  designers	  are	  
specialised	  in	  possessing	  the	  newest	  knowledge	  of	  products	  and	  trends	  
within	  the	  lighting	  industry.	  The	  days	  where	  fixtures	  were	  selected	  based	  
on	  their	  wattage	  and	  price	  is	  long	  gone.	  Fixtures	  are	  selected	  through	  a	  
large	  set	  of	  requirements	  and	  properties	  originating	  in	  qualitative	  lighting	  
design,	  which	  will	  be	  covered	  in	  later	  subsections.	  	  	  	  
	  
	  
To	  sum	  up,	  the	  lighting	  designer	  needs	  to	  combine	  the	  newest	  light	  sources	  
available	  and	  reduce	  the	  usage	  as	  much	  as	  possible	  in	  order	  to	  design	  for	  
energy	  reduction.	  This	  can	  be	  done	  through	  intelligent	  planning	  and	  zoning	  
of	  lights	  and	  through	  control	  systems	  that	  detects	  presence	  of	  people	  or	  
natural	  light	  available	  and	  dims	  the	  artificial	  lighting	  in	  accordance.	  This	  
job,	  however,	  is	  slightly	  overlapping	  the	  job	  of	  a	  conventional	  electrical	  
engineer	  or	  a	  building	  automation	  systems	  (BAS)	  engineer.	  	  
2.3.2. LIGHTING DESIGN FOR HUMAN QUALITY 
Daylight	  is	  the	  primary	  light	  source	  for	  human	  after	  which	  our	  bodies	  have	  
developed	  throughout	  our	  existence.	  However,	  the	  type	  of	  light	  that	  
typically	  stimulates	  humans	  during	  the	  day	  is	  the	  artificial	  light	  found	  in	  
offices,	  homes,	  schools	  etc.	  It	  is	  light	  that	  has	  only	  been	  part	  of	  our	  existence	  
the	  past	  135	  years	  (Descottes	  &	  Ramos,	  2011).	  Needless	  to	  say,	  the	  human	  
body	  adapts	  differently	  to	  artificial	  lighting	  than	  natural	  lighting.	  This	  is	  
why	  one	  of	  the	  key	  objectives	  for	  the	  lighting	  designer	  is	  to	  create	  
comfortable	  artificial	  lighting	  environments	  for	  human.	  	  
	  
The	  technical	  specialist	  ‘lighting	  designer’	  emerges	  from	  theatre	  (Ford,	  
2007).	  For	  ages,	  a	  lighting	  designer	  was	  responsible	  for	  creating	  a	  mood	  
through	  light	  and	  set	  a	  dynamic	  scene	  that	  shifts	  together	  with	  the	  wanted	  
expression.	  The	  main	  principle	  in	  the	  work	  of	  AEC	  lighting	  designers	  is	  not	  
much	  different.	  An	  architectural	  space	  needs	  to	  be	  lit	  to	  provoke	  a	  wanted	  
mood	  or	  feeling	  in	  the	  people	  present.	  For	  instance,	  light	  settings	  that	  were	  
uplifting	  and	  energising	  in	  offices;	  calm	  and	  relaxed	  in	  dwellings;	  or	  crisp	  
and	  bright	  in	  hospitals	  (Innes,	  2012).	  Also	  on	  building	  scale	  level	  the	  
architect	  can	  benefit	  from	  using	  a	  lighting	  designer	  to	  create	  a	  specific	  
atmosphere	  or	  expression	  around	  his	  building.	  	  
	  
All	  of	  this	  comes	  down	  to	  qualitative	  properties	  of	  the	  light,	  which	  cannot	  
be	  directly	  measured	  the	  same	  way	  as	  we	  are	  measuring	  illumination	  levels	  
(quantitative	  values).	  The	  difference	  between	  complying	  with	  standardised	  
lux-­‐values	  or	  designing	  with	  light	  directly	  for	  human	  beings,	  is	  the	  core	  
difference	  between	  the	  ‘lighting	  designer’	  specialist	  and	  the	  conventional	  
electrical	  engineer	  who	  ‘designs’	  lighting	  in	  buildings.	  	  
	  
Following	  is	  a	  list	  of	  some	  of	  the	  qualitative	  properties	  a	  lighting	  design	  
professional	  has	  to	  consider	  in	  his/her	  design:	  
==PAGE:43==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
24
Space illumination 
Light	  is	  used	  to	  illuminate	  and	  define	  boundaries	  within	  a	  space.	  Walls,	  
ceilings	  and	  floors	  need	  to	  be	  illuminated	  properly	  to	  give	  the	  right	  
conception	  of	  volume	  that	  the	  architect	  intended	  when	  designing	  the	  
building.	  The	  right	  illumination	  is	  a	  balance	  between	  light	  and	  shadows	  and	  
details	  (Innes,	  2012).	  
Visual performance 
The	  ability	  to	  look	  undisturbed	  on	  a	  task	  or	  orientate	  inside	  a	  space	  is	  an	  
important	  part	  of	  the	  finished	  design.	  Visual	  performance	  is	  usually	  
described	  through	  ‘lux’	  on	  a	  surface	  or	  task	  of	  which	  many	  standards	  and	  
guidelines	  dictate	  minimum	  values.	  However,	  studies	  show	  that	  visual	  
performance	  is	  not	  only	  dependent	  on	  lux	  but	  also	  a	  combination	  of	  other	  
factors,	  such	  as	  task	  size,	  contrasts	  or	  colour.	  It	  has	  been	  proven,	  that	  visual	  
performance	  is	  not	  increasing	  at	  the	  same	  pace	  as	  increase	  of	  lux	  (Viitanen,	  
Lehtovaara,	  Tetri,	  &	  Halonen,	  2013).	  It	  is	  also	  a	  belief,	  that	  visual	  
performance	  cannot	  be	  simplified	  to	  a	  number	  of	  lux	  but	  rather	  a	  
‘perception’	  of	  brightness	  (Fotios,	  2011).	  
Visual comfort 
This	  is	  not	  a	  measurable	  value,	  and	  is	  characterised	  by	  the	  ability	  to	  
perform	  a	  task	  without	  disturbing	  elements	  caused	  by	  lighting.	  Uniform	  or	  
uninteresting	  light	  patterns	  have	  been	  linked	  to	  result	  in	  degraded	  
motivation	  among	  office	  workers	  (Veitch,	  Stokkermans,	  &	  Newsham,	  2011).	  
Contrasts	  is	  also	  a	  key	  factor	  in	  visual	  comfort,	  especially	  as	  too	  much	  
daylight	  in	  a	  room	  or	  a	  bright	  lit	  surface	  in	  the	  field	  of	  view	  makes	  the	  
pupils	  strain	  too	  much	  and	  causes	  fatigue	  or	  headache	  (Boyce,	  2009).	  	  
Glare 
Glare	  is	  a	  common	  reason	  for	  discomfort	  among	  office	  workers	  (Winchip,	  
2011).	  Too	  much	  light	  in	  the	  field	  of	  view	  or	  reflections	  on	  the	  working	  
plane	  is	  common	  and	  is	  a	  factor	  the	  lighting	  designer	  needs	  to	  consider	  
when	  designing	  light.	  With	  the	  introduction	  of	  LEDs	  as	  light	  sources,	  a	  new	  
problem	  followed:	  namely	  that	  LEDs	  are	  tiny	  concentrated	  light	  sources	  
with	  a	  high	  lumen	  output.	  Glare	  is	  always	  a	  challenge	  to	  avoid	  when	  
designing	  and	  using	  LED	  light	  sources	  (Halonen	  et	  al.,	  2010)	  (Boyce,	  2003).	  	  
Colour and colour temperature 
Colour	  of	  light	  is	  caused	  by	  its	  spectral	  power	  distribution.	  Appearance	  of	  
light	  is	  described	  by	  either	  RGB	  colours	  or,	  for	  white	  light,	  the	  correlated	  
colour	  temperature	  (CCT),	  which	  is	  measured	  in	  kelvin	  and	  spans	  from	  
2700	  kelvin	  (warm)	  up	  to	  6500	  kelvin	  (cold)	  (Halonen	  et	  al.,	  2010).	  The	  
colour	  temperature	  of	  light	  defines	  its	  ability	  to	  represent	  natural	  colours	  of	  
objects	  –	  called	  the	  light	  source’s	  colour-­‐rendering	  index	  (CRI).	  Natural	  
daylight	  has	  most	  correct	  colour-­‐rendering	  index	  for	  the	  brain	  to	  perceive	  
colour,	  i.e.	  CRI	  of	  100	  (Innes,	  2012).	  
Light quality standardisations 
Even	  though	  all	  of	  the	  above	  described	  factors	  are	  qualitative,	  many	  
international	  organisations	  and	  standardisation	  agencies	  is	  working	  on	  
implementing	  them	  in	  their	  guidelines	  and	  standards.	  The	  work	  is	  foremost	  
==PAGE:44==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  25 
directed	  by	  CIE	  –	  International	  Commission	  on	  Illumination	  –	  and	  is	  being	  
adopted	  by	  various	  national	  standards.	  In	  Denmark	  standards	  such	  as	  the	  
DS700	  (Danish	  Standards,	  2005)	  or	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1-­‐1	  (Danish	  Standards,	  
2011)	  includes	  qualitative	  factors	  such	  as	  glare	  and	  colour	  rendering	  as	  
minimum	  values	  that	  has	  to	  be	  complied	  with.	  Other	  non-­‐measurable	  
factors	  are	  described	  in	  these	  standards	  showing	  the	  importance	  and	  need	  
to	  create	  qualitative	  lighting	  rather	  than	  just	  fulfilling	  conventional	  lux	  
values,	  which	  primarily	  favour	  visual	  performance.	  	  
	  
A	  whole	  new	  trend	  and	  focus	  within	  lighting	  design	  for	  humans	  is	  called	  
biological	  illumination,	  which	  is	  artificial	  lighting	  especially	  designed	  for	  the	  
human	  body.	  It	  works	  by	  imitating	  daylight	  qualities	  and	  stimulate	  the	  
circadian	  rhythm	  in	  our	  bodies	  (Figueiro,	  Rea,	  &	  Bullough,	  2002)	  (Howell,	  
2012)	  (Lang,	  2011)	  (Pavic,	  2014).	  To	  prove	  the	  importance	  of	  focusing	  on	  
this	  kind	  of	  lighting	  in	  buildings,	  official	  DIN	  SPEC	  67600	  guidelines	  were	  
published	  in	  2013	  (Deutsche	  Institut	  für	  Normung,	  2013).	  All	  these	  
indications	  showing	  increased	  focus	  on	  lighting	  just	  underline	  the	  
importance	  of	  a	  specialised	  lighting	  professional	  as	  part	  of	  the	  design	  team.	  	  	  
2.3.3. LIGHTING DESIGN PRACTICE IN DENMARK 
Lighting	  designers	  in	  Denmark	  are	  currently	  not	  protected	  by	  a	  title	  or	  
specific	  education	  –	  it	  is	  a	  profession	  and	  speciality	  within	  different	  fields	  of	  
services.	  Mainly	  within	  architecture	  and	  engineering.	  	  
	  
Lighting	  design	  originates	  in	  the	  theatre	  as	  previous	  mentioned,	  when	  a	  
specialist	  in	  larger	  theatres	  were	  assigned	  to	  plan	  and	  execute	  the	  dynamic	  
light	  settings	  associated	  with	  a	  show.	  In	  Denmark,	  theatre	  lighting	  
designers	  were	  educated	  at	  the	  Danish	  National	  School	  of	  Performing	  Arts.	  
Furthermore,	  there	  has	  been	  a	  long	  tradition	  from	  the	  Danish	  School	  of	  
Design	  to	  educate	  designers	  with	  focus	  on	  light	  fixtures	  –	  some	  of	  the	  
greatest	  architects	  in	  Denmark	  have	  light	  fixtures	  in	  their	  portfolios.	  
Designing	  with	  light	  for	  architecture	  and	  space	  is	  not	  a	  new	  phenomenon	  
but	  it	  is	  a	  relatively	  new	  trend	  to	  include	  specialists	  in	  lighting	  from	  the	  
very	  beginning	  of	  an	  AEC	  project	  (Ford,	  2007).	  Up	  until	  now,	  lighting	  
engineers	  in	  Denmark	  has	  been	  used	  late	  in	  the	  project	  to	  document	  
lighting	  conditions	  –	  not	  as	  design	  aiding	  consultants	  (Ford,	  2007).	  
	  
Larger	  consultancy	  companies	  in	  Denmark	  are	  now	  having	  separate	  
divisions	  particular	  specialising	  in	  lighting	  design.	  ÅF	  Lighting	  (ÅF	  Lighting,	  
n.d.),	  Ramboll	  Light	  (Ramboll,	  n.d.),	  COWI	  Lighting	  (COWI,	  2014)	  and	  
Grontmij	  (Grontmij,	  n.d.)	  are	  some	  of	  the	  largest	  consulting	  engineers	  
companies	  in	  Denmark	  and	  offers	  lighting	  design	  as	  a	  service	  in	  their	  AEC	  
projects.	  Definitely	  the	  industry	  has	  seen	  the	  importance	  of	  having	  these	  
competencies	  in	  house.	  
	  
These	  lighting	  design	  divisions	  usually	  consist	  of	  designers,	  architects,	  
engineers,	  electrical	  technicians	  and	  performing	  art	  technicians.	  All	  with	  a	  
self-­‐driven	  focus	  on	  lighting	  design.	  Denmark	  got	  its	  first	  education	  as	  a	  
dedicated	  master	  degree	  in	  lighting	  design	  starting	  summer	  2014	  on	  
==PAGE:45==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
26
Aalborg	  University	  (Hansen	  &	  Mullins,	  2013).	  This	  has	  been	  on-­‐going	  the	  
last	  ten	  years	  because	  of	  the	  obvious	  lack	  in	  dedicated	  educations	  in	  
Denmark	  compared	  to	  elsewhere	  in	  Europe	  and	  especially	  Denmark’s	  
neighbouring	  countries,	  Norway	  and	  Sweden,	  who	  has	  had	  educations	  for	  
several	  years	  (Dansk	  Center	  for	  Lys,	  2012).	  	  
	  
Lighting	  designers	  in	  Denmark	  are	  represented	  by	  the	  Danish	  Lighting	  
Center	  (DCL),	  which	  has	  existed	  since	  1948	  under	  the	  name	  ‘Lysteknisk	  
Selskab’	  (Society	  of	  Lighting	  Technicians).	  In	  2013	  DCL	  counted	  around	  
1000	  Danish	  members (Bay, 2013).	  
2.3.4. LIGHTING DESIGNER’S WORK AND BIM 
This	  paragraph	  will	  not	  go	  into	  detail	  about	  the	  specifics	  of	  a	  lighting	  
designer’s	  work	  –	  this	  will	  be	  covered	  later	  in	  the	  study.	  	  
	  
Lighting	  designers	  are	  practising	  design,	  which	  in	  its	  nature	  relies	  on	  a	  very	  
iterative	  process.	  As	  (Bates & Meliani, 2013)	  describe	  in	  their	  article	  about	  
BIM	  and	  the	  Lighting	  Professional:	  “The	  more	  a	  design	  is	  reviewed	  and	  
revised,	  the	  more	  refined	  it	  becomes”	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  2013,	  p.	  13).	  Figure	  8	  
describes	  this	  process	  as	  a	  spiral	  where	  the	  dashed	  line	  represents	  the	  
shortest	  and	  theoretically	  preferable	  way	  from	  start	  to	  finished	  design.	  
However,	  reality	  shows	  it	  is	  more	  likely	  to	  be	  the	  spiralled	  route.	  	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  8	  -­‐	  Iterative	  design	  process	  often	  used	  in	  lighting	  design.	  [Source:	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  2013)]	  
Iterations	  are	  all	  about	  changes	  and	  redoing	  work.	  It	  is	  essential	  to	  have	  the	  
right	  information	  at	  the	  right	  time	  and	  to	  communicate	  changes	  and	  
alterations	  dynamically	  so	  no	  work	  is	  wasted.	  Where	  BIM	  comes	  in	  handy,	  
is	  its	  ability	  to	  facilitate	  these	  needs	  in	  a	  lighting	  design	  iterative	  process.	  
Changes	  in	  the	  architectural	  design	  can	  be	  updated	  simultaneously	  with	  the	  
progress	  and	  communication	  between	  stakeholders	  about	  the	  newest	  
changes	  are	  significantly	  less	  time	  consuming	  than	  what	  was	  experienced	  
with	  2D	  drawings.	  Also	  the	  time	  consumed	  in	  the	  design	  process	  will	  be	  
reduced,	  as	  decisions	  can	  be	  taken	  quicker	  and	  earlier	  in	  the	  process	  (Bates	  
&	  Meliani,	  2013).	  	  
	  
==PAGE:46==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  27 
Lighting	  design	  in	  an	  AEC	  project	  can	  roughly	  be	  divided	  into	  three	  stages	  
as	  seen	  on	  Figure	  9,	  all	  with	  different	  benefits	  of	  using	  BIM:	  
	  
	  
Figure	  9	  -­‐	  Lighting	  design	  process	  [Source:	  (Architectural	  Engineering	  Design	  Group	  Inc.,	  n.d.)]	  
Previous	  studies	  has	  investigated	  in	  which	  fields	  of	  engineering	  BIM	  is	  
particular	  useful.	  A	  survey	  in	  2010	  (Kreider,	  Messner,	  &	  Dubler,	  2010)	  
asked	  a	  vast	  number	  of	  industry	  professionals	  in	  the	  U.S.	  (100+)	  how	  often	  
they	  used	  BIM	  in	  lighting	  analysis	  and	  how	  they	  rated	  the	  benefits	  of	  using	  
BIM.	  The	  benefits	  were	  rated	  as	  Positive	  (2),	  Neutral	  (0)	  and	  Negative	  (-­‐2).	  
The	  results	  showed,	  that	  17	  %	  of	  the	  industry	  professionals	  used	  BIM	  in	  
their	  lighting	  analysis	  work,	  with	  an	  overall	  benefit	  rating	  of	  0.73.	  These	  
results	  show,	  that	  the	  lighting	  industry	  has	  not	  adopted	  the	  potential	  of	  
BIM.	  The	  potentials	  in	  the	  three	  Lighting	  Design	  stages	  are	  as	  follows:	  
Design phase 
The	  design	  phase	  is	  the	  most	  creative	  part	  where	  the	  lighting	  designer	  is	  
defining	  and	  developing	  the	  overall	  lighting	  concept.	  This	  happens	  in	  close	  
collaboration	  with	  the	  architect	  and	  electrical	  engineers.	  	  
	  
The	  architectural	  space	  and	  volume	  is	  of	  great	  interest	  for	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  and	  3D	  modelling	  facilitates	  a	  pleasant	  way	  to	  visualise	  the	  given	  
space	  (Architectural	  Engineering	  Design	  Group	  Inc.,	  n.d.).	  It	  is	  much	  easier	  
to	  understand	  the	  volume	  when	  interactively	  navigating	  in	  a	  3D	  model.	  A	  
central	  model	  can	  also	  be	  dynamically	  updated,	  so	  every	  time	  a	  change	  is	  
made	  to	  the	  architectural	  design,	  the	  lighting	  designer	  will	  be	  updated	  
immediately	  and	  alter	  the	  lighting	  concept	  accordingly	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  
2013).	  	  
	  
A	  lighting	  designer	  will	  then	  become	  more	  important	  in	  the	  early	  stages	  of	  
the	  AEC	  project.	  Conventionally,	  a	  lighting	  designer	  creates	  his/her	  work	  as	  
part	  of	  an	  architectural	  or	  electrical	  project,	  and	  usually	  not	  accounting	  for	  
drawings	  themselves.	  This	  could	  change	  with	  the	  implementation	  of	  BIM,	  as	  
the	  lighting	  designer	  would	  design	  and	  draw	  directly	  in	  a	  3D	  model,	  thus	  
creating	  a	  new	  demand	  for	  a	  lighting	  specialist	  and	  lower	  the	  work	  
responsibilities	  of	  the	  other	  disciplines	  (Granese,	  2011).	  	  
Communication phase 
When	  a	  design	  is	  defined	  it	  needs	  to	  be	  communicated	  to	  all	  stakeholders.	  
Usually	  an	  architect	  needs	  visualisations	  and	  photorealistic	  renderings	  to	  
communicate	  the	  lighting	  concept.	  The	  building	  owner	  or	  electrical	  
engineers	  needs	  calculations	  showing	  the	  adequate	  lighting	  levels	  being	  
met.	  Working	  in	  a	  BIM	  model	  opens	  up	  to	  cater	  for	  both	  possibilities.	  
	  
DESIGN 
COMMUNICATION 
CONSTRUCTION 
==PAGE:47==
STATE OF THE ART REVIEW // 2 
	  
	  
28
Inserting	  3D	  luminaire	  objects	  directly	  into	  the	  BIM	  model	  can	  create	  
lighting	  concept	  illustrations.	  These	  objects	  should	  contain	  basic	  
photometric	  information	  to	  create	  realistic	  renderings	  of	  the	  light.	  The	  
architectural	  model	  would	  ideally	  already	  contain	  information	  about	  
surface	  reflections	  and	  materials	  and	  will	  be	  updated	  simultaneously	  
whenever	  a	  change	  appears.	  	  
Multiple	  spaces	  and	  lighting	  concepts	  can	  be	  visualised	  simultaneously	  and	  
ease	  the	  work	  process	  for	  the	  lighting	  designer	  by	  not	  moving	  the	  3D	  
geometry	  from	  one	  software	  to	  another	  to	  perform	  renderings	  
(Architectural	  Engineering	  Design	  Group	  Inc.,	  n.d.)	  (Howard	  &	  Sexton,	  
2013).	  
	  
All	  these	  benefits	  also	  go	  for	  detailed	  lighting	  level	  calculations,	  as	  this	  
could	  be	  embedded	  in	  the	  BIM	  software	  as	  well.	  It	  would	  be	  a	  much	  easier	  
workflow	  if	  lighting	  analysis	  were	  performed	  based	  on	  a	  dynamically	  
updated	  model	  and	  limit	  the	  information	  exchanged	  between	  different	  
software	  packages	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  2013).	  
Construction phase 
3D	  luminaire	  objects	  used	  to	  create	  visualisations	  and	  calculations	  can	  be	  
utilised	  to	  carry	  additional	  information	  useful	  in	  the	  tendering	  and	  
construction	  phase.	  This	  additional	  information	  can	  completely	  replace	  the	  
datasheets	  typically	  attached	  to	  2D	  drawings	  showing	  what	  the	  2D	  
signatures	  on	  a	  drawing	  symbolises	  (Granese,	  2011).	  This	  technical	  
information	  can	  allow	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  create	  circuit	  plans	  directly	  in	  
the	  3D	  model	  and	  determine	  necessary	  control	  gear	  and	  switchboards	  in	  
collaboration	  with	  an	  electrical	  engineer	  (Granese,	  2011).	  	  
	  
Physical	  attributes	  of	  the	  light	  fixtures	  could	  also	  be	  embedded	  in	  the	  3D	  
objects,	  which	  could	  result	  in	  accurate	  placement	  of	  the	  fixtures,	  and	  more	  
detailed	  ceiling	  plans	  directly	  in	  the	  BIM	  model.	  BIM	  allows	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  to	  draw	  directly	  in	  3D,	  plan	  and	  section,	  and	  conflicts	  and	  clashes	  
with	  other	  elements,	  such	  as	  structural	  or	  mechanical,	  can	  be	  avoided	  or	  at	  
least	  detected	  earlier	  in	  the	  project	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  2013)	  (Granese,	  
2011).	  	  
2.3.5. CHALLENGES WITH BIM IMPLEMENTATION IN LIGHTING DESIGN 
Overall	  evaluation	  of	  using	  BIM	  in	  lighting	  design	  shows	  many	  potential	  
benefits.	  However,	  using	  BIM	  is	  not	  without	  complications.	  	  
	  
One	  of	  the	  largest	  critics	  about	  using	  BIM	  is	  the	  fact	  that	  lighting	  designers	  
needs	  to	  adapt	  to	  new	  knowledge,	  new	  software	  and	  eventually	  new	  
hardware.	  This	  is	  costly	  and	  could	  potentially	  eliminate	  smaller	  practices	  in	  
being	  part	  of	  the	  new	  BIM	  development	  (Granese,	  2011).	  	  
	  
The	  idea	  with	  BIM	  is	  that	  all	  information	  is	  embedded	  in	  the	  model.	  This,	  
however,	  makes	  the	  model	  very	  complex	  for	  lighting	  designer’s	  to	  use	  and	  
very	  complicated	  to	  navigate	  or	  make	  calculations	  in	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  
2013)	  (Lighting,	  2013)	  (Howard	  &	  Sexton,	  2013).	  Lighting	  designers	  
conventionally	  uses	  software	  packages	  that	  calculates	  lighting	  based	  on	  a	  
==PAGE:48==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  29 
simple	  3D	  model,	  such	  as	  DIALux	  or	  Relux,	  which	  are	  both	  free	  software.	  So	  
a	  solution	  could	  be	  to	  develop	  plug-­‐ins	  or	  support	  interoperability	  between	  
BIM	  and	  the	  conventional	  tools,	  so	  that	  lighting	  designers	  would	  be	  able	  to	  
work	  in	  their	  ‘normal’	  software	  packages	  (Lighting,	  2013).	  This	  requires,	  
that	  all	  necessary	  information	  from	  the	  BIM	  model	  is	  transferred	  correct	  
and	  seamlessly.	  In	  general,	  the	  lighting	  industry	  would	  benefit	  from	  
interoperability	  so	  that	  models	  could	  be	  exchanged	  across	  software	  
platforms	  and	  not	  lose	  information	  on	  the	  way.	  “BIM	  is	  useless	  if	  practices	  
cannot	  easily	  communicate	  and	  share	  information	  because	  of	  compatibility	  
issues”	  (Granese,	  2011,	  s.	  2).	  	  
	  
Interoperability	  and	  standardisation	  also	  goes	  for	  the	  3D	  luminaire	  objects	  
in	  a	  BIM	  model.	  Currently	  all	  the	  major	  lighting	  manufacturers	  create	  files	  
of	  their	  luminaires	  for	  DIALux	  and	  Relux	  containing	  photometric	  data	  and	  
eventually	  geometric	  properties.	  If	  manufacturers	  were	  to	  create	  object	  
files	  for	  use	  in	  BIM	  models,	  the	  information	  in	  these	  files	  has	  to	  be	  
streamlined	  –	  and	  not	  dependent	  on	  the	  single	  manufacturer’s	  or	  
consultancy	  company’s	  way	  to	  do	  things	  (Granese,	  2011)	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  
2013).	  The	  lack	  of	  luminaires	  that	  lighting	  designers	  can	  chose	  from	  in	  the	  
simulation	  software	  also	  limits	  the	  use	  of	  that	  particular	  manufacturer	  in	  
the	  project	  (Granese,	  2011).	  The	  3D	  objects	  have	  to	  contain	  information	  
about	  dimensions,	  photometric	  data,	  global	  type	  parameters	  and	  unique	  
type	  parameters.	  Several	  instances	  are	  at	  the	  moment	  trying	  to	  standardise	  
what	  information	  is	  necessary	  and	  what	  should	  be	  embedded	  in	  these	  3D	  
objects.	  For	  instance,	  CIBSE	  (Chartered	  Institution	  of	  Building	  Services	  
Engineers)	  are	  currently	  sending	  out	  surveys	  to	  the	  lighting	  industry	  in	  
Great	  Britain	  about	  what	  data	  is	  necessary	  to	  include	  in	  lighting	  objects	  
(Lighting,	  2014).	  The	  fixtures	  in	  a	  3D	  model	  should	  be	  assigned	  either	  on	  a	  
level	  base	  (independent	  components,	  independent	  on	  architectural	  
changes)	  or	  on	  a	  face-­‐base	  where	  the	  fixture	  follows	  changes	  in	  the	  
architecture	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  2013).	  	  
	  
In	  general,	  software	  cannot	  replace	  the	  knowledge	  of	  a	  lighting	  
professional,	  and	  cannot	  substitute	  the	  classical	  work	  process	  of	  sketching,	  
discussing	  and	  visualising	  light	  concepts	  (Bates	  &	  Meliani,	  2013).	  Lighting	  
design	  can	  potentially	  benefit	  from	  BIM,	  but	  the	  future	  requires	  focus	  on	  
standardisation	  from	  both	  software	  providers	  and	  fixture	  manufacturers.	  In	  
order	  to	  make	  BIM	  work	  it	  is	  also	  necessary	  to	  clarify	  deliveries,	  ownership	  
and	  responsibilities,	  which	  calls	  for	  a	  focus	  on	  the	  work	  process	  itself	  (Bates	  
&	  Meliani,	  2013).	  It	  should	  be	  completely	  clear	  what	  information	  is	  needed	  
in	  the	  BIM	  model	  at	  certain	  stages	  of	  the	  lighting	  design.	  This	  is	  where	  IDM	  
packages	  and	  work	  process	  standardisations	  could	  potentially	  be	  a	  solution.	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:49==
3
INDUSTRY
SURVEY
==PAGE:50==
==PAGE:51==
INDUSTRY SURVEY // 3 
	  
	  
30
3. 
INDUSTRY SURVEY 
3.1. INTRODUCTION 
This	  chapter	  will	  cover	  an	  industry	  survey	  with	  a	  selection	  of	  industry	  
professionals.	  The	  main	  goal	  of	  interviewing	  industry	  experts	  is	  to	  identify	  
state-­‐of-­‐the-­‐art	  working	  methods	  and	  current	  information	  exchange	  
processes,	  which	  will	  later	  be	  used	  to	  develop	  IDM	  packages.	  	  
	  
The	  industry	  survey	  follows	  the	  state	  of	  the	  art	  review	  because	  experience	  
gained	  in	  that	  chapter	  helps	  sharpen	  the	  focus	  of	  the	  interviews.	  It	  is	  a	  
necessity	  to	  involve	  industry	  experts	  in	  the	  development	  of	  IDMs	  in	  order	  to	  
identify	  and	  define	  proper	  exchange	  requirements	  (Aram,	  Eastman,	  Sacks,	  
Panushev,	  &	  Venugopal,	  2010).	  	  
	  
Danish	  industry	  experts	  with	  relation	  to	  lighting	  design	  are	  selected	  in	  
order	  to	  get	  as	  relevant	  input	  as	  possible.	  It	  should	  also	  be	  strived	  to	  select	  
industry	  experts	  with	  a	  grade	  of	  experience	  with	  BIM	  working	  processes	  so	  
that	  dynamic	  discussions	  about	  the	  benefits	  or	  disadvantages	  are	  possible.	  	  
	  
Overall,	  these	  surveys	  should	  result	  in	  quite	  detailed	  specifications	  of	  the	  
individual	  industry	  professional’s	  requirements	  for	  information	  exchange	  –	  
both	  what	  is	  required	  to	  perform	  the	  work	  and	  what	  is	  required	  by	  others	  
to	  be	  delivered.	  	  
3.2. SURVEY METHOD 
3.2.1. QUESTIONNARIE DEVELOPMENT 
A	  consistent	  survey	  structure	  has	  to	  be	  developed	  in	  order	  to	  streamline	  the	  
results	  from	  the	  surveys.	  The	  questions	  asked	  need	  to	  be	  constructed	  so	  
that	  irrelevant	  deviations	  from	  the	  path	  towards	  the	  goal	  of	  the	  survey	  is	  
minimised.	  That	  being	  said,	  the	  survey	  structure	  should	  at	  the	  same	  time	  
allow	  for	  interesting	  deviations	  to	  be	  explored,	  which	  could	  potentially	  
yield	  some	  unpredicted	  perspectives	  on	  the	  whole	  case.	  The	  structure	  
should	  be	  used	  as	  a	  guideline	  and	  allow	  the	  questions	  to	  be	  flexible	  and	  able	  
to	  be	  modified	  depending	  on	  the	  specific	  interview	  situation.	  	  
	  
A	  step-­‐wise	  interview	  structure	  were	  chosen	  starting	  with	  open-­‐ended	  
questions	  and	  ending	  with	  more	  specific,	  case-­‐oriented	  leading	  questions.	  
The	  individual	  steps	  are	  illustrated	  on	  Figure	  10.	  All	  questions	  are	  designed	  
for	  industry	  experts	  with	  relation	  to	  lighting	  design	  in	  AEC	  projects;	  hence	  
knowledge	  to	  the	  lighting	  industry	  is	  mandatory.	  A	  Danish	  elaboration	  of	  
the	  questions	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  A.	  
==PAGE:52==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  31 
	  
	  
Figure	  10	  -­‐	  Survey	  step	  overview	  illustrating	  on	  which	  information	  level	  the	  different	  questions	  are	  
placed.	  
Stage 1 questions: Identification 
The	  purpose	  of	  these	  questions	  is	  to	  identify	  the	  industry	  expert,	  both	  in	  
terms	  of	  personal	  identification	  as	  well	  as	  his/her	  position	  and	  function	  
within	  AEC	  projects.	  It	  is	  important	  for	  the	  further	  work	  and	  usability	  of	  the	  
results	  to	  be	  certain	  of	  the	  industry	  expert’s	  significance	  and	  importance	  
within	  the	  company	  and	  the	  companies’	  projects.	  Questions	  in	  this	  stage	  
could	  be	  as	  follows:	  
	  
-­‐ 
“Name,	  education,	  position,	  specialisation,	  work	  experience	  etc.	  “	  
-­‐ 
“What	  is	  your	  primary	  type	  of	  tasks?”	  
-­‐ 
“In	  which	  AEC	  life	  cycle	  process	  are	  your	  tasks	  primarily	  located?”	  
-­‐ 
“What	  type	  and	  size	  of	  AEC	  projects	  are	  you	  typically	  involved	  in?”	  
-­‐ 
“What	  is	  the	  core	  purpose	  of	  your	  work?”	  
Stage 2 questions: Working process 
Stage	  2	  questions	  aims	  to	  dig	  deeper	  into	  the	  industry	  expert’s	  working	  
routines	  and	  to	  identify	  whether	  the	  use	  of	  BIM	  is	  present.	  It	  is	  also	  relevant	  
to	  identify	  working	  routines	  that	  carry	  signs	  of	  standardised	  processes,	  
either	  company-­‐specific	  or	  industry-­‐wide.	  Or	  the	  expert	  could	  also	  have	  
his/her	  own	  personal	  preferences	  on	  how	  to	  approach	  and	  carry	  out	  a	  new	  
task.	  Questions	  in	  this	  stage	  could	  be:	  
	  
-­‐ 
“When	  assigned	  a	  new	  task,	  how	  do	  you	  initiate	  your	  work	  and	  
collaboration	  with	  others?“	  
-­‐ 
“To	  what	  grade	  are	  your	  working	  methods	  influenced	  by	  company	  
standards,	  or	  national	  standards?”	  
==PAGE:53==
INDUSTRY SURVEY // 3 
	  
	  
32
-­‐ 
“To	  what	  grade	  are	  your	  working	  methods	  influenced	  by	  personal	  
preferences?	  Can	  you	  exemplify	  a	  working	  approach	  that	  you	  have	  
developed?”	  
-­‐ 
“How	  is	  your	  working	  routines	  changed	  depending	  on	  project	  type	  and/or	  
project	  size?”	  
Stage 3 questions: Information exchange 
Information	  exchanges	  between	  the	  industry	  expert	  and	  relevant	  project	  
actors	  will	  be	  identified	  through	  stage	  3	  questions.	  These	  questions	  are	  
designed	  to	  gradually	  narrow	  down	  answer	  possibilities	  in	  order	  to	  drag	  
out	  information	  from	  the	  industry	  expert	  that	  he/she	  would	  not	  have	  
provided	  in	  the	  first	  place.	  By	  asking	  “what	  kind	  of	  information	  do	  you	  need”	  
opens	  up	  for	  a	  vast	  amount	  of	  answer	  possibilities,	  why	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  
take	  control	  and	  only	  ask	  questions	  that	  can	  provide	  answers	  related	  to	  the	  
scope	  of	  the	  interview.	  Questions	  in	  this	  stage	  could	  be	  narrowed	  down	  like	  
this:	  
	  
-­‐ 
“Who	  is	  typically	  using	  the	  results	  from	  your	  work?	  How	  do	  you	  adjust	  
your	  work	  depending	  on	  the	  receiver?“	  
-­‐ 
“What	  kind	  of	  demands	  and	  requirements	  about	  your	  work	  exists	  and	  who	  
is	  specifying	  them?”	  
-­‐ 
“How	  determined	  are	  you	  on	  the	  required	  output	  from	  your	  task?	  Are	  you	  
always	  aware	  of	  what	  to	  deliver	  and	  when?”	  
-­‐ 
Do	  you	  have	  examples	  of	  output	  information	  that	  you	  cannot	  deliver?	  
What	  is	  typically	  the	  reason(s)	  for	  this?”	  
-­‐ 
“How	  determined	  are	  you	  on	  your	  required	  inputs	  to	  perform	  your	  task?”	  
-­‐ 
“How	  do	  you	  obtain	  necessary	  inputs	  and	  who	  is	  typically	  the	  supplier	  of	  
information?”	  
-­‐ 
“Do	  you	  have	  examples	  of	  input	  information	  that	  you	  occasionally	  cannot	  
obtain?	  What	  is	  your	  next	  step	  if	  you	  cannot	  obtain	  this	  information?”	  
Stage 4 questions: Tools  
Usually	  when	  sufficient	  information	  is	  gathered	  the	  next	  step	  is	  to	  use	  this	  
information	  in	  specific	  tools.	  This	  stage	  aims	  to	  identify	  what	  kind	  of	  tools	  
the	  industry	  expert	  uses	  and	  to	  what	  purpose.	  This	  stage	  also	  includes	  a	  
direct	  confrontation	  with	  the	  industry	  expert,	  to	  what	  excess	  he/she	  uses	  
BIM	  software	  or	  other	  3D	  modelling	  in	  their	  work.	  Stage	  4	  questions	  opens	  
up	  for	  a	  discussion	  in	  the	  end,	  hopefully	  exposing	  some	  hidden	  point	  of	  
views	  from	  the	  industry	  expert	  due	  to	  this	  stage-­‐wise	  information	  
extraction.	  If	  the	  industry	  expert	  were	  to	  be	  asked	  “What	  do	  you	  feel	  about	  
BIM	  and	  your	  working	  methods”	  in	  the	  beginning	  of	  the	  interview,	  the	  
answer	  would	  more	  than	  likely	  have	  caused	  deviations	  from	  the	  initial	  goal	  
of	  the	  survey.	  Questions	  asked	  in	  this	  stage	  would	  be:	  
	  
-­‐ 
“What	  kind	  of	  tools	  do	  you	  usually	  prefer	  when	  performing	  your	  task?”	  
-­‐ 
“What	  are	  the	  key	  benefits	  of	  these	  tools,	  and	  how	  do	  they	  support	  your	  
working	  methods?”	  
-­‐ 
“What	  are	  the	  inconveniences	  or	  disadvantages	  of	  these	  tools?	  How	  would	  
you	  see	  them	  solved	  or	  made	  better?”	  
-­‐ 
“To	  what	  extent	  do	  you	  use	  BIM	  or	  other	  3D	  modelling	  in	  your	  work?”	  
==PAGE:54==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  33 
-­‐ 
“How	  do	  you	  think,	  BIM	  would	  be	  beneficial	  in	  your	  work?”	  	  
-­‐ 
“If	  BIM	  were	  to	  solve	  some	  of	  the	  problems	  with	  your	  tools	  as	  stated	  
before,	  how	  likely	  would	  you	  be	  to	  change	  your	  working	  methods	  
accordingly?”	  
-­‐ 
“What	  would	  be	  the	  large	  turning	  point	  for	  you	  to	  change	  working	  
methods?”	  
Stage 5: Conclusion  
Last	  stage	  is	  not	  directly	  a	  set	  of	  questions	  but	  rather	  an	  introduction	  to	  
discussion,	  provided	  it	  has	  not	  escalated	  during	  the	  previous	  stage.	  The	  
discussion	  should	  end	  with	  a	  conclusion	  about	  how	  the	  industry	  expert	  sees	  
his/her	  own	  working	  routines	  and	  how	  they	  could	  be	  optimised/changed	  
for	  better	  workflow	  and	  information	  exchange.	  Hopefully	  during	  this	  
survey,	  the	  industry	  expert	  should	  gradually	  open	  up	  and	  assess	  his/her	  
own	  working	  methods	  more	  critically.	  
3.2.2. SURVEY PARTICIPANT SELECTION 
Industry	  experts	  from	  three	  major	  Danish	  companies	  working	  with	  or	  
involved	  in	  lighting	  design	  were	  chosen	  to	  partake	  in	  this	  survey.	  The	  
participants	  were	  chosen	  based	  on	  the	  diagram	  in	  Figure	  11	  stating	  the	  
main	  actors	  in	  lighting	  design	  processes,	  being	  lighting	  designers,	  electrical	  
engineers	  and	  product	  manufacturers.	  	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  11	  -­‐	  Main	  actors	  in	  lighting	  design	  concepts	  and	  their	  collaboration	  and	  interactions.	  	  
	  
Figure	  11	  illustrates	  the	  involved	  actors	  in	  a	  lighting	  design	  project.	  The	  
three	  actors	  work	  in	  close	  collaboration	  hence	  the	  circular	  array	  
composition.	  The	  terminology	  used	  in	  this	  figure	  is	  borrowed	  from	  software	  
development	  that	  typically	  divides	  projects	  into	  three	  requirements:	  
==PAGE:55==
INDUSTRY SURVEY // 3 
	  
	  
34
functional,	  operational	  and	  technical	  requirements.	  These	  three	  
terminologies	  are	  also	  useful	  when	  describing	  the	  actors	  within	  artificial	  
lighting	  projects:	  	  
	  
The	  creative	  lighting	  designers	  (LD)	  develop	  the	  conceptual	  lighting	  design	  
to	  comply	  with	  the	  provided	  architecture	  and	  add	  aesthetical	  value	  to	  the	  
building	  when	  natural	  lighting	  is	  not	  present.	  Lighting	  designers	  define	  
what	  the	  lighting’s	  functional	  requirements	  are.	  	  
	  
Electrical	  engineers	  (EE)	  work	  with	  lighting	  designers	  to	  make	  the	  design	  
work	  in	  practise	  and	  provide	  solutions	  to	  get	  the	  system	  to	  be	  operational.	  
Operational	  also	  means	  ready	  to	  operate	  hence	  making	  sure	  the	  lighting	  
system	  complies	  with	  all	  relevant	  requirements.	  	  
	  
Product	  manufacturers	  (PRM)	  are	  the	  ones	  delivering	  the	  lighting	  fixtures	  
and	  additional	  technical	  solutions	  to	  support	  both	  the	  ideas	  of	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  and	  the	  requirements	  from	  the	  electrical	  engineer.	  	  
	  
Based	  on	  this	  model,	  the	  participants	  from	  the	  Danish	  lighting	  design	  
industry	  as	  seen	  in	  Table	  2	  were	  chosen:	  
	  
Table	  2	  -­‐	  Selected	  industry	  experts	  to	  participate	  in	  the	  surveys.	  
ROLE
