3.3. SURVEY RESULTS 
An	  overview	  of	  survey	  responses	  from	  the	  industry	  experts	  will	  be	  
presented	  in	  the	  following	  subsections.	  This	  section	  is	  the	  author’s	  
summary	  and	  concluding	  interpretation	  of	  responses	  based	  on	  the	  
relevance	  to	  the	  overall	  subject	  of	  this	  study.	  Viewpoints	  are	  not	  listed	  
based	  on	  who	  said	  what	  but	  rather	  to	  ensure	  a	  natural	  flow	  through	  the	  
section.	  A	  more	  thorough	  presentation	  of	  the	  survey	  responses	  is	  available	  
in	  Appendix	  B.	  All	  responses	  have	  been	  summarised	  and	  categorised	  to	  fit	  
into	  four	  major	  categories	  for	  the	  sake	  of	  readability	  and	  flow:	  	  
	  
o Working	  methods	  
o Information	  exchange	  
o Computer-­‐aided	  design	  workflow	  	  
o Tools	  used	  in	  lighting	  design	  
==PAGE:56==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  35 
3.3.1. GENERAL 
All	  of	  the	  appointed	  industry	  experts	  worked	  with	  artificial	  lighting	  at	  some	  
point	  during	  an	  AEC	  project	  life	  cycle.	  Lighting	  designers	  were	  most	  
prominently	  used	  to	  define	  overall	  lighting	  requirements	  in	  the	  outline	  
feasibility	  stage.	  In	  larger	  projects	  this	  would	  be	  where	  the	  majority	  of	  focus	  
lies	  and	  where	  the	  work	  of	  a	  lighting	  designer	  is	  most	  influential.	  Defining	  
lighting	  requirements	  means	  determining	  what	  kind	  of	  light	  should	  be	  used	  
in	  the	  project	  and	  how	  much.	  This	  stage	  is	  where	  lighting	  designers	  were	  
able	  to	  be	  fully	  creative	  in	  close	  collaboration	  with	  the	  project’s	  architects	  
and	  building	  owner.	  Lighting	  designers	  are	  also	  widely	  present	  during	  the	  
outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  (ISO	  life	  cycle	  stage	  ‘E’)	  and	  through	  to	  the	  
coordinated	  design	  stage	  (ISO	  life	  cycle	  stage	  ‘G’)	  –	  the	  latter	  having	  least	  
influence	  on	  design	  alterations.	  Often	  lighting	  designer’s	  work	  is	  even	  
present	  in	  construction	  or	  post-­‐construction	  phases	  where	  they	  are	  
supposed	  to	  focus	  or	  adjust	  the	  light	  fixtures	  in	  accordance	  to	  their	  own	  
requirements.	  	  
	  
Figure	  12	  illustrates	  an	  overview	  of	  the	  presence	  and	  involvement	  of	  
lighting	  designers	  in	  various	  life	  cycle	  stages,	  based	  on	  information	  
extracted	  from	  the	  survey.	  Each	  ring	  of	  the	  spider-­‐diagram	  in	  represents	  
the	  grade	  of	  involvement,	  where	  the	  inner	  ring	  is	  the	  least	  and	  outer	  ring	  
the	  most.	  Note	  that	  the	  life	  cycle	  stage	  ‘K’	  -­‐	  disposal	  is	  not	  included,	  since	  
this	  is	  the	  less	  relevant	  life	  cycle	  stage	  for	  lighting	  designers.	  One	  can	  see	  
from	  the	  figure	  that	  a	  lighting	  designer’s	  involvement	  and	  influences	  is	  
substantial	  in	  life	  cycle	  stage	  ‘C’	  to	  ‘E’;	  then	  reduced	  significantly	  between	  ‘F’	  
to	  ‘G’;	  and	  then	  again	  present	  during	  the	  construction	  phase.	  
	  
Figure	  12	  –	  Spider-­‐diagram	  showing	  involvement	  of	  lighting	  designers	  in	  ISO	  life	  cycle	  stages	  A-­‐J.	  
Electrical	  engineers	  are	  used	  to	  make	  the	  work	  of	  a	  lighting	  designer	  
operational.	  This	  means	  help	  defining	  the	  concept	  through	  the	  outline	  
conceptual	  design	  stage	  in	  close	  collaboration	  with	  lighting	  designers.	  
Electrical	  engineers	  gets	  more	  influenced	  in	  the	  lighting	  design	  when	  
proceeding	  through	  to	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage.	  They	  have	  the	  
==PAGE:57==
INDUSTRY SURVEY // 3 
	  
	  
36
responsibility	  to	  verify	  that	  the	  selected	  fixtures	  comply	  with	  energy	  
consumption	  and	  budget,	  and	  finally	  design	  the	  reflected	  ceiling	  plan	  
together	  with	  other	  related	  disciplines.	  Surely,	  they	  continue	  having	  a	  close	  
communication	  with	  the	  lighting	  designers	  but	  the	  demand	  for	  creativity	  is	  
limited.	  	  	  
	  
Product	  manufacturers	  are	  not	  directly	  involved	  in	  the	  AEC	  project	  
alongside	  with	  the	  others.	  They	  have	  a	  more	  supporting	  role	  providing	  
technical	  solutions	  if	  the	  lighting	  designer	  or	  electrical	  engineers	  need	  
specific	  knowledge	  about	  products	  available	  to	  support	  their	  needs.	  It	  is	  
mainly	  during	  the	  procurement	  where	  product	  manufacturers	  are	  used	  and	  
they	  have	  to	  work	  together	  with	  contractors	  to	  bid	  on	  projects.	  
Occasionally,	  lighting	  designers	  have	  very	  specific	  requirements	  for	  a	  
lighting	  solution	  that	  no	  product	  available	  on	  the	  market	  can	  comply	  with.	  
This	  is	  where	  the	  product	  manufacturer	  could	  –	  depending	  on	  the	  size	  of	  
the	  project	  –	  develop	  a	  special	  off-­‐the-­‐market	  solution	  for	  this	  particular	  
project.	  If	  this	  is	  the	  case,	  product	  manufacturers	  is	  utilised	  as	  early	  in	  the	  
project	  lifecycle	  as	  possible.	  	  	  
3.3.2. WORKING METHODS 
Lighting	  design	  is	  a	  creative	  process	  since	  it	  is	  about	  designing	  with	  light	  
and	  to	  adapt	  the	  lighting	  solution	  for	  a	  specific	  architectural	  concept.	  All	  
industry	  experts	  agreed	  on	  not	  having	  a	  linear	  ‘project	  recipe’	  but	  rather	  
having	  more	  iterative	  approaches	  highly	  depending	  on	  the	  complexity	  and	  
scale	  of	  the	  project.	  Simple	  office	  buildings	  would	  be	  more	  straightforward	  
and	  reliant	  on	  personally	  developed	  procedures	  than	  larger	  outdoor	  
projects	  that	  need	  more	  complicated	  and	  innovative	  solutions.	  But	  at	  the	  
same	  time,	  they	  agreed	  on	  almost	  always	  having	  an	  idea	  of	  direction	  in	  their	  
work	  and	  how	  to	  proceed	  to	  the	  final	  goal.	  
	  
The	  industry	  experts	  also	  stated	  that	  the	  grade	  of	  linearity	  depends	  on	  what	  
stage	  of	  the	  project	  a	  lighting	  designer	  is	  activated.	  If	  used	  in	  the	  outline	  
feasibility	  or	  outline	  conceptual	  phase	  the	  work	  is	  very	  creative	  and	  the	  
working	  methods	  are	  mostly	  non-­‐linear	  with	  a	  different	  approach	  every	  
time.	  However,	  if	  a	  lighting	  designer	  were	  only	  supposed	  to	  simulate	  
lighting	  conditions	  or	  make	  ceiling	  plans,	  this	  would	  call	  for	  more	  linear	  
working	  processes	  with	  specific	  procedures.	  Again	  this	  is	  of	  course	  
dependent	  on	  the	  complexity	  of	  the	  building	  and	  thereby	  geometry	  
available.	  On	  Figure	  13	  one	  can	  see	  the	  level	  of	  linearity	  in	  working	  
methods	  in	  relation	  to	  the	  ISO	  life	  cycle	  stage.	  This	  illustration	  is	  created	  
based	  on	  information	  from	  the	  survey.	  Each	  ring	  represents	  the	  level	  of	  
linearity	  where	  the	  inner	  ring	  is	  the	  least	  and	  outer	  ring	  the	  most.	  A	  lighting	  
designer’s	  work	  gets	  more	  linear	  and	  standardised	  further	  into	  the	  project.	  
In	  stage	  C	  or	  D	  the	  working	  method	  depends	  highly	  on	  complexity	  and	  scale	  
of	  the	  project,	  where	  as	  in	  G	  and	  H	  the	  process	  is	  more	  straightforward.	  	  
	  
==PAGE:58==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  37 
	  
	  
Figure	  13	  –	  Spider-­‐diagram	  showing	  level	  of	  linearity	  in	  lighting	  designer’s	  working	  methods	  in	  relation	  
to	  ISO	  life	  cycle	  stages	  A-­‐J.	  
Even	  though	  the	  lighting	  designer’s	  working	  methods	  were	  not	  
‘standardised’	  as	  per	  a	  pre-­‐set	  recipe	  to	  solve	  a	  problem,	  many	  of	  the	  survey	  
subjects	  agreed	  to	  have	  re-­‐occurring	  approaches	  when	  assigned	  new	  
projects.	  Even	  creative	  lighting	  designers	  had	  some	  personal	  preferences	  on	  
how	  to	  tackle	  new	  problems	  and	  usually	  knew	  with	  what	  and	  where	  to	  
start.	  Each	  subject’s	  working	  methods	  were	  mostly	  influenced	  by	  personal	  
preferences	  based	  on	  work	  experience	  –	  regardless	  of	  which	  company	  of	  
origin.	  	  
	  
Those	  tasks	  that	  requires	  a	  high	  grade	  of	  collaboration	  with	  other	  
disciplines	  within	  the	  same	  company	  show	  indications	  of	  working	  methods	  
that	  is	  influenced	  by	  either	  a	  standardised	  method	  on	  company-­‐level	  or	  
agreed	  methods	  between	  project	  team	  members.	  All	  surveyed	  experts	  
agreed	  on	  not	  following	  a	  pre-­‐defined	  routine.	  	  
 
One	  factor	  that	  deviated	  most	  among	  the	  lighting	  designers	  was	  how	  they	  
communicated	  their	  design	  externally	  to	  the	  architect	  or	  building	  owner.	  
One	  lighting	  designer	  used	  physical	  tests	  and	  principal	  mock-­‐ups	  to	  solve	  
complicated	  lighting	  conditions	  and	  present	  solutions	  for	  the	  customer.	  
Another	  lighting	  designer	  relied	  more	  on	  renderings	  produced	  in	  3D	  
modelling	  software.	  These	  two	  approaches	  require	  different	  working	  
methods	  again	  dependent	  on	  personal	  preference.	  But	  on	  an	  overall	  scale,	  
both	  lighting	  designers	  started	  with	  the	  same	  approach	  and	  finished	  with	  
the	  same	  results	  regardless	  of	  how	  personal	  their	  working	  processes	  were.	  	  
3.3.3. INFORMATION EXCHANGE 
Even	  though	  the	  lighting	  design	  development	  process	  is	  quite	  creative	  with	  
few	  signs	  of	  standardisation,	  it	  seems	  for	  all	  survey	  subjects	  to	  be	  clear	  
what	  information	  is	  required	  to	  perform	  the	  task	  and	  what	  the	  preferred	  
outcome	  should	  be.	  	  
==PAGE:59==
INDUSTRY SURVEY // 3 
	  
	  
38
First	  necessary	  input	  for	  a	  lighting	  designer	  is	  to	  see	  the	  architectural	  vision	  
in	  terms	  of	  sketches,	  renderings	  or	  drawings	  to	  determine	  what	  space	  
needs	  to	  be	  lit.	  The	  building	  owner	  should	  specify	  the	  function	  of	  the	  
building	  and	  spaces	  as	  well	  as	  the	  intended	  users.	  Other	  inputs	  from	  the	  
architects	  could	  be	  the	  preferred	  shape	  of	  a	  light	  fixture,	  or	  if	  there	  were	  
any	  preferences	  on	  where	  to	  mount	  the	  fixtures;	  e.g.	  in	  the	  ceiling,	  wall	  or	  
floor.	  	  
	  
Next,	  a	  lighting	  designer	  has	  to	  know	  the	  required	  energy	  frame	  for	  the	  
building	  and	  how	  much	  is	  assigned	  for	  lighting	  systems.	  A	  watt	  per	  square	  
meter	  value	  is	  usually	  negotiated	  with	  the	  building	  owner	  together	  with	  
electrical	  engineers.	  The	  energy	  frame	  for	  lighting	  is	  calculated	  based	  on	  a	  
rough	  initial	  building	  simulation	  or	  using	  standard	  values.	  It	  should	  state	  
how	  much	  is	  assigned	  for	  each	  discipline	  in	  order	  to	  comply	  with	  the	  overall	  
energy	  frame.	  Based	  on	  maximum	  energy	  consumption	  for	  lighting,	  the	  
designer	  can	  continue	  defining	  lighting	  zones	  in	  the	  building	  and	  how	  much	  
light	  intensity	  is	  needed.	  Usually	  this	  is	  supported	  by	  additional	  information	  
in	  terms	  of	  national	  standards	  defining	  required	  minimum	  values.	  
Alternatively	  this	  could	  also	  be	  personal	  preference	  of	  the	  lighting	  designer	  
who	  determines	  the	  minimum	  values	  based	  on	  the	  overall	  lighting	  concept	  
of	  the	  building.	  	  
	  
Figure	  14	  shows	  that	  when	  all	  this	  information	  is	  in	  possession	  of	  the	  
lighting	  designer	  he/she	  starts	  the	  creative	  process	  and	  ends	  up	  having	  a	  
lighting	  strategy.	  The	  strategy	  should	  tell	  something	  about	  the	  light	  
intensities	  in	  the	  spaces;	  the	  colour	  of	  light	  or	  colour	  temperature	  of	  white	  
light;	  what	  kind	  of	  light	  fixtures	  are	  intended;	  where	  they	  are	  placed;	  and	  
also	  what	  kind	  of	  light	  emitting	  source	  is	  used.	  By	  providing	  an	  approximate	  
amount	  of	  fixtures	  and	  what	  kind	  of	  light	  source	  intended,	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  or	  electrical	  engineer	  is	  able	  to	  estimate	  energy	  consumption	  for	  
lighting.	  This	  value	  needs	  to	  be	  compliance	  checked	  with	  the	  overall	  energy	  
frame.	  Also	  the	  controlling	  concept	  should	  be	  roughly	  defined	  in	  the	  lighting	  
strategy.	  Other	  significant	  outcomes	  resulting	  from	  the	  lighting	  strategy	  
could	  be	  renderings	  or	  physical	  mock-­‐ups	  showing	  the	  lighting	  effects	  in	  the	  
finished	  project.	  	  	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  14	  –	  Overview	  diagram	  of	  information	  needed	  by	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  design	  a	  lighting	  
strategy.	  
When	  the	  architect	  and	  building	  owner	  has	  approved	  the	  lighting	  
strategy/concept	  and	  electrical	  engineers	  are	  satisfied	  with	  the	  energy	  
consumption	  the	  concept	  can	  to	  be	  detailed.	  This	  is	  typically	  done	  in	  the	  
==PAGE:60==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  39 
outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage.	  A	  lighting	  designer	  performs	  simulations	  
with	  an	  array	  of	  light	  fixtures	  to	  visualise	  the	  light	  intensities	  in	  the	  
building.	  For	  this	  he/she	  needs	  an	  architectural	  model	  showing	  relevant	  
interior	  and	  fixed	  furniture.	  This	  model	  should	  also	  include	  the	  intended	  
floor	  to	  ceiling	  height	  to	  know	  the	  space	  volume.	  Available	  space	  above	  the	  
recessed	  ceiling	  allocated	  for	  light	  fixtures	  is	  also	  useful	  information.	  This	  
value	  can	  potentially	  limit	  the	  selection	  of	  fixtures	  possible	  due	  to	  physical	  
dimensions.	  Recessed	  ceiling	  height	  is	  usually	  given	  by	  the	  electrical	  
engineers	  or	  architects	  through	  a	  long	  process	  of	  coordinating	  the	  space	  
with	  other	  engineering	  disciplines.	  	  
	  
Interior	  finishes	  in	  terms	  of	  paint	  colours	  or	  materials	  used	  is	  a	  necessary	  
input	  from	  the	  architect.	  This	  information,	  however,	  is	  not	  always	  easy	  to	  
get	  early	  in	  the	  project	  why	  the	  lighting	  designer	  has	  to	  make	  some	  
precautions.	  Usually	  personal	  work	  experience	  or	  commonly	  anticipated	  
values	  comes	  in	  handy.	  Interior	  surface	  reflectances	  have	  to	  be	  determined	  
as	  precise	  as	  possible	  or	  it	  can	  have	  serious	  impacts	  on	  the	  final	  result	  if	  
wrong	  or	  changed	  later	  in	  the	  project.	  All	  the	  surveyed	  lighting	  designers	  
agreed	  on	  it	  being	  a	  problem	  that	  architects	  have	  not	  yet	  determined	  
interior	  materials	  precisely	  enough	  when	  lighting	  simulations	  is	  due	  to	  be	  
initiated,	  hence	  not	  having	  standard	  procedures	  for	  delivering	  this	  
information.	  	  	  
	  
Figure	  15	  shows	  that	  when	  all	  this	  information	  is	  obtained	  a	  lighting	  
simulation	  can	  be	  performed.	  A	  usual	  output	  from	  lighting	  simulations	  can	  
be	  illumination	  levels	  on	  all	  surfaces	  within	  a	  space.	  This	  is	  used	  to	  show	  
either	  the	  architect	  or	  building	  owner	  that	  light	  levels	  are	  satisfactory	  and	  
complies	  with	  national	  requirements.	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  15	  –	  Required	  information	  to	  perform	  a	  lighting	  simulation	  and	  end	  up	  with	  information	  about	  
lighting	  conditions	  
Electrical	  engineers	  receive	  the	  placement	  of	  fixtures	  on	  a	  ceiling	  drawing	  
and	  a	  list	  of	  all	  fixtures	  used	  in	  the	  simulation,	  including	  wattage,	  type	  and	  
size.	  This	  will	  be	  used	  to	  calculate	  energy	  consumption	  and	  electrical	  
circuits	  for	  lighting	  as	  well	  as	  determine	  what	  kind	  of	  system	  should	  control	  
the	  lights.	  Electrical	  engineers	  also	  produce	  final	  ceiling	  plans	  in	  thorough	  
coordination	  with	  other	  engineering	  disciplines	  present	  in	  the	  ceiling	  –	  such	  
as	  HVAC,	  fire	  and	  IT/AV	  systems.	  This	  part	  of	  the	  project	  is	  where	  the	  
lighting	  designer	  is	  not	  very	  active.	  In	  the	  case	  where	  the	  electrical	  engineer	  
has	  to	  change	  some	  of	  the	  fixtures	  due	  to	  budget,	  energy	  consumption	  or	  
==PAGE:61==
INDUSTRY SURVEY // 3 
	  
	  
40
physical	  size,	  he	  turns	  to	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  modify	  his	  simulations	  to	  
check	  if	  the	  changes	  affect	  the	  results.	  	  
	  
A	  final	  light	  simulation	  is	  performed	  by	  the	  lighting	  designer	  when	  ceiling	  
plans	  are	  finally	  coordinated	  and	  energy	  consumption	  is	  accepted.	  The	  
lighting	  design	  is	  considered	  finished	  if	  illumination	  levels	  are	  still	  
satisfactory.	  Final	  output	  from	  this	  process	  will	  include	  a	  detailed	  tendering	  
document	  with	  all	  relevant	  data	  about	  the	  light	  fixtures	  used.	  This	  is	  given	  
to	  the	  bidding	  contractor	  to	  show	  what	  requirements	  they	  have	  to	  comply	  
with.	  Light	  fixture	  requirements,	  placement	  and	  amount	  of	  fixtures	  make	  up	  
the	  basis	  for	  a	  later	  price	  calculation.	  This	  is	  usually	  where	  a	  product	  
manufacturer	  is	  entering	  the	  AEC	  project.	  	  
	  
A	  consistent	  input,	  which	  is	  of	  great	  importance	  for	  all	  industry	  experts	  
throughout	  the	  project,	  is	  economy	  and	  budget.	  Especially	  the	  surveyed	  
lighting	  designers	  were	  focused	  on	  both	  energy	  requirements	  and	  budget	  to	  
determine	  their	  lighting	  concepts.	  All	  of	  the	  industry	  experts	  repeatedly	  
mentioned	  the	  importance	  of	  economy	  when	  defining	  the	  concept,	  choosing	  
fixtures	  and	  detailing	  the	  solution,	  as	  lighting	  is	  often	  subject	  to	  be	  under-­‐
prioritised	  in	  AEC	  projects.	  The	  quality	  level	  and	  amount	  of	  creativity	  and	  
innovative	  solutions	  is	  largely	  dependent	  on	  money	  available.	  	  
3.3.4. COMPUTER AIDED DESIGN WORKFLOW 
At	  a	  current	  state	  the	  surveyed	  lighting	  designers	  do	  not	  use	  BIM	  working	  
methods	  in	  their	  work.	  As	  illustrated	  on	  Figure	  16,	  the	  conventional	  way	  to	  
work	  is	  to	  receive	  2D	  drawings	  from	  an	  architect	  showing	  basic	  geometry,	  
which	  will	  be	  re-­‐modelled	  in	  light	  simulation	  software.	  Afterwards	  the	  final	  
light	  calculations	  will	  be	  plotted	  onto	  a	  2D	  ceiling	  plan	  where	  light	  fixtures	  
are	  placed	  as	  well.	  	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  16	  -­‐	  Conventional	  computer	  aided	  workflow	  among	  lighting	  designers	  and	  the	  rest	  of	  a	  BIM-­‐
based	  project,	  e.g.	  architects,	  structural	  engineers	  and	  mechanical	  engineers.	  	  
==PAGE:62==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  41 
A	  downside	  of	  this	  workflow	  is	  that	  relevant	  changes	  to	  the	  architectural	  
geometry	  is	  not	  updated	  simultaneously	  and	  might	  not	  be	  revealed	  to	  the	  
lighting	  designer.	  This	  requires	  much	  more	  communication	  between	  the	  
architect	  and	  lighting	  designer.	  Especially	  relevant	  because	  a	  lighting	  
designer’s	  work	  is	  usually	  initiated	  while	  the	  architectural	  detailing	  is	  not	  
yet	  fixed.	  Both	  surveyed	  subjects	  from	  Ramboll	  mentioned	  issues	  with	  a	  
large	  research	  facility	  in	  Copenhagen,	  where	  the	  architect	  changed	  floor	  to	  
ceiling	  heights	  several	  times.	  This	  had	  great	  impact	  on	  both	  placement	  and	  
amount	  of	  fixtures	  needed	  to	  obtain	  satisfactory	  light	  levels.	  This	  was	  of	  
course	  an	  extreme	  example,	  but	  smaller	  adjustments	  happen	  all	  the	  time	  
and	  it	  is	  not	  always	  the	  case	  that	  lighting	  designers	  gets	  instantaneous	  
updates.	  
	  
Even	  though	  lighting	  designers	  do	  not	  use	  BIM	  workflow	  at	  the	  moment	  
they	  are	  still	  faced	  with	  3D	  models.	  Occasionally	  lighting	  designer’s	  work	  on	  
projects	  where	  a	  3D	  model	  is	  prepared.	  But	  they	  do	  not	  utilise	  the	  
information	  embedded.	  Lighting	  designers	  simulate	  lighting	  conditions	  in	  a	  
3D	  model	  space,	  which	  they	  build	  themselves.	  Complexity	  and	  level	  of	  detail	  
are	  the	  core	  reasons	  for	  why	  they	  do	  not	  use	  the	  provided	  3D	  model	  
directly.	  One	  surveyed	  subject	  pinpointed	  that	  in	  order	  to	  import	  a	  
complete	  3D	  model	  this	  would	  cause	  the	  lighting	  software	  to	  run	  slower.	  
Also	  it	  would	  increase	  the	  calculation	  time	  due	  to	  the	  program	  calculating	  
the	  whole	  model/building	  even	  if	  smaller	  changes	  are	  made.	  So	  in	  all	  cases	  
it	  is	  considered	  easier	  and	  simpler	  to	  create	  single	  representative	  rooms	  
directly	  in	  the	  lighting	  software	  package.	  	  
	  
After	  finished	  simulation	  in	  lighting	  software	  the	  fixtures	  need	  to	  be	  placed	  
on	  a	  ceiling	  plan.	  Either	  through	  a	  direct	  export	  from	  the	  software	  to	  a	  
DWG-­‐file	  or	  manually	  by	  drawing	  the	  fixtures	  on	  a	  provided	  2D	  ceiling	  plan.	  
Both	  methods	  present	  the	  light	  fixtures	  as	  symbols	  on	  a	  drawing	  with	  
separate	  data	  documents	  describing	  important	  properties	  about	  each	  
fixture	  type.	  	  
	  
If	  3D	  projecting	  or	  BIM	  workflows	  are	  or	  were	  to	  be	  used	  on	  a	  project,	  a	  
separate	  technical	  drawer	  will	  receive	  the	  information	  about	  placement	  and	  
type	  of	  fixtures	  from	  either	  the	  lighting	  designer	  or	  electrical	  engineer.	  This	  
drawer	  will	  then	  place	  the	  objects	  into	  the	  3D	  model	  and	  all	  objects	  will	  
have	  relevant	  properties	  attached	  as	  well	  as	  having	  correct	  physical	  
dimensions.	  It	  is	  especially	  the	  electrical	  engineers	  who	  benefit	  from	  this	  
embedded	  information	  as	  it	  helps	  when	  making	  collision	  tests	  and	  
coordinating	  with	  other	  components	  in	  the	  ceilings.	  	  
	  
On	  the	  previous	  mentioned	  research	  facility	  building	  by	  Ramboll	  this	  
workflow	  was	  used.	  The	  electrical	  engineers	  would	  provide	  input	  about	  
placement	  and	  type	  of	  fixtures	  to	  the	  technical	  drawer	  who	  inserted	  objects	  
in	  the	  3D	  model.	  These	  light	  fixture	  objects	  include	  so	  many	  details	  that	  a	  
direct	  print	  out	  from	  the	  model	  can	  replace	  the	  otherwise	  separate	  
documents	  conventionally	  enclosed	  with	  2D	  drawings.	  The	  surveyed	  
subjects	  showed	  enthusiasm	  in	  this	  workflow	  and	  agreed	  on	  3D	  modelling	  
==PAGE:63==
INDUSTRY SURVEY // 3 
	  
	  
42
being	  the	  basis	  for	  collision	  testing	  and	  ceiling	  coordination	  as	  well	  as	  a	  
basis	  for	  tendering	  documents	  in	  the	  future.	  It	  also	  opened	  up	  for	  the	  
contractors	  to	  bid	  digitally	  and	  extract	  relevant	  data	  directly	  from	  the	  
model	  instead	  of	  interacting	  with	  electrical	  engineers	  or	  lighting	  designer	  to	  
get	  additional	  information.	  	  
	  
Product	  manufacturers	  are	  already	  adapting	  to	  this	  enlarged	  focus	  on	  use	  of	  
3D	  modelling	  software.	  The	  surveyed	  Danish	  company	  Louis	  Poulsen	  has	  
already	  built	  up	  all	  their	  light	  fixtures	  as	  Revit	  objects	  with	  associated	  
properties.	  It	  is	  the	  industry	  demand	  that	  dictates	  which	  software	  or	  tool	  
the	  product	  manufacturers	  should	  adapt	  to.	  According	  to	  them,	  Revit	  is	  the	  
most	  widely	  used	  software.	  There	  is,	  however,	  one	  issue	  among	  the	  product	  
manufacturers,	  which	  is	  the	  lack	  of	  standardisation	  behind	  the	  information	  
provided.	  Louis	  Poulsen	  attaches	  one	  set	  of	  properties	  for	  each	  light	  fixture	  
object,	  but	  other	  manufacturers	  might	  attach	  their	  own	  versions.	  This	  way,	  
there	  is	  no	  consistency	  in	  which	  information	  is	  delivered	  to	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  or	  electrical	  engineer	  thus	  making	  the	  products	  harder	  to	  
compare.	  	  
3.3.5. TOOLS USED IN LIGHTING DESIGN 
Through	  the	  industry	  expert	  survey	  a	  set	  of	  different	  tools	  used	  in	  the	  
lighting	  design	  process	  were	  identified:	  
DIALux 
German	  DIALux	  is	  a	  freeware	  lighting	  calculation	  software	  developed	  by	  
DIAL	  GmbH.	  It	  is	  able	  to	  calculate	  both	  indoor	  and	  outdoor	  lighting.	  The	  
program	  calculates	  lighting	  based	  on	  a	  3D	  geometry	  and	  its	  affiliated	  
materials	  and	  reflectances.	  The	  geometry	  can	  be	  built	  directly	  in	  the	  
software	  based	  on	  an	  imported	  2D	  CAD-­‐drawing.	  Complete	  3D	  models	  can	  
also	  be	  imported.	  Currently	  the	  software	  supports	  import	  of	  .3ds	  or	  .gbXML	  
files,	  the	  latter	  being	  able	  to	  import	  assigned	  materials	  or	  reflectances	  from	  
the	  native	  3D	  modelling	  software.	  All	  major	  light	  fixture	  manufacturers	  
have	  catalogue	  plugins	  available	  making	  selection	  of	  light	  fixtures	  easy	  and	  
streamlined.	  Light	  calculations	  are	  done	  using	  the	  light	  fixture’s	  
photometric	  data	  included	  in	  an	  .ies	  or	  .ldt	  photometric	  file.	  It	  is	  possible	  to	  
create	  photorealistic	  renderings	  of	  the	  light	  settings	  through	  a	  POV-­‐
Raytracer	  plugin.	  	  
3ds Max 
3ds	  Max	  is	  a	  3D	  computer	  graphics	  program	  developed	  by	  Autodesk.	  A	  
lighting	  designer	  mainly	  uses	  this	  software	  when	  creating	  photorealistic	  
visualisations	  of	  light	  settings.	  Due	  to	  the	  program’s	  complexity	  it	  is	  not	  
very	  commonly	  used	  for	  simple	  lighting	  simulations.	  Primarily	  it	  is	  only	  
used	  if	  realistic	  images	  displaying	  correct	  predictions	  of	  light,	  shadows	  and	  
materials	  are	  needed.	  3ds	  Max	  happens	  to	  support	  the	  native	  workflow	  of	  
Autodesk	  software,	  which	  means	  it	  is	  able	  to	  import	  Revit	  models	  directly.	  
It	  can	  simulate	  lighting	  conditions	  directly	  based	  on	  light	  fixtures	  inserted	  
in	  Revit.	  None	  of	  the	  surveyed	  industry	  experts	  used	  this	  software	  in	  their	  
work,	  mainly	  due	  to	  its	  features	  not	  weighing	  up	  for	  the	  complexity	  
compared	  to	  other	  software.	  	  	  
==PAGE:64==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  43 
MagiCAD for Revit 
MagiCAD	  is	  a	  plugin	  used	  in	  Autodesk	  Revit	  to	  design	  building	  installations	  
–	  in	  this	  case	  the	  survey	  participants	  used	  MagiCAD	  Electrical.	  Developed	  by	  
Program	  Oy	  in	  Finland,	  this	  software	  is	  commonly	  used	  in	  Scandinavian	  
projects.	  One	  clear	  benefit	  of	  this	  software	  is	  that	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  work	  with	  
real	  products	  provided	  by	  a	  given	  manufacturer,	  with	  geometrical	  accurate	  
dimensions	  and	  properties	  associated.	  Symbols	  in	  2D	  view	  and	  objects	  in	  
3D	  view	  represent	  the	  objects.	  This	  feature,	  however,	  is	  only	  available	  if	  the	  
product	  manufacturer	  has	  made	  objects	  specifically	  to	  suit	  the	  MagiCAD	  
database	  and	  not	  as	  Revit	  families.	  Technical	  drawers	  are	  the	  usual	  users	  of	  
this	  software	  occasionally	  assisted	  by	  electrical	  engineers.	  It	  is	  possible	  to	  
export	  a	  MagiCAD	  model	  with	  associated	  objects	  into	  DIALux	  to	  perform	  
lighting	  simulations.	  	  	  
3.4. DISCUSSION 
The	  survey	  granted	  the	  authors	  with	  valuable	  information	  about	  the	  
lighting	  design	  process	  from	  start	  project	  until	  tendering.	  After	  getting	  to	  
hear	  the	  expert’s	  viewpoints	  it	  was	  made	  clear	  that	  a	  lighting	  designer’s	  
work	  changes	  significantly	  depending	  on	  life	  cycle	  stage,	  why	  it	  will	  be	  
necessary	  to	  create	  several	  IDM	  packages	  describing	  different	  life	  cycle	  
stages.	  It	  seems	  clear,	  that	  the	  lighting	  concept	  is	  developed	  early	  in	  the	  AEC	  
life	  cycle,	  then	  defined	  further	  and	  evaluated	  in	  the	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  
and	  lastly	  detailed	  in	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  with	  other	  disciplines.	  	  
	  
In	  general,	  it	  was	  difficult	  to	  get	  the	  industry	  experts	  to	  answer	  streamlined	  
on	  questions	  about	  working	  methods.	  It	  is	  the	  impression	  of	  the	  authors	  
that	  industry	  experts	  see	  themselves	  and	  their	  work	  as	  definite	  hence	  
indicating	  a	  relatively	  conservative	  approach.	  It	  was	  difficult	  for	  all	  industry	  
experts	  to	  sit	  down	  and	  map	  their	  work	  on	  a	  piece	  of	  paper.	  They	  
repeatedly	  indicated	  that	  their	  work	  was	  unpredictable	  and	  iterative,	  which	  
required	  a	  lot	  of	  interaction	  and	  meetings	  with	  other	  disciplines.	  Regardless	  
to	  mention,	  the	  creative	  designers	  were	  the	  ones	  with	  the	  most	  iterative	  
working	  methods.	  A	  creative	  process	  is	  difficult	  to	  map	  down	  into	  a	  flow	  
chart	  and	  all	  industry	  experts	  proclaimed	  that	  a	  flow	  chart	  was	  too	  simple	  
to	  illustrate	  their	  work.	  	  
	  
Even	  though	  it	  was	  generally	  difficult	  to	  get	  the	  industry	  experts	  to	  admit	  to	  
streamline	  their	  work,	  it	  ended	  up	  being	  possible	  due	  to	  intelligently	  
designed	  questions.	  Digging	  deeper	  into	  the	  industry	  expert’s	  knowledge	  
and	  viewpoints	  revealed	  recurrent	  processes	  each	  industry	  expert	  went	  
through.	  Also	  regardless	  of	  project	  complexity	  and	  size	  which	  they	  admitted	  
to	  in	  the	  end.	  Again	  depending	  on	  the	  profession	  –	  lighting	  designers	  were	  
the	  ones	  with	  less	  repetitive	  work	  and	  electrical	  engineers	  with	  the	  most.	  	  
	  
Actors	  who	  are	  least	  willing	  to	  adapt	  to	  new	  digital	  methods	  are	  the	  creative	  
lighting	  designers.	  They	  have	  a	  workflow,	  which	  is	  dependent	  on	  creativity	  
hence	  software	  is	  not	  allowed	  to	  limit	  their	  creativity.	  If	  the	  software	  is	  too	  
complex	  compared	  to	  the	  potential	  benefit	  from	  a	  lighting	  designer’s	  point	  
of	  view,	  it	  will	  be	  very	  difficult	  for	  them	  to	  adapt.	  They	  are	  using	  simple,	  
==PAGE:65==
INDUSTRY SURVEY // 3 
	  
	  
44
easy-­‐to-­‐use	  programs	  that	  deliver	  all	  outputs	  necessary	  for	  them	  to	  perform	  
their	  work.	  It	  is	  only	  going	  to	  be	  an	  incentive	  if	  the	  industry	  demands	  new	  
outputs	  from	  the	  lighting	  designer	  that	  requires	  use	  of	  complex	  software.	  
Another	  incentive	  to	  adapt	  to	  BIM	  working	  methods	  could	  be	  to	  minimise	  
errors	  due	  to	  geometric	  issues	  in	  the	  models	  performed	  during	  the	  design	  
process.	  It	  is	  often	  the	  case	  that	  the	  recess	  depth	  in	  ceilings	  is	  not	  provided	  
accurately	  by	  the	  architect	  and	  lighting	  designers	  then	  chooses	  fixtures	  that	  
will	  not	  fit.	  This	  can	  be	  avoided	  by	  placing	  dimensionally	  correct	  lighting	  
objects	  directly	  in	  the	  3D	  architectural	  model.	  However,	  this	  requires	  a	  new	  
working	  method	  among	  lighting	  designers	  and	  suddenly	  places	  drawing	  
responsibility	  on	  lighting	  designers	  instead	  of	  technical	  drawers.	  	  	  	  
	  
It	  is	  noticeable	  how	  BIM	  software	  is	  used	  more	  frequently	  in	  the	  late	  part	  of	  
the	  project	  and	  less	  complex	  tools	  are	  used	  in	  the	  more	  creative	  phases	  of	  
the	  project.	  Despite	  that	  BIM	  supporting	  tools	  such	  as	  MagiCAD	  could	  
potentially	  meet	  all	  requirements	  for	  a	  lighting	  designer:	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  
import	  a	  3D	  geometry,	  place	  fixtures	  and	  import/export	  to	  DIALux	  to	  
perform	  simulations.	  All	  the	  time	  updated	  with	  a	  central	  model	  in	  case	  of	  
architectural	  changes.	  Why	  this	  method	  has	  not	  been	  adapted	  is	  hard	  to	  say,	  
but	  it	  might	  be	  related	  to	  the	  complexity	  as	  well.	  It	  is	  very	  difficult	  to	  
compare	  with	  the	  simplicity	  and	  user-­‐interface	  of	  a	  program	  as	  DIALux.	  So	  
even	  if	  it	  takes	  effort	  to	  re-­‐model	  the	  geometry	  every	  time	  architectural	  
changes	  occur	  it	  might	  not	  be	  worth	  it	  for	  lighting	  designers	  to	  spend	  time	  
transitioning	  into	  new	  complicated	  software	  in	  the	  long	  run.	  Hopefully,	  the	  
other	  way	  around	  will	  appeal	  to	  lighting	  designers:	  that	  it	  takes	  more	  time	  
to	  learn	  a	  complex	  program	  in	  the	  beginning	  but	  will	  save	  time	  in	  the	  long	  
run	  –	  but	  only	  if	  it	  generates	  more	  profit	  or	  time	  can	  be	  allocated	  for	  
optimisations	  purpose.	  	  
3.5. SUMMARY 
Based	  on	  the	  survey	  of	  industry	  experts	  the	  following	  overall	  conclusions	  
can	  be	  made:	  
	  
o Creative	  lighting	  design	  processes	  has	  the	  least	  linear	  working	  
methods	  and	  changes	  the	  approach	  according	  to	  type	  of	  project	  and	  
complexity.	  
o The	  most	  creative	  work	  is	  performed	  in	  the	  outline	  feasibility	  and	  
outline	  conceptual	  design	  phases.	  
o Verifying	  light	  conditions	  have	  more	  linear	  working	  methods	  and	  a	  
more	  uniform	  approach.	  Creating	  ceilings	  plans	  have	  the	  most	  
linearity.	  	  
o Complete	  3D	  models	  of	  buildings	  are	  too	  complex	  to	  simulate	  light	  
conditions	  early	  in	  the	  AEC	  project	  life	  cycle.	  Usually	  it	  is	  only	  
necessary	  to	  work	  on	  single	  rooms	  rather	  than	  whole	  buildings.	  
There	  is	  a	  great	  need	  to	  develop	  a	  method	  to	  simplify	  architectural	  
models	  so	  that	  lighting	  designers	  can	  extract	  the	  information	  only	  
relevant	  for	  them	  to	  perform	  their	  work.	  	  
o BIM	  models	  and	  3D	  objects	  are	  useful	  when	  collision	  testing	  with	  
other	  building	  installation	  components	  in	  the	  ceiling.	  It	  is	  also	  
==PAGE:66==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  45 
effortless	  to	  extract	  tender	  documents	  and	  datasheets	  from	  the	  
model.	  
o The	  conventional	  approach	  is	  to	  use	  different	  software	  tools	  with	  
each	  of	  their	  different	  purpose,	  even	  though	  software	  that	  combines	  
all	  is	  available.	  Due	  to	  unnecessary	  complexity	  it	  is	  not	  tempting	  for	  
lighting	  designers	  to	  use	  these	  tools.	  	  
o There	  is	  no	  standardisation	  of	  what	  information	  is	  provided	  about	  
lighting	  fixtures	  why	  it	  makes	  it	  difficult	  for	  lighting	  designers	  to	  
obtain	  adequate	  information	  and	  compare	  products	  from	  different	  
lighting	  manufacturers.	  A	  standardisation	  would	  be	  very	  beneficial	  
for	  both	  manufacturers	  as	  well	  as	  the	  engineers,	  who	  avoid	  pursuing	  
different	  properties	  among	  different	  manufacturers

IDM
DEVELOPMENT

4. 
IDM DEVELOPMENT 
4.1. INTRODUCTION 
This	  chapter	  will	  describe	  the	  third	  and	  final	  step	  towards	  creating	  IDM	  
packages	  based	  on	  knowledge	  gained	  in	  the	  literature	  review	  and	  industry	  
surveys.	  At	  this	  point,	  the	  knowledge	  gathered	  should	  be	  sufficient	  to	  
critically	  acclaim	  how	  industry	  experts	  within	  the	  lighting	  industry	  work	  
and	  how	  to	  display	  their	  working	  processes	  graphically	  through	  IDMs.	  	  
	  
Section	  4.2	  will	  describe	  the	  method	  used	  to	  design	  two	  IDM	  packages	  
describing	  lighting	  design	  in	  the	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  and	  the	  
coordinated	  design	  stage.	  The	  IDM	  packages	  are	  designed	  in	  accordance	  
with	  templates	  from	  buildingSMART	  (buildingSMART,	  2013)	  and	  the	  ISO	  
29481-­‐1	  ‘Information	  Delivery	  Manual’-­‐standard	  (ISO,	  2010);	  the	  
framework	  proposed	  by	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  2014a)	  and	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  
2014b);	  and	  guidelines	  provided	  through	  the	  Ph.D.	  study	  this	  project	  is	  part	  
of.	  In	  section	  4.3	  and	  4.4	  the	  resulting	  two	  IDM	  packages	  will	  be	  displayed	  
and	  finally	  followed	  by	  a	  common	  discussion	  and	  summary.	  	  
4.2. METHOD 
As	  described	  in	  subsection	  2.2.3	  an	  IDM	  package	  consists	  of	  four	  main	  parts:	  
The	  Use	  Case	  (UC),	  a	  Process	  Map	  (PM),	  Exchange	  Requirements	  (ER)	  and	  
Exchange	  Requirement	  Models	  (ERM).	  However,	  for	  the	  sake	  of	  simplifying	  
the	  further	  IDM	  design	  the	  ERs	  and	  ERMs	  will	  joined	  and	  from	  now	  on	  be	  
described	  as	  Exchange	  Requirements	  (ER)	  (Treldal,	  Mondrup,	  Karlshøj,	  &	  
Vestergaard,	  2014).	  ERs	  consist	  of	  IRMs	  and	  ORMs.	  When	  designing	  IDM	  
packages	  it	  is	  strived	  to	  use	  a	  standardised	  method	  in	  order	  to	  secure	  
consistency	  in	  the	  result.	  The	  overall	  method	  used	  in	  this	  study	  to	  maintain	  
information	  consistency	  is	  illustrated	  on	  Figure	  17.	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  17	  -­‐	  Overall	  methods	  used	  to	  design	  IDM	  packages	  	  
==PAGE:70==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  47 
USE CASE (UC) 
The	  first	  part	  –	  Use	  Case	  –	  is	  following	  the	  framework	  earlier	  described	  in	  
subsection	  2.2.2.	  The	  UC	  defines	  a	  business	  scenario	  in	  a	  particular	  
discipline	  described	  by	  OmniClass	  (OmniClass,	  2012)	  at	  a	  specific	  ISO	  life	  
cycle	  stage	  (ISO,	  2010).	  
Naming the packages 
An	  IDM	  package	  can	  be	  named	  by	  combining	  both	  the	  letter	  representing	  
ISO	  life	  cycle	  stages	  and	  the	  OmniClass	  discipline	  number	  sequence	  as	  
illustrated	  on	  Figure	  18.	  This	  way	  the	  name	  will	  be	  unique	  for	  each	  package	  
and	  will	  provide	  information	  about	  which	  discipline	  at	  what	  life	  cycle	  stage	  
is	  described.	  For	  further	  description	  of	  OmniClass	  or	  ISO	  life	  cycle	  stages	  
please	  refer	  to	  subsection	  2.2.2	  in	  this	  report.	  
	  
	  
Figure	  18	  -­‐	  IDM	  name	  generic	  system	  
4.2.1. PROCESS MAP (PM) 
The	  PM	  should	  give	  the	  user	  of	  the	  IDM	  package	  a	  quick	  and	  simple	  
overview	  of	  the	  business	  process	  displaying	  all	  activities,	  interactions	  and	  
data	  exchanged	  between	  activities.	  Using	  a	  generic,	  industry-­‐wide	  accepted	  
notation	  to	  graphically	  produce	  these	  PMs	  is	  necessary	  to	  ensure	  
adaptability	  across	  the	  industry.	  Therefore	  it	  has	  been	  decided	  by	  	  	  
buildingSMART	  that	  all	  IDM	  packages	  should	  preferably	  use	  the	  
acknowledged	  Business	  Process	  Model	  and	  Notation	  format	  (Wix	  &	  
Karlshøj,	  2010).	  
Business Process Model and Notation (BPMN) 
BPMN	  is	  invented	  by	  the	  Object	  Management	  Group	  and	  is	  an	  industry-­‐wide	  
accepted	  way	  of	  presenting	  business	  processes	  graphically.	  This	  is	  also	  the	  
reason	  why	  buildingSMART	  has	  acknowledged	  this	  notation	  method	  to	  
support	  their	  work	  with	  IDMs	  (Wix	  J.	  ,	  2007)	  (Wix	  &	  Karlshøj,	  2010).	  
Standardising	  PM	  notation	  makes	  IDMs	  readily	  understandable	  by	  all	  
stakeholders	  in	  a	  project	  and	  widens	  the	  usefulness	  of	  IDMs.	  	  
	  
BPMN	  version	  2.0	  from	  January	  2011	  is	  the	  current	  version,	  which	  is	  used	  
by	  buildingSMART.	  BPMN	  consists	  of	  four	  basic	  element	  categories	  used	  to	  
create	  graphical	  presentations:	  flow	  objects,	  data	  objects,	  connecting	  
objects,	  swim	  lanes	  and	  artefacts.	  Since	  BPMN	  is	  used	  to	  be	  a	  generic	  
notation	  method	  able	  to	  describe	  a	  vast	  amount	  of	  different	  business	  
processes,	  there	  are	  a	  lot	  of	  symbols	  available	  (over	  160	  symbols)	  but	  only	  
a	  fraction	  useful	  for	  IDMs.	  Due	  to	  this,	  a	  study	  carried	  out	  in	  2011	  specified	  
BPMN	  notation	  symbols	  that	  were	  relevant	  for	  use	  in	  IDM	  process	  maps	  
(Young,	  Chi,	  &	  Ghang,	  2011).	  This	  study	  analysed	  existing	  process	  maps	  
published	  on	  buildingSMART’s	  website	  and	  came	  up	  with	  a	  proposal	  for	  
useful	  subsets	  of	  BPMN	  for	  IDM	  development.	  They	  are	  divided	  into	  
==PAGE:71==
IDM DEVELOPMENT // 4 
	  
48
Essential	  Subsets	  and	  Supplementary	  Subsets.	  It	  is	  by	  using	  symbols	  within	  
the	  Essential	  Subset	  this	  project’s	  IDMs	  are	  developed.	  An	  example	  of	  some	  
of	  the	  symbols	  acknowledged	  to	  use	  in	  IDM	  design	  is	  visible	  on	  Figure	  19.	  A	  
more	  thorough	  description	  of	  BPMN	  symbols	  used	  in	  IDM	  design	  can	  be	  
seen	  in	  Appendix	  C.	  A	  web-­‐based	  academic	  version	  of	  the	  software	  called	  
Signavio	  (Signavio,	  n.d.)	  is	  used	  to	  design	  IDM	  packages	  using	  BPMN.	  	  
	  	  
	  
Figure	  19	  -­‐	  Typical	  BPMN	  symbols	  used	  in	  IDM	  packages	  according	  to	  (Young	  et	  al.,	  2011).	  
4.2.2. EXCHANGE REQUIREMENTS (ERs) 
ERs	  cover	  the	  Input	  Requirement	  Model	  (IRM)	  and	  Output	  Requirement	  
Model	  (ORM)	  consumed	  or	  produced	  by	  the	  IDM	  package.	  Since	  these	  data	  
objects	  contains	  important	  information	  it	  is	  necessary	  that	  it	  be	  delivered	  in	  
easy-­‐understandable	  and	  consistent	  formats	  as	  information	  often	  is	  
exchanged	  across	  organisations.	  It	  should	  be	  strived	  to	  keep	  information	  
exchange	  in	  common	  language	  and	  use	  consistent	  terms.	  	  
	  
Data	  exchanged	  between	  actors	  in	  a	  business	  process	  typically	  includes	  
information	  about	  object	  type	  or	  identification,	  geometry	  of	  the	  object	  and	  
lastly	  properties	  of	  the	  object.	  Each	  of	  these	  requires	  standardised	  
frameworks	  and	  in	  this	  study	  it	  has	  been	  chosen	  to	  use	  Cuneco	  
Classification	  System	  (CCS),	  Level	  of	  Development	  (LOD)	  and	  Industry	  
Foundation	  Classes	  (IFC)	  to	  describe	  objects	  as	  illustrated	  in	  Figure	  20.	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  20	  -­‐	  How	  an	  object	  is	  described	  in	  IDM	  design	  and	  which	  formats	  to	  use	  
==PAGE:72==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  49 
Cuneco Classification System (CCS) 
CCS	  is	  a	  classification	  system	  developed	  by	  Cuneco	  (Cuneco,	  2013).	  It	  is	  
made	  to	  standardise	  information	  exchange	  terminology	  in	  the	  built	  
environment	  in	  Denmark.	  All	  terms	  and	  phrases	  related	  to	  an	  AEC	  project	  is	  
classified	  with	  unique	  identifications.	  Hence	  CCS	  is	  useful	  to	  assign	  
standardised	  identification	  names	  to	  all	  spaces,	  entities,	  components,	  etc.	  
within	  the	  built	  environment.	  	  
	  
CCS	  uses	  five	  primary	  constituents:	  classification,	  information	  levels,	  views,	  
properties	  and	  identification,	  as	  seen	  on	  Figure	  21.	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  21	  -­‐	  CCS	  constituents	  [Source:	  (Cuneco, 2013)]	  
The	  classification	  part	  is	  the	  primary	  constituent	  used	  in	  IDM	  design	  for	  this	  
study.	  Classification	  is	  an	  up	  to	  three-­‐lettered	  classification	  code	  used	  as	  
identifier	  for	  objects	  found	  in	  the	  built	  environment.	  This	  three-­‐lettered	  
code	  can	  either	  be	  used	  as	  a	  simple	  code	  or	  be	  combined	  with	  other	  codes	  
and	  thereby	  provide	  information	  about	  relations	  to	  other	  objects,	  hence	  
locating	  the	  object	  in	  a	  building	  or	  a	  system.	  	  
	  
Currently	  CCS	  Classification	  categorises	  objects	  into	  four	  main	  tables:	  	  
	  
o Building	  elements	  
o Use	  of	  spaces	  
o Use	  of	  construction	  entities	  
o Construction	  aids.	  
	  
One	  can	  see	  all	  four	  categories	  on	  Table	  3.	  Here	  it	  is	  also	  possible	  to	  see	  the	  
letters	  in	  the	  classification	  system.	  The	  first	  letter	  in	  a	  classification	  code	  
will	  identify	  the	  type	  of	  component	  or	  space,	  followed	  by	  a	  project-­‐specific	  
identification	  number	  defining	  the	  specific	  object.	  This	  classification	  
==PAGE:73==
IDM DEVELOPMENT // 4 
	  
50
number	  can	  be	  combined	  with	  CCS	  Identification	  or	  even	  CCS	  Properties	  
where	  this	  ID-­‐tag	  can	  create	  unique	  series	  of	  identification	  numbers	  
providing	  further	  information.	  Every	  time	  codes	  are	  combined	  they	  use	  
specific	  prefixes	  to	  sort	  out	  what	  type	  of	  combination	  is	  used.	  	  
	  
In	  addition	  to	  the	  lettered	  classification	  codes,	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  attach	  a	  ‘top	  
node’	  that	  tells	  where	  that	  object	  belongs	  to.	  These	  top	  nodes	  are:	  
	  
[C]:	  
Construction	  complexes	  
[E]:	  
Construction	  entities	  
[S]:	  
Storeys	  
[Z]:	  
Zones	  
[B]:	  
Built	  spaces	  
[A]:	  
Activity	  spaces	  
	  
Table	  3	  -­‐	  Explanation	  of	  the	  CCS	  classification	  tables	  [Source:	  (Cuneco, 2013)]	  
Building	  
Elements	  
Functional	  
Systems	  
A	  -­‐	  P	  
Systems	  described	  on	  an	  
overall	  scale	  
Technical	  
Systems	  
A?	  -­‐	  B?	  
H?	  -­‐	  M?	  
P?	  -­‐	  R?	  
More	  detailed	  technical	  
system	  descriptions	  
Components	  
B??	  -­‐	  C??	  
E??	  -­‐	  H??	  
K??	  
M??	  -­‐	  N??	  
P??	  –	  U??	  
W??	  –	  X??	  
Particular	  components	  
used	  in	  the	  building	  
Use	  of	  Spaces	  
	  
A??	  
C??	  –	  E??	  
Z??	  
Describes	  the	  function	  
and	  use	  of	  spaces.	  	  
Use	  of	  
Construction	  
Entities	  
	  
A??	  –	  E??	  
Z??	  
Describes	  the	  function	  
and	  use	  of	  a	  building.	  
Construction	  
Aids	  
Functional	  
Systems	  
A	  –	  P	  
Systems	  used	  for	  
construction	  and	  not	  
permanently	  required	  in	  
the	  building	  
Components	  
A?	  –	  J?	  
Components	  used	  for	  
construction	  and	  not	  
permanently	  required	  in	  
the	  building	  
	  	  
As	  seen	  in	  Table	  3,	  each	  entity	  code	  could	  have	  up	  to	  three	  letters.	  The	  
amount	  of	  letters	  defines	  the	  entity	  level.	  Level	  1	  has	  one	  letter	  and	  is	  the	  
most	  superior	  level	  describing	  overall	  functional	  systems	  either	  
permanently	  or	  temporarily	  in	  the	  building.	  Overall	  system	  descriptions	  
==PAGE:74==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  51 
could	  be	  entities	  like	  ‘ventilation	  system’	  or	  ‘structural	  system’.	  Level	  2	  
would	  provide	  more	  detailed	  descriptions	  of	  each	  system	  and	  in	  level	  3	  the	  
description	  is	  on	  component	  scale.	  	  
	  
An	  example	  of	  a	  simple	  classification	  code	  could	  be	  ‘UAC’	  meaning:	  
	  
	  
	  
U??	  Fixing	  component	  à	  UA?	  Enclosing	  component	  à	  UAC	  Light	  fixture	  
	  
	  
This	  could	  be	  further	  combined	  with	  other	  classification	  codes	  creating	  a	  
string	  that	  tells	  in	  which	  hierarchy	  this	  light	  fixture	  is	  found.	  For	  example:	  
	  
EAC01.UAC01.HH01	  
	  
Which	  means	  it	  is	  an	  LED	  lamp	  (EAC)	  type	  1	  in	  light	  fixture	  (UAC)	  type	  1	  
located	  in	  the	  lighting	  system	  (HH)	  type	  1.	  Type	  number	  is	  just	  exemplary	  
here.	  	  
	  
Even	  though	  CCS	  is	  primarily	  used	  to	  classify	  and	  identify	  objects	  related	  to	  
buildings	  or	  the	  built	  environment,	  it	  is	  also	  possible	  to	  attach	  different	  
properties	  to	  that	  specific	  object.	  It	  is	  presently	  quite	  limited	  how	  many	  
standardised	  properties	  CCS	  includes,	  why	  it	  is	  strived	  to	  use	  a	  more	  
comprehensive	  system	  to	  define	  property	  information	  in	  IDM	  packages.	  	  
Level of Development (LOD) 
When	  defining	  geometry	  of	  objects	  in	  IDMs	  the	  level	  of	  detail	  is	  one	  way	  to	  
provide	  this.	  Level	  of	  detail	  is	  a	  common	  phrase	  in	  computer	  graphics	  and	  a	  
method	  to	  describe	  the	  complexity	  of	  a	  3D	  object	  representation	  and	  the	  
level	  of	  information	  embedded.	  Therefore	  this	  is	  a	  useful	  way	  to	  describe	  
building	  components	  where	  geometry	  can	  be	  assigned.	  	  
	  
The	  method	  used	  to	  describe	  level	  of	  detail	  in	  IDM	  exchange	  requirements	  
is	  according	  to	  (Mondrup	  et	  al.,	  2014b)	  chosen	  to	  be	  a	  Danish	  catalogue	  of	  
building	  components	  with	  information	  levels	  developed	  by	  MT	  Højgaard	  
(MT	  Højgaard,	  2014).	  This	  catalogue	  describes	  the	  Level	  of	  Development	  
(LOD)	  for	  a	  selection	  of	  components	  and	  systems	  within	  buildings.	  It	  is	  a	  
five-­‐step	  definition	  that	  goes:	  LOD100-­‐LOD200-­‐LOD300-­‐LOD350-­‐LOD400	  
where	  LOD100	  is	  the	  lowest	  level	  applicable.	  The	  Danish	  version	  of	  LODs	  is	  
inspired	  by	  the	  corresponding	  American	  system	  Level	  of	  Development	  
(BIMForum,	  n.d.).	  It	  has	  the	  same	  LOD100-­‐LOD400	  levels	  with	  quite	  similar	  
specifications.	  LOD100	  usually	  defines	  the	  basic	  geometric	  outline	  with	  a	  
simple	  graphical	  representation.	  LOD400	  is	  the	  highest	  level	  with	  very	  
accurate	  geometric	  representation	  in	  scale,	  and	  with	  all	  relevant	  adjoining	  
elements	  represented	  as	  well.	  A	  simple	  example	  of	  this	  system	  is	  shown	  on	  
Figure	  22.	  An	  LOD400	  representation	  is	  usually	  only	  provided	  later	  in	  the	  
project	  around	  the	  construction	  phase.	  	  
Both	  the	  DK	  and	  US	  specifications	  aim	  to	  provide	  valuable	  information	  to	  
help	  specify	  BIM	  deliverables	  and	  to	  create	  a	  common	  ground	  on	  how	  to	  
include	  information	  in	  geometry,	  why	  it	  is	  especially	  applicable	  when	  
defining	  exchange	  requirements	  in	  IDMs.	  	  
==PAGE:75==

LOD	  200	  
LOD	  300

Figure	  22	  –	  Level	  of	  Development	  (LOD)	  system	  exemplified	  by	  showing	  a	  window	  (MT Højgaard, 2014).	  
Industry Foundation Classes (IFC) 
IFC	  is	  used	  to	  specify	  properties	  for	  objects.	  IFC	  version	  4	  (IFC4)	  is	  the	  
newest	  addition	  from	  2013	  and	  holds	  the	  most	  updated	  data.	  Using	  IFC	  
format	  when	  describing	  properties	  means	  that	  the	  developed	  IDM	  packages	  
will	  be	  adaptable	  across	  organisations	  regardless	  of	  which	  software	  they	  
use.	  All	  properties	  will	  also	  use	  the	  same	  terminology	  regardless	  of	  the	  
supplier	  or	  receiver	  of	  the	  IRM	  and	  ORMs	  so	  that	  misunderstandings	  or	  
miscommunication	  is	  reduced.	  Furthermore	  IFC	  is	  ISO	  acclaimed	  (ISO,	  
2013)	  so	  describing	  properties	  using	  IFC	  will	  make	  the	  IDM	  packages	  
internationally	  accepted.	  In	  Denmark	  it	  is	  mandatory	  to	  use	  IFC	  format	  in	  
publicly	  funded	  projects,	  why	  it	  is	  a	  good	  idea	  to	  use	  IFC	  in	  order	  for	  IDM	  
packages	  to	  be	  applied	  on	  larger	  projects.	  
	  
When	  sharing	  information	  about	  objects	  in	  the	  IRMs	  and	  ORMs,	  data	  from	  
the	  IFC	  categories	  illustrated	  on	  Figure	  23	  can	  be	  used.	  
	  
	  
Figure	  23	  -­‐	  Five	  ways	  to	  describe	  objects	  or	  entities	  using	  IFC4	  
All	  objects	  related	  to	  construction	  or	  facility	  management	  of	  buildings	  are	  
normally	  placed	  as	  IFC	  Entities.	  Currently,	  the	  IFC4	  database	  covers	  766	  
entities	  (buildingSMART, n.d.).	  All	  IFC	  entities	  can	  then	  be	  described	  using	  
properties	  from	  the	  previous	  five	  groups,	  among	  others,	  as	  seen	  on	  Figure	  
==PAGE:76==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  53 
23.	  It	  should	  be	  aimed	  to	  keep	  information	  description	  simple	  and	  only	  use	  
from	  these	  five	  categories	  –	  preferably	  information	  contained	  in	  the	  
property	  sets.	  	  	  
	  
Property	  Sets	  
Property	  Sets	  are	  information	  assigned	  to	  an	  IFC	  Entity,	  either	  as	  standard	  
properties	  or	  entity-­‐specific.	  Property	  Sets	  cannot	  stand	  by	  itself	  and	  has	  to	  
be	  assigned	  to	  an	  object.	  Property	  Sets	  begins	  with	  the	  prefix	  ‘Pset’.	  
Property	  Sets	  are,	  as	  the	  name	  implies,	  ‘sets’	  of	  properties	  –	  one	  can	  choose	  
to	  assign	  all	  properties	  to	  the	  object	  or	  choose	  only	  the	  relevant	  ones.	  	  
	  
Quantity	  Sets	  
Quantity	  Sets	  are	  assigned	  to	  objects	  to	  define	  measurable	  physical	  
properties	  such	  as	  length,	  area,	  volume,	  weight	  etc.	  	  
	  
Enumeration	  Type	  
Enumeration	  Type	  is	  a	  set	  of	  predefined	  ‘Enumeration	  Values’	  that	  can	  be	  
selected	  and	  assigned	  to	  a	  specific	  object.	  Only	  one	  enumeration	  value	  can	  
be	  selected	  and	  these	  values	  are	  either	  numeric	  or	  descriptive.	  An	  example	  
could	  be	  that	  for	  IfcDoorType	  the	  enumeration	  values	  are:	  Door,	  Gate,	  
Trapdoor,	  Userdefined	  or	  Notdefined.	  Hence	  one	  of	  these	  values	  can	  be	  
assigned	  to	  that	  specific	  type	  of	  door	  telling	  what	  kind	  of	  door	  this	  is	  on	  a	  
very	  overall	  level.	  All	  Enumeration	  Types	  ends	  with	  ‘–Enum’.	  
	  
Material	  Constituents	  
Material	  Constituents	  provides	  the	  possibility	  to	  assign	  materials	  to	  an	  
object	  on	  all	  levels	  of	  detailing.	  Material	  Constituents	  is	  only	  the	  ‘parent’	  
material	  assigner,	  as	  the	  material	  itself	  is	  defined	  as	  IfcMaterial	  with	  
associated	  properties	  etc.	  	  
	  
Object	  Placement	  
Object	  Placement	  tells	  something	  about	  the	  positioning	  of	  the	  object	  in	  a	  
coordinate	  system.	  It	  needs	  to	  be	  defined	  whether	  the	  placement	  is	  in	  
relation	  to	  a	  world/absolute	  coordinate	  system;	  a	  relative	  placement	  to	  
another	  product;	  or	  as	  a	  placement	  on	  a	  referenced	  grid.	  	  
IRM and ORM template 
The	  creation	  of	  IRM	  and	  ORM	  follow	  a	  template	  to	  ensure	  the	  same	  layout	  
and	  order	  of	  information	  provided.	  Each	  document	  should	  be	  filled	  in	  like	  
the	  seen	  in	  Table	  4.	  This	  template	  has	  been	  provided	  as	  framework	  by	  the	  
Ph.D.	  project	  this	  study	  is	  participating	  in,	  to	  ensure	  consistency	  in	  all	  IDM	  
packages	  developed.

Lighting information breakdown 
This	  study	  is	  about	  lighting	  design	  processes	  why	  a	  consistent	  method	  to	  
describe	  lighting	  fixtures	  or	  lighting	  systems	  is	  necessary.	  In	  order	  to	  share	  
information	  properly	  the	  information	  about	  a	  lighting	  system	  can	  be	  broken	  
down	  into	  four	  categories	  using	  WBS:	  
	  
• Space	  lighting	  
• Lighting	  system	  
• Lighting	  fixture	  
• Light	  source	  
	  
Space	  lighting	  
Space	  lighting	  holds	  all	  relevant	  information	  about	  lighting	  within	  a	  space.	  
This	  is	  where	  information	  on	  space-­‐level	  is	  contained,	  such	  as	  the	  
illumination	  levels	  or	  requirements	  for	  lighting	  conditions.	  	  
	  
Lighting	  system	  
All	  information	  related	  to	  a	  lighting	  system	  is	  categorised	  here.	  A	  lighting	  
system	  consists	  of	  several	  light	  fixtures	  to	  obtain	  the	  lighting	  levels	  defined	  
in	  space	  lighting.	  Information	  on	  system-­‐level	  could	  be	  power	  consumption,	  
amount	  of	  fixtures,	  and	  placement	  of	  cable	  routing	  to	  service	  light	  fixtures.	  	  
	  
Lighting	  fixture	  
All	  fixture-­‐specific	  information	  is	  provided	  in	  this	  category.	  Fixture-­‐specific	  
information	  could	  be	  geometric	  shape,	  electrical	  properties,	  design	  and	  
material.	  Also	  optic	  properties,	  such	  as	  what	  kind	  of	  reflector	  or	  shading	  is	  
used	  to	  distribute/shade	  the	  light	  emitted	  from	  a	  light	  source	  properly	  is	  
included	  in	  this	  category.	  Properties	  should	  be	  assigned	  to	  each	  type	  of	  
fixture	  used	  in	  the	  project.	  	  
	  
Light	  source	  
Light	  source	  contains	  all	  information	  related	  to	  the	  light	  output	  from	  a	  
fixture.	  A	  light	  fixture	  is	  the	  ‘container’	  of	  a	  light	  source;	  hence	  it	  is	  the	  light	  
source	  that	  delivers	  the	  preferred	  light	  in	  a	  space.	  All	  photometric	  data	  
about	  the	  light	  source	  such	  as	  light	  output,	  colour,	  intensity	  etc.	  is	  included	  
here.	  	  
==PAGE:78==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  55 
4.2.3. REFERENCING 
The	  provided	  information	  about	  lighting	  in	  the	  exchange	  requirements	  has	  
been	  collected	  through	  the	  industry	  expert	  surveys	  and	  accumulated	  to	  a	  
list	  of	  relevant	  parameters	  used	  for	  lighting	  design.	  Every	  industry	  expert	  
has	  his	  own	  sets	  of	  relevant	  information	  to	  provide	  or	  obtain,	  which	  the	  
authors	  of	  this	  project	  has	  evaluated	  to	  give	  their	  own	  version	  of	  relevant	  
parameters.	  	  
	  
Every	  time	  a	  unique	  statement	  is	  found	  during	  the	  IDM	  packages,	  they	  will	  
be	  followed	  by	  a	  footnote	  stating	  where	  this	  information	  is	  obtained.	  
Footnotes	  are	  primarily	  placed	  if	  a	  statement	  is	  unique	  for	  one	  of	  the	  
industry	  experts	  or	  if	  there	  is	  an	  unclear	  source	  of	  origin	  behind	  the	  
statement.	  	  
	  
The	  footnotes	  will	  either	  reference	  to	  industry	  survey	  participants,	  to	  peer	  
review	  participants	  or	  to	  the	  author’s	  interpretation	  of	  the	  literature	  
review.	  If	  no	  footnote	  is	  present	  it	  will	  usually	  mean,	  that	  the	  statement	  is	  
so	  general	  for	  all	  industry	  experts	  or	  the	  literature	  review,	  that	  a	  single	  
reference	  is	  not	  possible.	  	  
	  
Participants	  peer	  reviewing	  this	  study	  includes	  Jørgen	  Kjer	  (COWI)	  and	  Lisa	  
Trapp,	  who	  is	  also	  a	  lighting	  designer	  at	  COWI	  and	  educated	  Cand.design.	  	  
	  
==PAGE:79==

4.3. IDM PACKAGE E 33 – 21 99 28 11 ARCHITECTURAL 
LIGHTING DESIGN 
4.3.1. INTRODUCTION 
This	  section	  presents	  the	  IDM	  Package	  E	  33–21	  99	  28	  11:	  Architectural	  
Lighting	  Design.	  The	  letter	  ‘E’	  represents	  the	  ISO	  29481-­‐1	  (ISO,	  2010)	  life	  
cycle	  stage	  ‘Outline	  Conceptual	  Design	  and	  the	  number	  is	  the	  OmniClass	  
(OmniClass,	  2012)	  discipline:	  
	  
Design	  Disciplines	  à	  Specialty	  Design	  à	  Lighting	  Design	  à	  	  
Architectural	  Lighting	  Design	  
	  
The	  IDM	  is	  developed	  based	  on	  information	  gathered	  throughout	  the	  
project	  and	  displayed	  using	  the	  method	  defined	  earlier	  in	  this	  chapter.	  The	  
IDM	  package	  is	  developed	  based	  on	  buildingSMART	  templates	  
(buildingSMART,	  2013).	  This	  chapter	  is	  about	  describing	  the	  IDM	  E	  33–21	  
99	  28	  11	  package	  with	  in-­‐depth	  explanations	  about	  all	  choices	  made	  to	  
complete	  the	  package.	  	  
4.3.2. USE CASE 
Architectural	  lighting	  design	  activities	  are	  typically	  activated	  in	  the	  outline	  
conceptual	  design	  stage	  of	  AEC	  projects	  when	  defining	  lighting	  conditions	  
in	  or	  around	  buildings.	  This	  IDM	  package	  E	  33	  –	  21	  99	  28	  11	  describes	  the	  
work	  performed	  by	  a	  Lighting	  Designer,	  hence	  a	  specialty	  engineer	  or	  
architect	  that	  design	  and	  plan	  artificial	  lighting	  in	  and	  around	  buildings.	  	  
	  
A	  lighting	  analysis	  is	  an	  important	  process	  when	  designing	  lighting	  
conditions	  to	  ensure	  that	  required	  lighting	  levels	  are	  obtained.	  These	  
requirements	  can	  be	  specified	  by	  either	  the	  building	  owner,	  architect	  or	  
through	  national	  and	  international	  standards.	  It	  is	  prerequisite	  for	  this	  IDM	  
package,	  that	  a	  preliminary	  lighting	  concept	  has	  been	  developed	  earlier	  in	  
another	  life	  cycle	  stage.	  This	  concept	  should	  indicate	  how	  the	  lighting	  is	  
preferably	  distributed	  through	  space;	  the	  illumination	  levels	  required;	  
qualitative	  properties;	  and	  what	  kind	  of	  light	  source	  is	  preferred,	  as	  well	  as	  
how	  it	  is	  controlled.	  In	  this	  IDM	  package	  it	  is	  the	  lighting	  designer’s	  main	  
objective	  to	  concretise	  the	  lighting	  concept	  through	  selecting	  and	  placing	  
light	  fixtures	  in	  accordance	  with	  the	  lighting	  concept	  and	  finally	  make	  
simulations	  to	  check	  if	  illumination	  levels	  are	  sufficient.	  	  
	  
The	  main	  outcome	  from	  this	  process	  is	  a	  list	  of	  the	  light	  fixtures	  including	  
relevant	  properties,	  and	  where	  they	  are	  placed	  on	  a	  reflected	  ceiling	  plan.	  
Together	  with	  charts	  showing	  the	  illumination	  levels	  provided	  by	  these	  
fixtures,	  this	  provides	  foundation	  to	  calculate	  a	  preliminary	  energy	  
consumption	  used	  for	  lighting,	  which	  needs	  to	  be	  checked	  by	  an	  electrical	  
engineer.	  The	  work	  resulting	  from	  the	  lighting	  design	  process	  is	  therefore	  
key	  information	  to	  proceed	  planning	  the	  reflected	  ceiling	  and	  coordination	  
with	  other	  engineering	  disciplines	  in	  later	  life	  cycle	  phases.	  A	  summary	  of	  
relevant	  input	  and	  outputs	  to	  and	  from	  this	  IDM	  package	  is	  displayed	  on	  
Table	  5.	  
==PAGE:80==

Goal and scope 
Due	  to	  lack	  of	  standardisation	  within	  information	  exchanges	  the	  necessary	  
information	  to	  initiate	  the	  preliminary	  lighting	  analysis	  process	  might	  not	  
always	  be	  available.	  In	  order	  to	  secure	  the	  work	  of	  lighting	  designers	  all	  
information	  to	  and	  from	  this	  process	  needs	  to	  be	  streamlined	  and	  
standardised.	  This	  IDM	  package	  provides	  a	  tool	  to	  standardise	  the	  structure	  
of	  lighting	  designers’	  work	  and	  the	  required	  information	  exchange	  and	  
where	  to	  get	  information	  from	  or	  deliver	  information	  to.	  	  
	  
The	  goal	  and	  scope	  of	  this	  IDM	  package	  is	  to	  deliver	  a	  standardised	  process	  
framework	  to	  lighting	  designers	  who	  need	  to	  select	  and	  place	  light	  fixtures	  
on	  a	  reflected	  ceiling	  plan	  and	  determine	  the	  lighting	  conditions	  within	  a	  
space.	  	  
Actors 
Actors	  involved	  in	  this	  IDM	  package	  include	  the	  lighting	  designer,	  building	  
owner,	  architect,	  electrical	  engineer	  and	  product	  manufacturer.	  	  
	  
Lighting	  Designer	  (LD)	  
Lighting	  designers	  perform	  the	  majority	  of	  activities	  described	  in	  this	  IDM	  
package.	  They	  perform	  activities	  based	  on	  information	  received	  from	  the	  
other	  actors	  and	  ends	  up	  with	  a	  specific	  list	  of	  required	  outputs.	  How	  
lighting	  designers	  perform	  each	  activity	  is	  not	  relevant	  for	  this	  IDM	  
package;	  but	  sequence	  and	  information	  exchange	  should	  be	  followed.	  
	  	  
A	  major	  input	  required	  for	  a	  lighting	  designer	  to	  perform	  the	  preliminary	  
lighting	  analysis	  is	  the	  lighting	  concept.	  This	  is	  an	  abstract	  definition	  of	  
different	  factors	  that	  indicates	  how	  the	  lighting	  is	  designed	  and	  planned	  in	  
the	  building.	  Normally	  the	  lighting	  designer	  would	  in	  most	  cases	  have	  
developed	  this	  concept	  in	  a	  prior	  process	  to	  this	  IDM	  package.	  Sometimes,	  
however,	  one	  lighting	  designer	  may	  perform	  the	  lighting	  analysis	  based	  on	  
another	  lighting	  designer’s	  concept.	  It	  should	  therefore	  be	  stated	  clear	  what	  
a	  lighting	  concept	  specification	  has	  to	  include.	  	  
	  
Building	  Owner	  (BO)	  
The	  building	  owner	  determines	  relevant	  requirements	  for	  the	  lighting	  
conditions	  in	  the	  building	  in	  terms	  of	  illumination	  levels	  –	  often	  inspired	  by	  
national	  standards.	  A	  building	  owner	  is	  responsible	  for	  delivering	  a	  
==PAGE:81==
E 33 – 21 99 28 11 ARCHITECTURAL LIGHTING DESIGN // 4	  
	  
58
required	  energy	  frame	  for	  the	  building	  in	  total,	  which	  will	  be	  translated	  into	  
an	  energy	  frame	  for	  lighting	  later	  on.	  
	  
Architect	  (ARCH)	  
The	  architect	  is	  providing	  all	  geometric	  and	  material-­‐related	  details	  about	  
the	  building	  necessary	  to	  perform	  the	  lighting	  analysis.	  Usually	  the	  
architectural	  detailing	  is	  not	  complete	  at	  this	  life	  cycle	  stage,	  why	  clear	  
information	  from	  the	  architect	  is	  sometimes	  difficult	  to	  obtain	  –	  and	  if	  
obtained	  these	  might	  change	  repeatedly	  throughout	  the	  project.	  	  
	  
Electrical	  Engineer	  (EE)	  
Electrical	  engineers	  support	  the	  lighting	  designer	  with	  functional	  solutions	  
to	  make	  the	  lighting	  design	  work	  in	  the	  project.	  Most	  of	  the	  electrical	  
engineer’s	  involvement	  in	  this	  package	  is	  that	  he	  is	  the	  main	  receiver	  of	  
final	  results	  so	  he	  can	  proceed	  with	  his	  own	  work.	  But	  an	  electrical	  engineer	  
is	  usually	  also	  the	  person	  who	  defines	  the	  energy	  frame	  allocated	  for	  
lighting	  based	  on	  the	  building’s	  overall	  energy	  frame.	  
	  
Product	  Manufacturer	  (PRM)	  
A	  product	  manufacturer	  delivers	  the	  technical	  solutions	  to	  support	  a	  given	  
lighting	  design.	  In	  this	  particular	  package,	  a	  product	  manufacturer	  will	  be	  
responsible	  for	  delivering	  information	  about	  lighting	  fixtures	  used	  to	  
simulate	  lighting	  conditions.	  Without	  these	  data	  a	  simulation	  cannot	  be	  
carried	  out.	  Whether	  the	  product	  manufacturer	  is	  actively	  participating	  in	  
the	  project	  and	  delivers	  information,	  or	  if	  this	  information	  is	  accessible	  
elsewhere	  (i.e.	  on	  a	  website	  or	  datasheet)	  depends	  on	  the	  specific	  project.	  If	  
standard	  solutions	  were	  chosen	  then	  the	  last	  possibility	  would	  be	  most	  
likely.	  	  
4.3.3. PROCESS MAP 
This	  process	  map	  describing	  the	  preliminary	  lighting	  analysis	  in	  the	  outline	  
conceptual	  design	  stage	  is	  illustrated	  on	  Figure	  24.	  It	  is	  also	  available	  in	  
Appendix	  D	  in	  a	  higher	  resolution.	  	  
	  
Tables	  describing	  activities	  in	  the	  process	  map	  section	  will	  have	  blue	  
headers.	  	  
	  
Tables	  describing	  the	  ERMs	  in	  subsection	  4.3.4	  will	  have	  grey	  headers.	  	  
	  
==PAGE:82==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  59 
	  
Figure	  24	  -­‐	  IDM	  E	  33	  -­‐	  21	  99	  28	  11:	  Architectural	  Lighting	  Design.	  A	  large	  version	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  D.
