IDENTIFY LIGHTING CRITERIA 
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD	  
Documentation	   Identifying	  lighting	  criteria	  is	  based	  on	  the	  preliminary	  lighting	  
concept	  usually	  developed	  by	  a	  lighting	  designer	  in	  an	  earlier	  
AEC	  life	  cycle	  stage	  –	  typically	  the	  brief	  phase.	  This	  concept	  
usually	  defines	  the	  lighting	  design	  in	  a	  building	  on	  a	  more	  
general	  level.	  Qualitative	  properties	  the	  lighting	  has	  to	  provide;	  
what	  architectural	  features	  the	  lighting	  has	  to	  highlight;	  where	  
the	  lighting	  should	  come	  from	  (from	  above,	  from	  the	  ground,	  
from	  the	  walls,	  etc.);	  what	  light	  fixture	  type	  should	  be	  used,	  
would	  be	  some	  of	  the	  factors	  described	  in	  a	  preliminary	  
lighting	  concept1.	  As	  a	  general	  rule	  of	  thumb,	  a	  preliminary	  
lighting	  concept	  should	  only	  state	  requirements	  for	  a	  lighting	  
system	  and	  not	  define	  solutions	  for	  this.	  That	  is	  what	  this	  
process	  is	  all	  about.	  Additional	  requirements	  could	  also	  be	  
energy	  consumption	  and	  minimum	  illumination	  levels	  on	  task	  
or	  floor	  surfaces,	  and	  available	  budget	  reserved	  for	  the	  lighting	  
solution.	  
ERs

CREATE SIMULATION GEOMETRY 
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD	  
Documentation	   In	  order	  to	  specify	  solutions	  for	  a	  lighting	  design	  to	  work	  in	  
compliance	  with	  the	  concept	  requirements,	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  needs	  to	  simulate	  a	  number	  of	  design	  solutions.	  A	  
lighting	  simulation	  is	  performed	  in	  a	  computer	  program	  that	  
calculates	  the	  light	  distribution	  from	  lighting	  fixtures	  inserted	  
in	  a	  3D	  geometry.	  This	  3D	  geometry	  usually	  represents	  a	  
building,	  a	  room	  or	  an	  outdoor	  scene	  that	  needs	  to	  be	  
illuminated	  with	  artificial	  lighting.	  Before	  beginning	  the	  actual	  
simulation	  some	  steps	  to	  produce	  the	  calculation	  basis	  has	  to	  
be	  performed.	  The	  first	  step	  is	  to	  create	  the	  3D	  geometry	  in	  
which	  lighting	  levels	  have	  to	  be	  calculated.	  This	  is	  done	  either	  
directly	  in	  the	  light	  calculation	  software	  based	  on	  a	  2D	  CAD	  
drawing	  or	  by	  importing	  a	  3D	  geometry.	  	  
When	  the	  geometry	  is	  built	  up,	  all	  surfaces	  within	  the	  model	  
need	  to	  have	  surface	  material	  reflectance	  defined.	  This	  is	  
necessary	  in	  order	  to	  simulate	  the	  light	  distribution	  accurately.	  
Surface	  reflectance	  is	  determined	  based	  on	  the	  material	  
provided	  from	  the	  architect	  and	  the	  lighting	  designer’s	  
experience	  telling	  what	  the	  usual	  reflectance	  for	  a	  material	  of	  
this	  type	  would	  be2.	  
ERs	  
IRM	  1.2	  
Add	  1.2	  
	  
[1.3]	  
SELECT INITIAL LIGHT FIXTURES	  
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD	  
Documentation	   Selecting	  which	  light	  fixtures	  to	  use	  is	  based	  on	  the	  
requirements	  in	  the	  lighting	  concept,	  the	  individual	  lighting	  
designer’s	  experience	  and	  a	  lot	  of	  iterations.	  A	  lighting	  designer	  
will	  also	  normally	  have	  a	  budget	  to	  clinch	  to,	  which	  can	  limit	  
the	  selection	  of	  potential	  light	  fixtures	  available3.	  	  
Once	  fixtures	  are	  selected	  it	  is	  important	  to	  ask	  for	  product	  
manufacturer	  information,	  both	  light	  fixture	  properties	  and	  
photometric	  files	  for	  simulation	  purpose.	  This	  photometric	  
data	  will	  be	  the	  basis	  for	  the	  lighting	  simulation	  and	  therefore	  
the	  simulation	  depends	  on	  the	  product	  manufacturer	  to	  deliver	  
sufficient	  information	  about	  their	  product	  in	  order	  to	  use	  them	  
in	  the	  calculation.	  
ERs

PLACE FIXTURES IN SIMULATION GEOMETRY	  
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD	  
Documentation	   After	  selecting	  which	  products	  to	  use	  they	  has	  to	  be	  placed	  
directly	  in	  the	  simulation	  model.	  Light	  fixture	  objects	  can	  be	  
placed	  directly	  in	  the	  3D	  geometry	  modelled	  in	  light	  calculation	  
software.	  These	  objects	  represent	  actual	  light	  fixture	  products	  
available	  on	  the	  market.	  Sometimes	  the	  light	  fixture	  objects	  
represent	  actual	  dimensions	  of	  the	  fixtures	  making	  placement	  
of	  them	  easier.	  Other	  times	  they	  are	  only	  representative	  boxes.	  
The	  dimensional	  detailing	  is	  not	  necessary	  at	  this	  stage.

[1.5]	  
PERFORM LIGHTING SIMULATION	  
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD	  
Documentation	   A	  lighting	  simulation	  will	  be	  carried	  out	  and	  calculates	  light	  
distribution	  based	  on	  photometric	  data	  and	  the	  surfaces	  in	  the	  
model.	  When	  the	  lighting	  simulation	  is	  finished	  it	  should	  show	  
illuminance	  (lux)	  levels	  in	  the	  3D	  geometry	  or	  a	  user-­‐defined	  
calculation	  surface.	  	  
ERs	  
N/A	  
	  
GATEWAY	  
IS LIGHTING CONDITIONS ADEQUATE?	  
Type	  	  
Gateway	  
Actor	  
LD	  
Documentation	   The	  exclusive	  gateway	  asking	  the	  question:	  “Is	  lighting	  
conditions	  adequate?”	  need	  to	  be	  assessed	  by	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  after	  each	  simulation.	  The	  lighting	  designer	  has	  to	  
look	  at	  the	  results	  to	  determine	  if	  lighting	  conditions	  follows	  
the	  requirements	  stated	  in	  the	  lighting	  concept.	  If	  lighting	  
conditions	  are	  adequate	  and	  everything	  else	  is	  acceptable	  
(such	  as	  selection,	  placement	  and	  budget	  of	  fixtures)	  the	  
lighting	  designer	  can	  proceed	  to	  communicate	  the	  results.	  If	  
not	  the	  lighting	  designer	  has	  to	  go	  back	  and	  re-­‐evaluate	  the	  
fixture	  selection.	  	  
ERs	  
N/A

[IRM 1.1]	  
LIGHTING CONCEPT	  
Type	  	  
Data	  object	  
Actor	  
LD/ARCH/BO/EE	  
Documentation	  
A	  lighting	  concept	  defines	  requirements	  for	  the	  lighting	  design	  
in	  a	  building,	  stating	  all	  relevant	  qualitative	  and	  quantitative	  
values	  the	  lighting	  has	  to	  fulfil.	  As	  well	  as	  data	  a	  lighting	  
concept	  often	  includes	  visualisations	  and	  drawings	  that	  
illustrates	  the	  thoughts	  about	  the	  final	  lighting	  design,	  which	  
can	  be	  helpful	  in	  later	  processes.

LIGHT SOURCE	  
Actor	  
LD	  
Lamp	  Type

Dimming	  
signal

SURFACE REFLECTANCE	  
Type	  	  
Additional	  information	  
Actor	  
ARCH/LD	  
Documentation	  
	  
From	  surface	  materials	  provided	  by	  the	  architect	  a	  lighting	  
designer	  has	  to	  convert	  this	  into	  reflectance	  properties	  in	  the	  
lighting	  software.	  Usually	  materials	  would	  have	  commonly	  
used	  reflectances	  and	  the	  lighting	  designer	  needs	  to	  use	  
his/her	  experience	  to	  verify	  these	  values.	  Default	  values	  in	  
national	  standards	  could	  also	  be	  useful.	  However,	  this	  is	  one	  
point	  where	  there	  are	  no	  clear	  guidelines	  on	  how	  to	  estimate	  
reflectances	  for	  preliminary	  lighting	  simulations10.

DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  (Danish Standards, 2011)

EA?	  –	  Light	  emitting	  component

4.4. IDM PACKAGE G 33 – 21 99 28 11 ARCHITECTURAL 
LIGHTING DESIGN 
4.4.1. INTRODUCTION 
This	  section	  presents	  the	  IDM	  Package	  G	  33–21	  99	  28	  11:	  Architectural	  
Lighting	  Design	  situated	  in	  the	  ISO	  29481-­‐1	  (ISO,	  2010)	  life	  cycle	  stage	  
‘Outline	  Conceptual	  Design’	  and	  the	  number	  is	  the	  OmniClass	  (OmniClass,	  
2012)	  discipline:	  
	  
Design	  Disciplines	  à	  Specialty	  Design	  à	  Lighting	  Design	  à	  	  
Architectural	  Lighting	  Design	  
	  
The	  IDM	  is	  developed	  based	  on	  information	  gathered	  throughout	  the	  
project	  and	  displayed	  using	  the	  method	  defined	  earlier	  in	  this	  chapter.	  The	  
IDM	  package	  is	  developed	  based	  on	  buildingSMART	  templates	  
(buildingSMART,	  2013).	  This	  chapter	  is	  about	  describing	  the	  IDM	  G	  33–21	  
99	  28	  11	  package	  with	  in-­‐depth	  explanations	  about	  all	  choices	  made	  to	  
complete	  the	  package.	  	  
4.4.2. USE CASE 
During	  the	  previous	  life	  cycle	  stage	  (outline	  conceptual	  design)	  the	  lighting	  
design	  were	  specified	  on	  a	  preliminary	  scale,	  based	  on	  a	  lighting	  concept.	  	  
This	  specification	  resulted	  in	  a	  selection	  and	  placement	  of	  fixtures,	  that	  both	  
suited	  illuminance	  and	  energy	  requirements.	  It	  is	  that	  preliminary	  selection	  
of	  fixtures	  that	  this	  process	  is	  aiming	  to	  detail	  even	  further	  with	  additional	  
requirements	  from	  other	  actors.	  The	  process	  G	  33-­‐21	  99	  28	  11	  
Architectural	  Lighting	  Design	  describes	  the	  work	  primarily	  done	  by	  a	  
lighting	  designer	  but	  not	  exclusively.	  This	  depends	  on	  the	  company	  
structure	  and	  resources	  available	  in	  a	  project.	  	  
	  
Detailing	  the	  lighting	  design	  means	  to	  turn	  a	  design	  proposal	  into	  a	  concrete	  
solution	  to	  be	  functional	  in	  a	  building.	  As	  so,	  this	  process	  calls	  for	  much	  
closer	  cooperation	  between	  a	  lighting	  designer	  and	  electrical	  engineer	  to	  
specify	  requirements	  for	  both	  electrical	  controlling	  and	  ceiling	  layout	  
coordination.	  	  
	  
Lighting	  control	  is	  a	  large	  part	  of	  the	  finalised	  lighting	  design.	  Lighting	  is	  not	  
fixed	  at	  a	  constant	  level	  and	  should	  be	  adapted	  to	  the	  conditions	  –	  either	  by	  
adapting	  itself	  to	  natural	  light	  available	  or	  controlled	  by	  presence	  of	  people	  
in	  the	  space.	  Especially	  in	  our	  times	  greater	  focus	  on	  energy	  optimisation,	  
lighting	  controlling	  is	  mandatory	  to	  limit	  excess	  energy	  consumption.	  But	  
since	  light	  controlling	  changes	  the	  appearance	  and	  amount	  of	  light,	  this	  has	  
to	  be	  thought	  into	  the	  design.	  The	  lighting	  designer’s	  job	  is	  therefore	  to	  
optimise	  the	  lighting	  system	  to	  function	  under	  both	  normal	  and	  reduced	  
conditions12.	  	  
The	  main	  outcome	  from	  this	  process	  is	  a	  definition	  of	  the	  controlling	  
concept	  made	  in	  close	  collaboration	  between	  the	  lighting	  designer	  and	  an	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
12	  Survey:	  Martin	  Sømberg,	  Ramboll	  
==PAGE:93==
G 33 – 21 99 28 11 ARCHITECTURAL LIGHTING DESIGN // 4	  
	  
70
electrical	  engineer.	  To	  do	  so,	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  evaluate	  the	  building	  needs,	  
type	  of	  occupants	  in	  the	  building,	  and	  the	  energy	  requirements.	  Preliminary	  
control	  concept	  has	  usually	  been	  developed	  in	  the	  lighting	  concept	  and	  it	  is	  
now	  up	  to	  the	  electrical	  engineer	  and	  lighting	  designer	  to	  specify	  it	  further.	  
Then	  the	  next	  major	  outcome	  is	  a	  ceiling	  coordination	  with	  other	  
engineering	  disciplines,	  where	  the	  final	  placement	  of	  fixtures	  is	  determined	  
and	  placed	  in	  an	  architectural	  model.	  All	  in	  all	  this	  process	  should	  end	  up	  
with	  detailed	  specifications	  of	  requirements	  for	  a	  light	  fixture	  used	  for	  
tendering	  documents	  since	  no	  manufacturer	  name	  can	  be	  specified	  in	  the	  
tendering	  stage.	  A	  summary	  of	  the	  input	  and	  outputs	  to/from	  IDM	  package	  
G	  33-­‐21	  99	  28	  11	  is	  visible	  below:	  
	  
Table	  6	  –	  Summarised	  input	  and	  output	  from	  IDM	  package	  G	  33-­‐21	  99	  28	  11

Goal and scope 
This	  IDM	  package	  aims	  to	  provide	  a	  common	  framework	  for	  a	  lighting	  
design	  process	  in	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage.	  It	  provides	  working	  process	  
standardisation	  to	  ensure	  that	  everyone	  is	  aware	  of	  the	  working	  process	  
and	  how	  to	  optimise	  it.	  As	  well	  as	  information	  exchange	  standardisation,	  
defining	  what	  kind	  of	  information	  is	  needed	  at	  what	  time.	  This	  information	  
standardisation	  can	  eventually	  be	  utilised	  by	  product	  manufacturers	  and	  
lighting	  designers	  to	  work	  towards	  a	  commonly	  agreed	  framework	  that	  
potentially	  could	  increase	  productivity	  and	  maintain	  consistency	  in	  digital	  
construction.	  
Actors 
Actors	  involved	  in	  this	  IDM	  packed	  include	  the	  lighting	  designer,	  electrical	  
engineer,	  building	  owner,	  architect,	  daylight	  engineer	  and	  product	  
manufacturer.	  	  
	  
Lighting	  Designer	  (LD)	  
Lighting	  designers	  are	  the	  primary	  actor	  behind	  this	  process	  but	  in	  large	  
cooperation	  with	  electrical	  engineers	  throughout	  almost	  all	  activities.	  
Lighting	  designers	  develops	  the	  control	  concept	  and	  re-­‐evaluates	  fixture	  
selection	  input	  from	  the	  previous	  lighting	  design	  process.	  When	  changes	  in	  
placement	  or	  fixtures	  happen,	  either	  due	  to	  control	  demands	  or	  clashes	  in	  
==PAGE:94==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  71 
the	  ceiling,	  the	  lighting	  designer	  performs	  a	  validating	  lighting	  simulation	  to	  
ensure	  lighting	  conditions	  are	  adequate.	  Lighting	  designers	  develop	  a	  light	  
fixture	  requirement	  list	  in	  the	  end	  of	  this	  process	  to	  be	  used	  as	  a	  basis	  for	  
tendering	  documents.	  	  	  	  
	  
Electrical	  Engineer	  (EE)	  
Electrical	  engineers	  support	  and	  cooperate	  with	  lighting	  designers	  
throughout	  the	  process.	  They	  support	  with	  technical	  knowledge	  about	  
control	  systems	  and	  electrical	  routing	  within	  the	  building.	  The	  main	  activity	  
for	  electrical	  engineers	  is,	  however,	  to	  coordinate	  the	  ceiling	  with	  other	  
engineering	  disciplines.	  The	  electrical	  engineer’s	  responsibility	  is	  to	  gather	  
information	  about	  all	  components	  installed	  on	  the	  surface	  and	  ensure	  their	  
interest	  in	  electrical	  devices	  –	  light	  fixtures	  among	  these.	  If	  light	  fixtures	  
need	  to	  be	  moved	  the	  electrical	  engineer	  has	  to	  coordinate	  with	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  to	  re-­‐evaluate	  placing	  and	  new	  lighting	  conditions.	  Electrical	  
engineers	  are	  also	  involved	  in	  defining	  the	  light	  fixture’s	  electrical	  
requirements	  on	  tendering	  documents.	  	  
	  
Building	  Owner	  (BO)	  
A	  building	  owner	  will	  usually	  provide	  information	  about	  space	  function	  and	  
expected	  user’s	  patterns.	  This	  is	  relevant	  for	  a	  lighting	  designer	  to	  know	  
when	  assigning	  control	  patterns	  in	  different	  spaces.	  	  
	  
Architect	  (ARCH)	  
The	  architect	  is	  providing	  all	  geometric	  information	  about	  the	  spaces	  either	  
in	  form	  of	  3D	  models	  or	  2D	  drawings.	  It	  is	  expected	  that	  detailing	  level	  of	  
the	  architecture	  is	  higher	  in	  this	  IDM	  package	  than	  in	  the	  previous	  life	  cycle	  
stage.	  Therefore	  precise	  dimensional	  information	  from	  the	  architect	  is	  
relevant	  when	  coordinating	  the	  reflected	  ceilings.	  	  
	  
Daylight	  Engineer	  (DE)	  
A	  daylight	  engineer	  is	  responsible	  for	  delivering	  information	  about	  natural	  
light	  available	  in	  the	  building	  used	  by	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  define	  control	  
concepts.	  Usually	  simple	  daylight	  factor	  analysis	  will	  be	  sufficient	  for	  this.	  	  
	  
Product	  Manufacturer	  (PRM)	  
Product	  manufacturers	  are	  only	  present	  in	  this	  process	  as	  being	  responsible	  
for	  delivering	  technical	  data	  about	  their	  products,	  in	  case	  the	  lighting	  
system	  has	  to	  be	  changed	  from	  the	  previous	  stage.	  It	  is	  the	  product	  
manufacturer’s	  responsibility	  to	  provide	  sufficient	  information	  to	  fill	  out	  
the	  requirement	  list	  specified	  later.	  	  	  
4.4.3. PROCESS MAP 
The	  process	  map	  describing	  the	  lighting	  design	  in	  the	  coordinated	  design	  
stage	  is	  illustrated	  on	  	  Figure	  25.	  A	  higher	  resolution	  is	  available	  in	  
Appendix	  F.	  	  
Tables	  describing	  activities	  in	  the	  process	  map	  section	  will	  have	  blue	  
headers.	  Tables	  describing	  the	  exchange	  requirements	  in	  subsection	  4.4.4	  
will	  have	  grey	  headers.

	  Figure	  25	  –	  IDM	  G	  33-­‐21	  99	  28	  11:	  Architectural	  Lighting	  Design.	  A	  large	  version	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  F

DEFINE CONTROL ZONES	  
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD/EE	  
Documentation	   Based	  on	  the	  building	  geometry,	  room	  function	  and	  occupancy	  
patterns,	  the	  lighting	  control	  concept	  can	  be	  defined.	  This	  is	  
about	  sketching	  which	  zones	  require	  lighting	  to	  be	  controlled	  
after	  people	  presence	  or	  where	  to	  control	  according	  to	  natural	  
light	  available.	  The	  latter	  requires	  information	  about	  daylight	  
contribution	  in	  the	  space.	  	  
ERs	  
IRM	  1.1:	  Architectural	  space	  
	  
	  
[1.2]	  
DEFINE CONTROLLING CONCEPT	  
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD/EE	  
Documentation	   Defining	  the	  control	  concept	  takes	  foundation	  in	  the	  zones	  
developed	  in	  activity	  1.1.	  Zones	  were	  defined	  from	  space	  
geometry,	  function	  and	  light	  levels,	  and	  in	  this	  activity	  these	  
zones	  are	  translated	  into	  more	  specific	  control	  concepts.	  A	  
control	  concept	  will	  state	  how	  the	  light	  wishes	  to	  be	  
controlled13.	  For	  example,	  in	  an	  office	  environment,	  a	  lighting	  
designer	  might	  want	  the	  light	  to	  be	  controlled	  by	  daylight	  
contribution	  from	  8am	  to	  5	  pm	  and	  from	  5pm	  it	  switches	  to	  
people	  presence.	  Another	  example	  is	  to	  determine	  in	  which	  
spaces	  the	  light	  should	  be	  dimmed	  automatically	  or	  manually,	  
highly	  depending	  on	  the	  function	  and	  practical	  circumstances.	  	  
(Continues	  on	  the	  next	  page)

FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  73 
If	  effect	  lighting	  such	  as	  colour-­‐changing	  lights	  is	  used	  in	  the	  
building,	  these	  lights	  need	  to	  be	  controlled	  by	  another	  system	  
than	  the	  general	  lighting.	  All	  these	  considerations	  have	  to	  be	  
taken	  into	  account	  when	  defining	  the	  control	  concept.	  Usually	  
an	  electrical	  engineer	  adopts	  the	  control	  concept	  and	  specifies	  
the	  technical	  solutions	  to	  support	  it.	  	  
In	  order	  to	  initiate	  this	  activity	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  get	  
information	  about	  the	  lighting	  design	  defined	  in	  the	  previous	  
lighting	  design	  process.	  This	  information	  is	  necessary	  because	  
it	  defines	  what	  to	  control	  and	  where	  in	  the	  building	  the	  fixtures	  
are	  placed.	  	  	  	  
ERs	  
IRM	  1.2	  –	  Preliminary	  lighting	  design	  
	  
	  
[1.3]	  
RE-EVALUATE LIGHT FIXTURE SELECTION AND 
PLACEMENT	  
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD/EE	  
Documentation	   Re-­‐evaluation	  of	  the	  light	  fixtures	  used	  in	  the	  conceptual	  
lighting	  design	  happens	  by	  comparing	  fixture	  properties	  with	  
the	  technical	  demand	  stated	  in	  the	  control	  concept.	  The	  
fixtures	  has	  to	  support	  the	  control	  concept,	  both	  in	  terms	  of	  
placement	  and	  technical	  possibilities,	  otherwise	  the	  fixtures	  
are	  not	  useful.	  An	  example	  could	  be	  if	  the	  light	  in	  an	  office	  
needs	  to	  be	  dimmed	  and	  the	  chosen	  light	  fixture	  is	  not	  
dimmable.	  Re-­‐evaluating	  the	  fixtures	  can	  therefore	  potentially	  
change	  the	  initial	  fixture	  selection	  from	  the	  previous	  process.	  A	  
product	  manufacturer	  will	  be	  able	  to	  help	  with	  additional	  
information	  about	  the	  fixtures	  if	  necessary.	  If	  fixtures	  have	  to	  
be	  changed	  to	  new	  ones,	  this	  means	  gathering	  new	  data	  about	  
the	  fixture	  for	  use	  later	  in	  this	  process.	  	  	  
Re-­‐evaluating	  the	  light	  fixtures	  also	  means	  to	  assess	  physical	  
conditions	  around	  the	  fixtures.	  Physical	  dimensions	  and	  
placement	  of	  fixtures	  should	  be	  provided	  so	  that	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  or	  electrical	  engineer	  can	  evaluate	  if	  there	  is	  enough	  
room	  for	  the	  fixtures	  in	  the	  ceiling	  or	  elsewhere	  placed.	  
Activity	  1.3	  also	  happens	  to	  get	  feedback	  from	  a	  surface	  
coordination	  later	  in	  the	  process,	  through	  the	  form	  of	  a	  
gateway.	  This	  feedback	  tells	  if	  there	  is	  a	  clash	  situation	  on	  the	  
surface,	  e.g.	  a	  ventilation	  duct	  clashing	  with	  a	  light	  fixture,	  or	  
the	  ceiling	  recessing	  height	  being	  lower	  than	  allowed.	  If	  so,	  the	  
lighting	  designer	  or	  electrical	  engineer	  occasionally	  has	  to	  
select	  new	  fixtures	  that	  comply	  with	  both	  the	  new	  physical	  
restrictions	  and	  the	  control	  concept	  requirements.	  This	  
requires	  new	  information	  from	  a	  product	  manufacturer.	  	  
ERs	  
IRM	  1.3	  –	  Manufacturer	  data

[1.4]	  
VERIFY LIGHTING CONDITIONS	  
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD	  
	  
In	  case	  light	  fixtures	  have	  been	  changed	  during	  the	  prior	  
activities	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  perform	  a	  new	  lighting	  simulation.	  
This	  new	  lighting	  simulation	  aim	  to	  verify	  if	  lighting	  
requirements	  are	  still	  met	  and	  the	  energy	  frame	  is	  still	  
complied	  with.	  If	  a	  new	  simulation	  is	  required,	  this	  need	  a	  new	  
simulation	  model	  based	  on	  updated	  geometry	  from	  the	  
architect	  as	  well	  as	  updated	  surface	  materials.	  A	  3D	  simulation	  
model	  is	  built	  up	  in	  simulation	  software,	  surfaces	  are	  assigned,	  
and	  the	  new	  light	  fixtures	  are	  inserted.	  All	  this	  provides	  a	  new	  
simulation	  showing	  the	  illuminance	  levels	  at	  relevant	  surfaces.	  
If	  lighting	  conditions	  are	  satisfactory	  the	  process	  continues.	  If	  
not,	  the	  fixtures	  have	  to	  be	  re-­‐evaluated	  in	  activity	  1.3.

Type

lighting	  designer	  about	  the	  fixture	  placement	  and	  dimensions	  
of	  fixtures.	  This	  is	  where	  the	  electrical	  engineer	  coordinates	  
the	  particular	  placement	  with	  other	  disciplines	  responsible	  for	  
objects	  in	  the	  surface,	  such	  as	  HVAC,	  fire	  safety	  equipment,	  AV	  
equipment	  etc.14	  Lighting	  components	  is	  usually	  the	  least	  
prioritised	  when	  coordinating	  the	  surface,	  why	  it	  is	  quite	  often	  
that	  the	  placement	  has	  to	  be	  re-­‐evaluated15.	  Surfaces	  within	  a	  
space	  can	  include	  the	  reflected	  ceiling,	  walls,	  floors	  etc.

[2.1]	  
EXPORT LIGHT FIXTURES TO ARCH MODEL	  
Type	  	  
Activity	  
Actor	  
LD/ARCH	  
Documentation	   Once	  fixture	  placement	  and	  selection	  is	  satisfactory	  without	  
clashes,	  the	  information	  and	  placement	  of	  fixtures	  can	  be	  
provided	  to	  the	  architect	  who	  inserts	  the	  object	  in	  the	  central	  
architectural	  model.	  Placement	  can	  be	  provided	  either	  on	  a	  2D	  
plan	  or	  section	  or	  as	  3D	  objects	  with	  accurate	  dimensions.	  
Additionally	  to	  the	  placement	  a	  list	  of	  requirements	  to	  the	  
selected	  lighting	  fixture	  has	  to	  follow.	  Since	  the	  coordinated	  
lighting	  design	  eventually	  results	  in	  tendering	  documents,	  no	  
manufacturer-­‐specific	  information	  about	  the	  light	  fixtures	  can	  
be	  provided.	  Therefore	  a	  very	  detailed	  list	  of	  specific	  
requirements	  that	  best	  describes	  the	  light	  fixture,	  its	  
functionality,	  dimensions,	  light	  characteristics	  and	  energy	  
consumption	  has	  to	  be	  developed.	  Later	  on,	  contractors	  will	  try	  
to	  find	  lighting	  fixtures	  that	  match	  these	  requirements	  and	  
make	  a	  bid	  on	  the	  lighting	  contract.	  By	  standardising	  the	  list	  of	  
requirements	  a	  lighting	  designer	  might	  see	  as	  useful	  
information,	  it	  will	  be	  easier	  for	  both	  product	  manufacturers	  
and	  contractors	  to	  supply	  and	  comply	  with	  requirements16.	  	  
Occasionally	  some	  of	  the	  requirements	  will	  not	  be	  met,	  why	  a	  
lighting	  designer	  has	  to	  evaluate	  the	  products	  offered	  whether	  
they	  are	  satisfactory	  or	  not.

controlling	  concept	  has	  to	  know	  how	  the	  space	  is	  used	  in	  order	  
to	  tailor	  the	  control	  systems.	  For	  example,	  lighting	  controls	  
will	  be	  different	  in	  a	  restroom	  than	  a	  technical	  room.

ceiling	  to	  the	  slab,	  hence	  the	  actual	  space	  available	  for	  
installations	  and	  recess	  depth	  available	  for	  recessed	  fixtures.

Additional	  information

Documentation

Type

LD	  
Documentation	   A	  primary	  output	  from	  the	  coordinated	  lighting	  design	  is	  a	  
detailed	  specification	  of	  parameters	  the	  lighting	  system	  has	  to	  
comply	  with	  in	  order	  to	  deliver	  the	  required	  light	  conditions.

lighting	  contract.	  Therefore,	  no	  manufacturer-­‐specific	  
information	  can	  be	  included	  in	  the	  specification	  list.	  The	  
lighting	  requirements	  are	  divided	  into	  space	  lighting,	  lighting	  
system,	  lighting	  fixtures	  and	  light	  source.

fixtures,	  so	  that	  they	  can	  be	  easily	  sorted	  into	  type	  and	  unique	  
identifier.	  Each	  type	  of	  fixture	  should	  have	  assigned	  all	  
specifications	  listed	  below.

Service	  Life	  
Service	  life	  tells	  indicates	  the	  duration	  of	  hours	  the	  light

IDM DEVELOPMENT // 4	  
	  
84
4.5. DISCUSSION 
Naming the IDM packages 
The	  authors	  of	  this	  study	  saw	  it	  necessary	  to	  generate	  a	  new	  OmniClass	  
sub-­‐discipline	  ‘architectural	  lighting	  design’	  and	  chose	  that	  code	  to	  name	  
the	  IDM	  packages.	  The	  ‘E’	  and	  ‘G’	  represents	  that	  the	  packages	  are	  located	  in	  
the	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  and	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  
respectively	  according	  to	  (ISO,	  2010).	  ‘Architectural	  lighting	  design’	  is	  a	  
work-­‐break	  down	  of	  the	  parent	  discipline	  ‘Lighting	  Design’	  that	  has	  the	  
following	  description:	  	  
	  
“Design	  and	  implementation	  of	  both	  architectural	  lighting	  such	  as	  homes,	  
business,	  store,	  displays	  and	  theatrical	  or	  atmospheric	  lighting	  systems”	  
(OmniClass,	  2012,	  p.	  9)	  
	  
This	  definition	  of	  lighting	  design	  is	  quite	  open	  and	  does	  not	  suit	  the	  specific	  
application	  areas	  of	  the	  IDM	  packages.	  When	  creating	  the	  IDM	  packages	  the	  
work	  of	  designing	  light	  in	  and	  around	  buildings	  has	  been	  the	  primary	  focus.	  
This	  means	  it	  is	  not	  possible	  to	  adopt	  the	  processes	  described	  into	  other	  
lighting	  application	  disciplines	  -­‐	  theatrical	  lighting	  for	  instance.	  Therefore	  it	  
was	  necessary	  to	  specify	  exactly	  where	  the	  packages	  are	  applicable.	  By	  
creating	  a	  newer	  sub-­‐discipline	  under	  ‘Lighting	  Design’	  it	  also	  opens	  up	  for	  
the	  possibility	  of	  adding	  other	  specific	  disciplines	  in	  OmniClass,	  such	  as	  
sports	  lighting	  design,	  road	  lighting	  design	  etc.	  	  
	  
The	  processes	  in	  the	  IDM	  packages	  describe	  the	  most	  common	  activities	  
within	  lighting	  design	  in	  AEC	  projects	  at	  each	  specific	  life	  cycle	  stage.	  
Usually	  no	  parallel	  lighting	  design	  processes	  occur	  at	  the	  same	  life	  cycle	  
stage	  why	  there	  is	  no	  need	  for	  breaking	  down	  the	  OmniClass	  discipline	  even	  
further,	  hence	  the	  name	  ‘architectural	  lighting	  design’	  is	  adequately	  
describing	  the	  activities	  in	  each	  IDM	  package.	  	  
Actors 
All	  actors	  described	  in	  the	  IDM	  package	  activities	  are	  not	  fixed	  –	  the	  
activities	  and	  exchange	  requirements	  are	  not	  dependent	  on	  specific	  actors	  
itself,	  but	  are	  representations	  of	  the	  usual	  actors	  involved.	  Who	  performs	  
what	  is	  flexible	  to	  fit	  within	  specific	  organisational	  guidelines.	  Actors	  should	  
also	  not	  be	  seen	  as	  specific	  work	  titles	  but	  more	  as	  disciplines	  –	  for	  example	  
an	  electrical	  engineer	  and	  lighting	  designer	  could	  in	  some	  cases	  be	  the	  same	  
person	  performing	  two	  activities.	  An	  architect	  could	  also	  in	  some	  projects	  
or	  companies	  perform	  the	  work	  of	  a	  lighting	  designer.	  This	  supports	  the	  
generic	  composition	  and	  adaptability	  of	  the	  IDM	  packages.	  	  
	  
Other	  actors	  who	  could	  potentially	  be	  activated	  in	  an	  architectural	  lighting	  
design	  process	  could	  be:	  interior	  architects,	  exhibition	  designers	  and	  
scientists	  or	  researchers	  specialising	  on	  human	  effects	  of	  lighting	  –	  
especially	  within	  healthcare	  lighting	  applications.	  Product	  manufacturers	  
are	  primarily	  displayed	  as	  supplying	  products	  to	  lighting	  designers,	  but	  in	  
some	  projects	  they	  might	  even	  do	  consulting	  thus	  influencing	  the	  design	  
==PAGE:108==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  85 
concept	  as	  well.	  Other	  times	  they	  can	  have	  a	  large	  responsibility	  in	  
redesigning	  own	  products	  to	  fit	  a	  specific	  application.	  Product	  
manufacturers	  then	  become	  co-­‐designers.	  	  
	  
In	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  it	  is	  difficult	  to	  point	  out	  exactly	  what	  
activities	  are	  carried	  out	  by	  the	  lighting	  designer	  or	  the	  electrical	  engineer.	  
This	  depends	  highly	  on	  the	  project	  structure,	  hence	  if	  the	  electrical	  engineer	  
is	  responsible	  for	  lighting	  or	  if	  the	  lighting	  designer	  will	  carry	  all	  
responsibility.	  It	  could	  also	  be	  dependent	  on	  what	  background	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  has,	  as	  if	  he/she	  has	  a	  technical	  or	  creative	  approach	  to	  lighting	  
design.	  	  
Process Map 
Each	  PM	  is	  made	  simple	  and	  narrowed	  down	  to	  the	  most	  essential	  activities	  
but	  still	  flexible	  enough	  to	  be	  adapted	  in	  various	  lighting	  design	  scenarios.	  
As	  earlier	  mentioned	  the	  PMs	  show	  processes	  within	  architectural	  lighting	  
design	  but	  could	  possibly	  be	  adapted	  in	  other	  lighting	  design	  disciplines	  –	  
such	  as	  outdoor	  space	  lighting	  or	  store	  lighting.	  	  
	  
Iterations	  displayed	  in	  the	  PMs	  are	  kept	  to	  a	  minimum,	  even	  though	  in	  
practice	  it	  is	  more	  likely	  iterations	  happen	  between	  each	  activity.	  The	  
gateways	  inserted	  represents	  major	  iteration	  loops	  required.	  A	  gateway	  
loop	  is	  displayed	  to	  go	  back	  to	  a	  specific	  activity	  but	  it	  could	  easily	  be	  the	  
case	  that	  one	  has	  to	  change	  another	  activity	  instead.	  For	  example,	  in	  both	  
packages	  the	  gateway	  goes	  back	  to	  activity	  1.3	  but	  sometimes	  it	  might	  be	  
the	  case	  that	  lighting	  designers	  has	  to	  go	  all	  the	  way	  back	  and	  re-­‐define	  the	  
lighting	  concept	  in	  an	  earlier	  life	  cycle	  stage	  –	  often	  because	  of	  too	  
ambitious	  lighting	  concept	  compared	  to	  e.g.	  budget	  or	  physical	  limits.	  Other	  
times	  it	  might	  just	  be	  sufficient	  to	  adjust	  the	  placement	  to	  get	  the	  design	  to	  
work17.	  Therefore	  the	  gateways	  cannot	  be	  interpreted	  as	  a	  definitive	  loops	  
but	  rather	  how	  far	  back	  is	  usually	  sufficient.	  This	  depends	  on	  the	  project	  or	  
specific	  problem.	  	  
	  
In	  the	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  the	  activity	  1.2	  to	  1.5	  is	  primarily	  
based	  on	  preparing	  and	  performing	  a	  lighting	  simulation	  because	  that	  is	  the	  
most	  common	  activities	  performed	  by	  a	  lighting	  designer	  at	  this	  life	  cycle	  
stage.	  But	  by	  using	  the	  term	  ‘simulation’	  is	  not	  necessarily	  limiting	  the	  
process	  to	  be	  based	  on	  computer	  tools.	  The	  activities	  should	  be	  flexible	  to	  
allow	  for	  other	  validating	  activities,	  such	  as	  hand-­‐calculations	  or	  even	  
constructing	  a	  full-­‐scale	  mock-­‐up	  to	  check	  lighting	  conditions18.	  Regardless	  
of	  what	  method	  is	  performed	  the	  IDM	  package	  sequence	  will	  not	  change	  –	  it	  
still	  requires	  a	  lighting	  designer	  to	  select	  fixtures,	  place	  them	  and	  perform	  a	  
validation.	  The	  term	  ‘validation’	  is	  also	  used	  in	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  
activity	  1.4	  because	  it	  allows	  for	  a	  wide	  interpretation	  on	  how	  to	  validate	  
the	  lighting	  conditions.	  	  
	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
17	  Feedback:	  Lisa	  Trapp,	  COWI	  
18	  Feedback:	  Jørgen	  Kjer,	  COWI	  
==PAGE:109==
IDM DEVELOPMENT // 4	  
	  
86
In	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  the	  activity	  1.5	  (surface	  coordination)	  is	  in	  
terms	  of	  simplicity	  portrayed	  as	  one	  single	  activity.	  In	  reality	  this	  activity	  
would	  be	  rather	  large	  and	  usually	  an	  activity	  that	  is	  performed	  
simultaneous	  with	  the	  others	  and	  not	  subsequent.	  It	  would	  be	  more	  
suitable	  to	  place	  this	  activity	  ‘outside’	  the	  scope	  of	  this	  IDM	  and	  describe	  it	  
as	  a	  whole	  new	  IDM	  itself.	  That	  would	  include	  a	  lot	  more	  actors	  where	  all	  
would	  have	  influence	  on	  the	  final	  surface	  coordination.	  However,	  including	  
the	  activity	  here	  displays	  the	  importance	  of	  information	  delivered	  by	  the	  
lighting	  designer	  to	  the	  electrical	  engineer	  as	  evident	  for	  this	  activity	  to	  
initiate.	  If	  electrical	  engineer	  returns	  with	  positive	  information	  that	  surface	  
is	  clash-­‐free,	  the	  lighting	  designer	  can	  proceed.	  If	  not,	  the	  lighting	  designer	  
has	  to	  change	  the	  placement	  or	  selection	  of	  fixtures	  or	  sometimes	  go	  all	  the	  
way	  back	  and	  re-­‐evaluate	  the	  initial	  lighting	  concept.	  	  
	  
No	  time	  estimation	  has	  been	  assigned	  to	  the	  activities	  at	  this	  point,	  making	  
the	  PM	  difficult	  to	  use	  as	  a	  time-­‐planning	  tool.	  This	  could	  be	  assigned	  at	  the	  
company	  on	  a	  general	  level	  or	  from	  project	  to	  project.	  
	  
Both	  IDM	  packages	  displays	  creative	  design	  processes	  with	  human	  
influence.	  Especially	  the	  first	  three	  activities	  in	  the	  outline	  conceptual	  
design	  stage.	  It	  would	  be	  difficult	  to	  automatize	  these	  three	  activities.	  In	  
theory,	  activity	  1.4	  and	  all	  the	  way	  to	  the	  end	  of	  the	  second	  IDM	  package	  
could	  potentially	  be	  computer-­‐generated.	  One	  can	  already	  see	  DIALux	  
automatically	  calculating	  amount	  of	  fixtures	  and	  placement	  based	  on	  
required	  illumination	  levels.	  This	  could	  be	  supplied	  by	  checking	  compliance	  
with	  control	  a	  defined	  control	  concept	  and	  the	  automatically	  coordinate	  the	  
placement	  in	  a	  central	  BIM	  model	  where	  other	  objects	  would	  be	  visible.	  
However,	  identifying	  a	  lighting	  concept	  and	  selecting	  which	  fixtures	  to	  use	  
is	  still	  done	  manually	  and	  based	  on	  human	  knowledge	  and	  experience.	  	  
	  
In	  the	  future	  it	  could	  theoretically	  be	  possible	  to	  let	  the	  computer	  do	  this	  
activity	  as	  well	  if	  a	  ‘standard’	  design	  was	  preferred.	  A	  human	  would	  need	  to	  
define	  the	  space	  type,	  geometry	  including	  materials,	  and	  set	  up	  basic	  
requirements	  such	  as	  lux-­‐values,	  maximum	  energy	  consumption,	  user-­‐
patterns,	  control	  concept	  and	  even	  maximum	  budget.	  All	  this	  information	  
could	  be	  embedded	  in	  the	  space	  type	  by	  using	  CCS.	  Then	  the	  software	  could,	  
based	  on	  the	  inputted	  data,	  run	  through	  a	  database	  and	  calculate	  the	  most	  
optimal	  selection	  and	  placement	  of	  fixtures.	  By	  doing	  this	  the	  term	  ‘design’	  
would	  almost	  be	  non-­‐existent	  and	  the	  solution	  is	  purely	  computer-­‐
generated	  based	  on	  functionality	  and	  not	  aesthetics.	  
Exchange Requirements 
All	  exchange	  requirements	  are	  based	  on	  information	  obtained	  by	  surveying	  
the	  industry	  professionals	  and	  occasionally	  supplemented	  by	  the	  author’s	  
own	  experience	  with	  lighting	  design.	  The	  exchange	  requirements	  are	  
primarily	  for	  architectural	  lighting	  design	  projects	  and	  might	  not	  be	  fully	  
applicable	  for	  other	  lighting	  application	  disciplines.	  	  
	  
==PAGE:110==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  87 
In	  general	  it	  has	  not	  been	  difficult	  to	  map	  down	  the	  information	  required	  to	  
perform	  activities.	  All	  industry	  experts	  are	  fully	  aware	  of	  their	  own	  
requirements	  towards	  others	  and	  what	  other	  people	  expect	  from	  them.	  
Even	  so	  the	  information	  required	  can	  change	  depending	  on	  the	  project	  type	  
and	  complexity.	  So	  all	  exchange	  requirements	  has	  been	  evaluated	  through	  
assessing	  what	  is	  need-­‐to-­‐have	  to	  perform	  an	  activity	  and	  what	  is	  nice-­‐to-­‐
have.	  	  
	  
One	  IRM	  that	  was	  very	  difficult	  to	  standardise	  was	  the	  lighting	  concept	  in	  
the	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  (IRM	  1.1).	  It	  varies	  a	  lot	  how	  
comprehensive	  a	  lighting	  concept	  is	  delivered.	  It	  also	  typically	  depends	  if	  
the	  lighting	  designer	  developing	  the	  concept	  has	  to	  continue	  the	  next	  life	  
cycle	  stage	  or	  hand	  it	  over	  to	  another	  actor.	  No	  clear	  lines	  of	  what	  a	  lighting	  
concept	  should	  include	  is	  currently	  present	  neither	  on	  company	  level	  nor	  
national	  level,	  e.g.	  ICT.	  So	  the	  authors	  of	  this	  report	  have	  come	  up	  with	  their	  
own	  suggestion	  on	  how	  to	  distribute	  a	  lighting	  concept.	  In	  general	  IRM	  1.1	  
asks	  for	  lot	  of	  information	  that	  can	  possibly	  be	  difficult	  to	  obtain	  in	  the	  
beginning	  of	  a	  project	  life	  cycle.	  Often	  a	  lighting	  concept	  will	  be	  described	  
with	  words	  or	  communicated	  with	  illustrations	  and	  not	  fixed	  numbers	  as	  
what	  the	  IRM	  1.1	  calls	  for.	  Some	  of	  the	  information	  required	  might	  also	  be	  
irrelevant	  for	  certain	  projects	  and	  more	  essential	  for	  others,	  but	  in	  general	  
assumed	  to	  be	  necessary	  in	  order	  to	  accomplish	  a	  typical	  lighting	  design	  at	  
the	  respective	  life	  cycle	  stages.	  	  	  
	  
When	  providing	  space	  geometry	  in	  the	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  
(IRM	  1.2)	  it	  is	  typically	  sufficient	  with	  space	  boundaries.	  This	  represents	  
the	  geometry	  on	  an	  adequate	  simple	  level.	  However,	  space	  boundaries	  are	  
not	  very	  representative	  of	  fixed	  furniture	  or	  permanent	  installations	  within	  
the	  space	  e.g.	  staircases	  or	  podiums.	  Also	  space	  boundaries	  could	  be	  
problematic	  in	  case	  of	  inclined	  ceilings	  or	  other.	  Therefore	  each	  scenario	  
has	  to	  be	  evaluated	  individually	  –	  if	  space	  boundaries	  are	  sufficient	  or	  the	  
whole	  architectural	  model	  should	  be	  exported.	  Each	  space	  evaluated	  should	  
be	  identified	  using	  a	  common	  naming	  system	  such	  as	  CCS	  so	  all	  actors	  know	  
what	  space/room	  is	  talked	  about	  and	  the	  affiliated	  function	  of	  this	  space.	  	  
	  
There	  has	  been	  identified	  some	  issues	  when	  lighting	  designers	  assigns	  
materials	  to	  surfaces	  in	  the	  simulation	  program.	  At	  early	  life	  cycle	  stages	  it	  
is	  very	  difficult	  to	  obtain	  information	  about	  the	  exact	  materials	  from	  the	  
architect	  why	  many	  lighting	  designers	  has	  estimate	  reflection	  values.	  
Letting	  the	  architect	  choose	  more	  specific	  materials	  from	  a	  comprehensive	  
materials	  database	  that	  automatically	  converts	  the	  material	  selected	  to	  a	  
reflectance,	  could	  potentially	  optimise	  this	  information	  exchange	  and	  make	  
the	  simulations	  more	  accurate.	  
	  
When	  finishing	  off	  the	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  a	  lighting	  design	  
deliverable	  contains	  a	  lot	  of	  information	  about	  the	  fixtures	  selected	  and	  
placement.	  All	  this	  information	  is	  automatically	  included	  in	  the	  next	  life	  
cycle	  stage,	  hence	  the	  next	  IDM	  package.	  The	  first	  IRM1.1	  in	  package	  G	  
might	  therefore	  seem	  quite	  comprehensive.	  But	  at	  some	  point	  during	  the	  
==PAGE:111==
IDM DEVELOPMENT // 4	  
	  
88
process	  all	  this	  information	  will	  be	  utilised.	  The	  problem	  occurs	  if	  there	  in	  a	  
project	  has	  not	  been	  activated	  a	  lighting	  design	  in	  life	  cycle	  E	  and	  therefore	  
all	  this	  information	  required	  to	  initiate	  life	  cycle	  G	  would	  be	  missing.	  At	  this	  
point	  it	  might	  not	  be	  possible	  to	  get	  the	  exact	  information	  described	  in	  
IRM1.1	  but	  still	  the	  manufacturer	  name	  or	  identification	  number	  of	  light	  
fixtures.	  It	  is	  then	  up	  to	  the	  lighting	  designer	  or	  electrical	  engineer	  to	  
extract	  all	  relevant	  information	  from	  the	  manufacturer.	  	  
	  
It	  could	  be	  a	  general	  problem	  in	  the	  lighting	  industry,	  that	  datasheets	  and	  
information	  about	  light	  fixtures	  are	  not	  standardised.	  Some	  properties	  can	  
have	  different	  names	  depending	  on	  the	  lighting	  manufacturer,	  and	  some	  
fixture	  properties	  needed	  by	  the	  lighting	  designer	  is	  not	  even	  available	  at	  
selected	  manufacturers.	  This	  can	  limit	  the	  selection	  of	  potential	  light	  
fixtures	  to	  be	  used.	  In	  both	  IDMs	  the	  properties	  in	  the	  deliverables	  are	  using	  
IFC	  naming	  to	  ensure	  a	  common	  terminology	  independent	  of	  software	  
platform.	  Some	  properties	  were	  not	  found	  in	  the	  IFC	  database.	  See	  
Appendix	  H	  for	  a	  comprehensive	  list	  of	  all	  IFC	  properties	  not	  found	  and	  that	  
needs	  to	  be	  developed.	  The	  last	  ORM2.1	  in	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  is	  
comprehensive	  because	  of	  a	  need	  to	  describe	  as	  many	  properties	  as	  
possible	  without	  naming	  the	  manufacturer.	  If	  detailed	  enough	  the	  potential	  
selection	  of	  fixtures	  on	  the	  market	  is	  limited,	  hence	  forcing	  the	  contractor	  to	  
offer	  the	  same	  light	  fixture	  as	  initially	  designed	  with19.	  	  
4.6. SUMMARY 
Based	  on	  the	  IDM	  development	  chapter	  the	  following	  summarising	  points	  
can	  be	  made:	  
	  
o Each	  IDM	  package	  represents	  a	  newly	  generated	  OmniClass	  
discipline	  called	  ‘architectural	  lighting	  design’.	  	  
o Actors	  in	  the	  IDM	  packages	  are	  not	  definitive	  but	  should	  rather	  be	  
seen	  as	  disciplines.	  It	  should	  allow	  for	  flexibility	  and	  adaptability	  for	  
companies	  with	  different	  organisations.	  	  
o Activities	  are	  not	  dependent	  on	  specific	  actors	  but	  different	  actors	  
can	  partake	  in	  activities.	  This	  should	  be	  further	  developed	  in	  the	  
company	  where	  applied.	  	  	  
o Process	  maps	  illustrates	  the	  business	  process	  with	  activities	  and	  
information	  exchange.	  Iterations	  between	  activities	  occur	  but	  are	  
kept	  illustrated	  to	  a	  minimum	  with	  only	  the	  largest	  and	  most	  
influential	  displayed.	  A	  loop’s	  point	  of	  return	  should	  not	  be	  seen	  as	  
definitive	  but	  as	  a	  typical	  representation.	  
o An	  electrical	  engineer	  usually	  carries	  out	  the	  surface	  coordination	  
activity	  in	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  in	  collaboration	  with	  other	  
disciplines.	  This	  activity	  could	  eventually	  be	  described	  by	  an	  IDM	  
itself.	  	  
o It	  is	  theoretically	  possible	  to	  automatize	  the	  process	  maps	  if	  design	  
objective	  and	  human	  influence	  is	  limited,	  hence	  in	  very	  standard	  
lighting	  application	  situations.	  	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
19	  Feedback:	  Jørgen	  Kjer,	  COWI

o Space	  function,	  material	  reflectance	  and	  light	  fixture	  properties	  are	  
the	  information	  exchanged	  with	  the	  less	  standardisation	  and	  
consistency	  in	  both	  quality	  and	  accuracy	  delivered.	  The	  process	  
could	  be	  more	  effective	  if	  information	  were	  standardised	  to	  avoid	  
unnecessary	  iterations	  and	  dialogue.

SUGGESTIONS FOR FURTHER WORK 
Update	  IFC	  
database	  
Properties	  currently	  not	  found	  in	  the	  IFC	  database	  
(as	  of	  February	  2015)	  should	  be	  updated	  to	  make	  it	  
more	  comprehensive	  and	  strengthen	  the	  database’s	  
market	  position.	  Suggestions	  for	  IFC	  updates	  are	  
available	  in	  Appendix	  H	  
Material	  
reflectance	  
information	  
exchange	  
A	  common	  material	  to	  reflectance	  converter/tool	  
could	  be	  developed	  to	  streamline	  information	  
exchanged	  between	  architects	  and	  lighting	  designers.	  
Instead	  of	  calculating	  with	  default	  values	  more	  exact	  
values	  could	  be	  defined	  earlier	  in	  the	  project.	  
Lighting	  
requirements	  
embedded	  in	  
room	  type	  
If	  lighting	  requirements	  in	  a	  room,	  such	  as	  lux	  values,	  
energy	  consumption,	  control	  concept,	  etc.,	  could	  be	  
assigned	  the	  room	  type	  in	  form	  of	  embedded	  in	  the	  
room	  code	  (maybe	  CCS)	  this	  could	  easily	  
communicate	  the	  information	  needed.	  	  
Light	  fixture	  
properties	  
standardisation	  
The	  properties	  described	  in	  the	  coordinated	  design	  
stage	  ORM	  2.1	  could	  possibly	  be	  used	  as	  inspiration	  
for	  a	  standardisation	  of	  information	  provided	  by	  
both	  product	  manufacturers	  and	  lighting	  design	  
demands.	  If	  information	  were	  standardised	  it	  would	  
be	  easier	  for	  lighting	  designers	  to	  evaluate	  and	  
compare	  several	  products.	  
This	  standardisation	  could	  furthermore	  be	  developed	  
to	  consist	  of	  an-­‐easy-­‐to-­‐fill-­‐out	  digital	  form	  for	  
lighting	  professionals	  to	  use	  and	  also	  encourage	  them	  
to	  provide	  all	  information	  required20.	  	  
Develop	  new	  
IDM	  packages	  
Inspired	  in	  this	  report’s	  IDM	  packages	  it	  could	  be	  
relevant	  to	  develop	  further	  packages	  describing	  
other	  lighting	  design	  disciplines,	  such	  as	  theatrical	  
lighting,	  exterior	  lighting,	  sports	  lighting	  and	  road	  
lighting.	  Also	  it	  would	  be	  relevant	  to	  make	  an	  IDM	  
package	  describing	  the	  process	  performed	  when	  
coordinating	  ceilings	  or	  other	  surfaces	  with	  lighting	  
fixtures.

TOOL DEVELOPMENT // 5
