5. 
TOOL DEVELOPMENT 
5.1. INTRODUCTION 
This	  chapter	  will	  cover	  the	  development	  of	  three	  different	  tools	  to	  solve	  
some	  issues	  observed	  during	  the	  IDM	  development	  work	  in	  Chapter	  4.	  After	  
analysing	  these	  packages	  some	  issues	  that	  potentially	  complicate	  the	  
information	  exchange	  between	  actors	  were	  exposed.	  Issues	  that	  require	  
focus	  to	  be	  resolved	  in	  order	  to	  make	  the	  IDM	  packages	  flow	  more	  
streamlined	  and	  eventually	  more	  automatized.	  	  
	  
Based	  on	  the	  surveys	  and	  IDM	  package	  development	  the	  three	  main	  issues	  
are:	  
	  
• Space	  function	  
• Surface	  reflectance	  
• Fixture	  properties	  
	  
A	  common	  problem	  caused	  by	  these	  issues	  is	  that	  they	  are	  responsible	  for	  a	  
lot	  of	  miscommunication	  and	  unnecessary	  dialogue	  as	  seen	  on	  Figure	  26.	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  26	  -­‐	  Three	  issues	  that	  base	  the	  focus	  in	  the	  tool	  development	  chapter.	  
In	  space	  naming	  and	  space	  function	  there	  happens	  to	  be	  an	  issue	  with	  
mutual	  agreement	  on	  how	  spaces	  should	  be	  named	  in	  order	  to	  define	  
lighting	  requirements.	  A	  common	  naming	  system	  such	  as	  CCS	  could	  be	  used	  
to	  solve	  this	  problem.	  Having	  CCS	  codes	  for	  each	  room	  classifies	  it	  and	  
describes	  its	  function	  directly	  from	  the	  code	  and	  makes	  it	  able	  to	  precisely	  
assign	  lighting	  standards	  to	  each	  code.	  Thereby	  it	  will	  be	  possible	  to	  look	  up	  
lighting	  requirements	  based	  on	  the	  CCS	  code.	  	  
==PAGE:116==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  91 
Surface	  reflectances	  are	  based	  on	  personal	  evaluations	  and	  require	  proper	  
communication	  between	  architect	  and	  lighting	  designer.	  There	  are	  
potentially	  room	  for	  errors	  if	  architects	  communicate	  the	  material	  used	  
wrong	  or	  if	  lighting	  designers	  misunderstands	  the	  material	  and	  estimates	  
incorrect	  reflectance.	  	  
A	  lack	  of	  standardisation	  in	  product	  manufacturer’s	  supply	  of	  information	  
about	  their	  fixtures	  makes	  it	  difficult	  for	  lighting	  designers	  to	  obtain	  
adequate	  information	  and	  compare	  different	  products.	  A	  standardisation	  
would	  therefore	  be	  a	  relevant	  and	  interesting	  subject	  to	  look	  further	  into.	  	  
	  
Based	  on	  these	  observations	  three	  tools	  will	  be	  developed	  in	  this	  chapter:	  	  
	  
o A	  CCS	  space	  illumination	  tool,	  linking	  CCS	  classification	  with	  lighting	  
standards;	  
o A	  surface	  reflectance	  database	  with	  a	  large	  number	  of	  commonly	  
used	  finish	  materials	  in	  buildings;	  
o A	  standard	  datasheet	  of	  fixture	  properties	  used	  by	  lighting	  designers	  
and	  product	  manufacturers	  to	  streamline	  and	  synchronise	  data	  
exchange.	  	  
5.2. CCS SPACE ILLUMINATION TOOL 
5.2.1. BACKGROUND 
Illumination	  requirements	  in	  a	  building	  are	  closely	  related	  to	  the	  type	  and	  
function	  of	  spaces.	  When	  lighting	  designers	  in	  collaboration	  with	  building	  
owners	  determines	  the	  illumination	  requirements	  they	  would	  need	  to	  know	  
the	  different	  room	  functions	  and	  set	  different	  requirements	  for	  each.	  CCS	  
includes	  a	  comprehensive	  list	  of	  specific	  use	  of	  rooms	  with	  around	  90	  room	  
types	  related	  to	  buildings.	  This	  makes	  it	  a	  common	  basis	  to	  name	  spaces	  
and	  exchange	  and	  communicate	  information	  and	  prevent	  
misunderstandings	  about	  room	  functions	  between	  actors.	  	  
	  
As	  stated	  in	  the	  IDM	  packages	  it	  would	  be	  beneficial	  if	  CCS	  codes	  were	  
assigned	  to	  each	  room	  to	  describe	  its	  function.	  Presently	  CCS	  codes	  can	  
contain	  properties	  associated	  to	  that	  specific	  room	  why	  information	  of	  
lighting	  requirements	  for	  each	  room	  type	  could	  be	  the	  next	  step.	  Doing	  so	  
requires	  identifying	  all	  illumination	  requirements	  for	  different	  room	  types	  
and	  then	  assign	  them	  according	  to	  CCS	  room	  types.	  	  
	  
The	  conventional	  way	  to	  determine	  illumination	  requirements	  is	  through	  
communication	  and	  information	  exchange	  with	  the	  building	  owner.	  A	  
lighting	  designer	  receives	  a	  drawing	  or	  model	  with	  room	  types	  and	  then	  has	  
to	  follow	  up	  on	  requirements	  for	  each	  room	  type	  manually	  –	  either	  by	  
information	  from	  the	  building	  owner	  or	  cross	  check	  the	  rooms	  with	  
national	  standards.	  Regardless	  to	  say,	  this	  is	  a	  process	  where	  time	  could	  be	  
saved	  if	  performed	  more	  automatically.	  A	  way	  to	  do	  this	  could	  be	  to	  have	  
illumination	  requirements	  available	  directly	  in	  the	  model	  or	  easily	  
accessible	  by	  a	  tool.	  This	  tool	  is	  what	  this	  section	  intends	  to	  develop.	  	  
==PAGE:117==
TOOL DEVELOPMENT // 5 
	  
92
5.2.2. METHOD 
At	  a	  current	  state	  the	  lighting	  requirements	  follows	  the	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  
(Danish	  Standards,	  2011)	  standard	  that	  covers	  a	  large	  range	  of	  room	  
functions	  and	  assign	  specific	  illumination	  requirements	  for	  each	  room.	  This	  
standard	  has	  a	  large	  amount	  of	  rooms	  that	  exceed	  the	  current	  room	  
definitions	  in	  CCS.	  For	  a	  starter	  it	  is	  relevant	  to	  look	  at	  the	  Danish	  defined	  
room	  types.	  This	  tool	  is	  therefore	  an	  overview	  of	  illumination	  requirements	  
for	  each	  CCS	  space	  function	  based	  on	  the	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  standard.	  	  
	  
First	  step	  in	  developing	  this	  tool	  is	  crosschecking	  every	  room	  type	  to	  see	  if	  
there	  are	  equivalents	  between	  CCS	  room	  types	  and	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  room	  
types.	  In	  the	  case	  where	  no	  equivalent	  between	  CCS	  and	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  is	  
found,	  it	  is	  investigated	  whether	  there	  is	  an	  equivalent	  with	  the	  other	  
Danish	  lighting	  standard	  DS700	  (Danish	  Standards,	  2005).	  CCS	  room	  types	  
are	  generally	  specified	  on	  a	  more	  overall	  level	  than	  the	  DS	  standards.	  This	  is	  
due	  to	  the	  standard’s	  focus	  on	  work	  functions	  rather	  than	  room	  types.	  One	  
room	  in	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  can	  easily	  be	  divided	  further	  into	  separate	  
workspace	  zones.	  If	  a	  room	  in	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  has	  several	  lighting	  
requirements	  they	  will	  all	  be	  linked	  to	  CCS.	  	  
	  
Every	  time	  a	  CCS	  corresponding	  room	  is	  found	  in	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  the	  
reference	  number	  for	  that	  room	  is	  noted	  in	  the	  scheme.	  A	  qualified	  
assessment	  has	  to	  be	  taken	  when	  the	  room	  types	  cannot	  unite.	  If	  there	  are	  
no	  corresponding	  room	  types	  to	  CCS	  in	  either	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  or	  DS700	  it	  is	  
simply	  not	  possible	  to	  assign	  an	  illuminance	  requirement	  for	  that	  room.	  	  
5.2.3. RESULTS 
A	  small	  part	  of	  the	  developed	  tool	  is	  visible	  on	  Table	  8.	  The	  developed	  tool	  
defines	  illumination	  requirements	  for	  CCS	  room	  types	  according	  to	  national	  
standards.	  The	  full	  list	  of	  rooms	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  I.	  
	  
Table	  8	  –	  Part	  of	  the	  CCS	  room	  type	  illumination	  requirements.	  	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:118==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  93 
5.2.4. DISCUSSION 
At	  the	  current	  state	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  is	  on	  its	  way	  to	  become	  the	  national	  
standard	  of	  which	  the	  Danish	  Building	  Regulations	  (DBR)	  will	  require	  new	  
buildings	  to	  comply	  with	  (Danish	  Standards,	  2014).	  It	  was	  originally	  due	  to	  
be	  the	  national	  standard	  in	  the	  DBR	  2015	  but	  it	  is	  more	  likely	  it	  will	  be	  
finally	  approved	  in	  2016	  where	  also	  additional	  Danish	  requirements	  will	  be	  
included.	  Since	  this	  is	  the	  future	  for	  Danish	  lighting	  requirements	  it	  only	  
makes	  sense	  to	  create	  the	  tool	  based	  on	  these	  requirements.	  Even	  though	  it	  
is	  not	  legally	  mandatory	  to	  follow	  the	  standard,	  it	  has	  long	  been	  the	  
unofficial	  standard	  in	  the	  industry.	  It	  is	  also	  way	  more	  comprehensive	  than	  
the	  current	  DS700	  standard.	  Making	  the	  tool	  and	  assigning	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  
minimum	  requirements	  to	  CCS	  room	  types	  makes	  CCS	  ready	  for	  the	  future.	  	  
	  
Some	  of	  the	  CCS	  room	  types	  had	  no	  corresponding	  room	  types	  in	  DS/EN	  
12464-­‐1	  why	  DS700	  were	  used	  as	  a	  substitute.	  One	  reason	  for	  this	  could	  be	  
that	  DS700	  is	  more	  nationally	  focused	  on	  the	  Danish	  building	  sector	  and	  
might	  include	  room	  types	  more	  commonly	  found	  in	  Denmark.	  DS/EN	  
12464-­‐1	  is	  still	  a	  European	  standard,	  which	  eventually	  will	  get	  a	  separate	  
national	  annex	  for	  each	  country	  in	  which	  it	  is	  implemented.	  Another	  reason	  
could	  be	  that	  these	  rooms	  are	  irrelevant	  for	  a	  lighting	  point-­‐of-­‐view;	  either	  
because	  human	  presence	  is	  limited	  if	  any	  or	  there	  are	  no	  need	  for	  excess	  
security.	  In	  other	  cases	  it	  might	  apply	  that	  it	  is	  simply	  not	  relevant	  or	  
possible	  to	  assign	  a	  minimum	  illuminance	  requirement.	  One	  of	  the	  rooms	  
not	  having	  any	  minimum	  requirement	  is	  ‘AAB’	  –	  living	  room,	  which	  is	  a	  
room	  with	  residential	  purpose.	  No	  work	  task	  is	  performed	  a	  room	  like	  this	  
and	  there	  are	  no	  special	  safety	  conditions	  to	  take	  into	  account.	  Therefore	  it	  
does	  not	  make	  sense	  to	  talk	  about	  minimum	  requirements	  towards	  lighting.	  
Another	  example	  is	  ‘AHD’	  –	  exhibition,	  which	  one	  cannot	  demand	  minimum	  
requirements	  for.	  In	  a	  dynamic	  exhibition	  it	  is	  one	  of	  the	  core	  principles	  
that	  lighting	  is	  allowed	  to	  span	  from	  very	  light	  to	  very	  dark.	  	  
	  
The	  final	  outcome	  of	  this	  tool	  is	  a	  list	  of	  all	  CCS	  room	  types	  with	  
corresponding	  international	  lighting	  requirements.	  This	  list	  is	  very	  useful	  
for	  further	  work	  in	  CCS	  terms,	  as	  illumination	  values	  could	  potentially	  be	  
one	  of	  the	  properties	  assigned	  to	  space	  functions	  in	  CCS.	  For	  all	  room	  types	  
there	  could	  be	  a	  property	  group	  named	  ‘Indoor	  environment’	  where	  further	  
properties	  could	  be	  assigned	  such	  as:	  ventilation	  rate,	  daylight	  factor	  and	  
illumination	  requirement,	  among	  others.	  If	  properties	  cannot	  be	  described	  
as	  actual	  numbers	  they	  could	  at	  least	  refer	  to	  the	  related	  standards.	  	  
	  
CCS	  could	  also	  look	  towards	  the	  standard	  and	  evaluate	  if	  CCS	  should	  include	  
more	  room	  types	  and	  expand	  their	  list.	  Although	  one	  of	  the	  key	  remarks	  
with	  CCS	  is	  that	  it	  is	  simple	  and	  precise.	  For	  this	  reason	  it	  might	  also	  be	  
interesting	  for	  DS/EN	  to	  look	  at	  the	  CCS	  classification	  to	  see	  if	  some	  of	  their	  
room	  types	  are	  actually	  irrelevant.	  Especially	  when	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  
becomes	  the	  national	  standard	  in	  Denmark,	  the	  national	  annex	  could	  
include	  a	  list	  of	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  rooms	  described	  with	  corresponding	  CCS	  
room	  types	  as	  an	  alternative	  to	  their	  own	  room	  naming	  system.	  This	  
argument	  is	  further	  supported	  with	  CCS	  being	  a	  growing	  classification	  
==PAGE:119==
TOOL DEVELOPMENT // 5 
	  
94
system	  and	  if	  the	  Danish	  building	  sector	  at	  some	  point	  completely	  adopts	  
CCS,	  then	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  would	  secure	  itself	  for	  the	  future	  by	  using	  CCS	  
now.	  Of	  course	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  includes	  a	  large	  number	  of	  very	  specific	  
rooms,	  tasks	  and	  activities	  that	  is	  not	  possible	  to	  find	  corresponding	  in	  the	  
CCS	  classification,	  why	  CCS	  cannot	  replace	  the	  DS/EN	  system	  developed	  
completely.	  	  
	  
The	  relevance	  of	  having	  this	  tool	  is	  undeniable	  since	  it	  helps	  building	  bridge	  
between	  several	  standards	  and	  makes	  it	  easier	  for	  the	  lighting	  designer,	  to	  
gather	  relevant	  information	  without	  going	  through	  a	  large	  set	  of	  documents.	  
This	  tool	  aims	  directly	  at	  the	  core	  problem	  with	  too	  many	  naming	  formats:	  
if	  there	  is	  no	  common	  ‘language’,	  it	  is	  the	  end	  user	  that	  ends	  up	  being	  
confused	  and	  wasting	  time.	  With	  this	  tool	  the	  lighting	  designer	  can	  quickly	  
get	  an	  overview	  of	  the	  lighting	  requirements	  in	  all	  sorts	  of	  rooms.	  
5.3. REFLECTANCE CALCULATOR 
5.3.1. BACKGROUND 
When	  a	  lighting	  designer	  performs	  a	  lighting	  simulation	  the	  surface	  
reflectance	  is	  essential	  information.	  When	  calculating	  the	  light	  distribution	  
the	  software	  takes	  into	  account	  three	  things:	  the	  3D	  space	  geometry,	  
surface	  reflectance	  and	  the	  photometric	  data	  included	  in	  the	  light	  source	  
object.	  The	  major	  lighting	  simulation	  software	  packages,	  DIALux	  and	  Relux,	  
both	  uses	  reflectance	  based	  on	  either	  the	  surface	  material	  or	  colour	  
provided	  in	  the	  program.	  	  
	  
A	  ‘reflectance’	  is	  the	  percentage	  of	  light	  emitted	  on	  the	  surface	  that	  is	  
reflected	  back.	  The	  rest	  of	  light	  is	  either	  absorbed	  by	  the	  surface	  or	  
transmitted	  through.	  Different	  kinds	  of	  reflections	  occur	  as	  seen	  on	  Figure	  
27:	  specular,	  diffuse	  or	  spread.	  Specular	  reflection	  is	  when	  the	  light	  is	  
reflected	  into	  a	  single	  outgoing	  direction	  and	  the	  reflected	  angle	  is	  the	  same	  
as	  the	  incident	  ray	  –	  often	  the	  case	  with	  metallic	  or	  mirrored	  surfaces.	  
Diffuse	  reflection	  happens	  when	  the	  ingoing	  ray	  is	  reflected	  in	  many	  
directions	  -­‐	  often	  the	  case	  with	  matte	  materials	  i.e.	  the	  majority	  of	  building	  
materials.	  A	  mix	  between	  specular	  and	  diffuse	  reflection	  is	  called	  spread	  
reflection	  that	  occurs	  when	  light	  hits	  a	  rough	  surface	  with	  many	  
irregularities	  that	  has	  both	  specular	  and	  diffuse	  reflection	  –	  for	  example	  
corrugated	  metal,	  plastic	  or	  glass.	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  27	  -­‐	  Different	  types	  of	  reflection	  
Conventionally	  the	  lighting	  designer	  will	  receive	  information	  about	  the	  
surfaces	  from	  the	  architect	  who	  has	  an	  opinion	  about	  this	  due	  to	  aesthetics	  
or	  design	  guidelines.	  This	  information	  will	  usually	  be	  what	  kind	  of	  materials	  
==PAGE:120==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  95 
or	  colour	  is	  used	  for	  the	  surfaces.	  Next	  step	  in	  the	  process	  is	  for	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  to	  evaluate	  this	  material	  selection	  and	  translate	  this	  into	  a	  surface	  
reflectance	  to	  use	  in	  lighting	  simulation	  software.	  Often	  this	  evaluation	  is	  
based	  on	  personal	  experience	  thus	  not	  a	  standardised	  method,	  why	  
different	  lighting	  designers	  might	  have	  different	  opinions	  on	  what	  values	  to	  
put	  in.	  It	  is	  very	  often	  seen	  that	  when	  another	  lighting	  designer	  is	  taking	  
over	  the	  simulation	  model	  the	  reflectances	  will	  be	  re-­‐evaluated	  and	  
adjusted	  to	  fit	  the	  new	  designer’s	  opinion.	  All	  these	  personal	  opinions	  could	  
potentially	  influence	  the	  final	  result	  and	  are	  obstacles	  towards	  creating	  
standardised	  methods.	  	  
	  
Another	  big	  problem	  is	  that	  the	  lighting	  designer	  evaluating	  the	  reflectances	  
is	  usually	  not	  a	  materials	  expert	  and	  purely	  base	  his/her	  evaluating	  upon	  
information	  received	  by	  the	  architect.	  Unless	  the	  architect	  provides	  the	  
lighting	  designer	  with	  very	  precise	  material	  definition	  (which	  is	  rare)	  it	  
ends	  up	  being	  the	  lighting	  designer	  guessing	  what	  exact	  material	  the	  
architect	  means.	  For	  example,	  if	  the	  architect	  says	  the	  flooring	  is	  made	  of	  
wood,	  the	  lighting	  designer	  has	  to	  assume	  the	  type	  and	  shading	  or	  colour	  of	  
the	  wood,	  or	  whether	  there	  are	  finish	  or	  surface	  glossiness,	  to	  estimate	  a	  
correct	  reflection.	  Of	  course	  the	  lighting	  designer	  could	  go	  back	  to	  the	  
architect	  to	  get	  approval	  of	  his/her	  assumed	  material	  but	  this	  is	  just	  
another	  time-­‐consuming	  step	  that	  is	  better	  off	  avoided.	  It	  also	  happens	  
often	  that	  walls,	  ceiling	  and	  floor	  are	  assumed	  to	  have	  ‘default’	  values	  of	  
reflectances.	  These	  values	  will	  in	  most	  cases	  be	  lower	  and	  therefore	  more	  
pessimistic	  than	  if	  more	  realistic	  values	  were	  chosen.	  This	  can	  potentially	  
cause	  the	  lighting	  design	  to	  be	  over-­‐dimensioned	  resulting	  in	  more	  lighting	  
fixtures	  than	  actually	  needed.	  As	  seen	  in	  the	  investigation	  in	  Appendix	  J	  the	  
average	  illumination	  levels	  can	  be	  increased	  with	  up	  to	  27	  %	  if	  choosing	  
more	  realistic	  and	  optimistic	  values	  of	  white	  walls	  than	  default	  estimations.	  	  
	  
One	  way	  to	  facilitate	  a	  solution	  for	  this	  could	  be	  to	  provide	  the	  architect	  
with	  a	  simple	  and	  easy	  tool	  that	  calculates	  a	  reflectance	  from	  the	  exact	  
material	  the	  architect	  had	  in	  mind	  –	  perhaps	  with	  pictures	  or	  other	  visual	  
references	  to	  support	  the	  architect’s	  imagination.	  After	  calculating	  a	  
reflectance	  this	  value	  can	  then	  be	  provided	  to	  the	  lighting	  designer	  as	  
primary	  information.	  Material	  selection	  and	  pictures	  of	  it	  would	  be	  
secondary	  information.	  	  
5.3.2. METHOD 
First	  step	  is	  to	  gather	  information	  about	  different	  material’s	  reflectances	  
and	  construct	  a	  database	  containing	  this	  information.	  The	  most	  commonly	  
used	  building	  materials	  will	  be	  included	  and	  evaluated	  in	  this	  database	  –	  
based	  on	  the	  author’s	  selection.	  As	  a	  basis	  for	  the	  author’s	  evaluation	  of	  
material’s	  reflections	  the	  sources	  in	  Table	  9	  has	  been	  used.	  	  
==PAGE:121==
TOOL DEVELOPMENT // 5 
	  
96
	  
Table	  9	  –	  Main	  sources	  used	  to	  evaluate	  material’s	  reflectances.	  	  
SOURCES 
SBI	  203	  
DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  (Danish	  Standards,	  2011)	  
DBR10	  (Danish	  Energy	  Agency,	  2014)	  
Datasheets	  from	  product	  manufacturers	  
DIALux	  material	  database	  
Relux	  material	  database	  
Velux	  Visualizer	  
Autodesk	  material	  library	  2014	  
	  
Each	  source	  has	  a	  selection	  of	  materials	  with	  reflectances.	  Where	  more	  than	  
one	  source	  has	  values	  for	  the	  same	  material	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  make	  an	  
evaluation	  of	  the	  most	  realistic	  value.	  If	  some	  materials	  have	  a	  large	  range	  
of	  reflectance	  values,	  this	  range	  will	  be	  provided	  in	  the	  tool	  as	  well.	  This	  
makes	  it	  possible	  for	  the	  end-­‐user	  to	  make	  a	  final	  personal	  call	  on	  what	  
value	  is	  the	  most	  realistic.	  	  	  
	  
	  The	  tool	  itself	  is	  developed	  in	  Excel	  as	  drop-­‐down	  menus	  that	  references	  to	  
an	  Excel	  database.	  The	  database	  will	  consist	  of	  three	  subcategories:	  
	  
o Material	  category	  (metal,	  wood,	  paint	  etc.)	  
o Specific	  material	  (metal	  type,	  wood	  type,	  paint	  colour,	  etc.)	  
o Finish	  (gloss,	  matte,	  brightness,	  etc.)	  
	  
It	  will	  also	  be	  these	  three	  categories	  the	  end-­‐user	  would	  need	  to	  evaluate	  
the	  material	  selection	  upon.	  If	  all	  three	  are	  filled	  out	  correctly	  the	  tool	  will	  
give	  a	  reflectance	  based	  on	  the	  database	  value.	  	  
5.3.3. RESULTS 
A	  fragment	  of	  the	  developed	  tool	  can	  be	  seen	  on	  Figure	  28	  and	  is	  also	  
obtainable	  as	  an	  Excel	  by	  accessing	  the	  Excel	  file	  through	  the	  URL	  provided	  
in	  Figure	  28	  or	  the	  attached	  CD-­‐ROM.	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  28	  -­‐	  Reflectance	  tool.	  Download	  the	  Excel	  file	  on:	  
https://www.dropbox.com/s/ml13kopay8cbvro/Material_to_reflection_converter.xlsm?dl=0	  
In	  this	  example	  the	  reflectance	  for	  polished	  aluminium	  is	  found	  to	  be	  0.7	  or	  
70	  %.	  The	  typical	  range	  of	  this	  material	  is	  0.65-­‐0.75	  depending	  on	  the	  
specific	  aluminium	  material.	  Type	  of	  reflection	  is	  specular	  due	  to	  its	  
polished	  surface.	  As	  seen	  here,	  aluminium	  is	  found	  under	  the	  ‘Metal’	  
==PAGE:122==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  97 
material	  category,	  which	  is	  the	  first	  to	  be	  filled	  out.	  A	  demonstration	  on	  how	  
to	  use	  the	  tool	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Appendix	  K.	  
5.3.4. DISCUSSION 
The	  main	  purpose	  of	  this	  tool	  was	  to	  simplify	  the	  working	  process	  and	  
interaction	  between	  architect	  and	  lighting	  designer	  and	  ensure	  that	  
information	  exchanged	  is	  the	  most	  correct	  one.	  With	  this	  simple	  tool	  it	  is	  
easier	  for	  the	  architect	  to	  define	  its	  material	  as	  precise	  as	  possible	  in	  order	  
to	  determine	  a	  correct	  reflectance.	  It	  is	  the	  author’s	  persuasion	  that	  this	  tool	  
will	  require	  a	  little	  more	  action	  from	  the	  architect	  but	  with	  more	  correct	  
lighting	  analysis	  and	  less	  dialogue	  with	  the	  lighting	  designers	  as	  a	  result.	  
Also	  by	  creating	  the	  tool	  with	  a	  simple	  interface	  reduces	  the	  complexity	  of	  
which	  the	  architect	  has	  to	  relate	  to.	  	  
	  
This	  tool	  could	  also	  be	  used	  as	  an	  aid	  in	  the	  design	  process	  by	  helping	  the	  
architect	  understand	  the	  selection	  of	  materials	  and	  the	  consequence	  on	  
lighting.	  One	  scenario	  could	  be,	  that	  the	  lighting	  designer	  tells	  the	  architect	  
that	  walls	  or	  ceilings	  has	  to	  be	  at	  least	  0.7,	  then	  the	  architect	  can	  change	  the	  
materials	  and	  see	  the	  effects	  directly.	  	  	  
	  
Values	  and	  sources	  this	  tool	  has	  been	  developed	  upon	  are	  limited.	  It	  is	  very	  
difficult	  to	  obtain	  ‘general’	  reflectance	  values	  about	  materials	  as	  they	  are	  so	  
fluctuating.	  For	  example	  the	  reflectance	  of	  aluminium	  could	  be	  very	  
different	  from	  one	  product	  manufacturer	  to	  another	  one,	  depending	  on	  the	  
very	  specific	  material	  composition	  or	  finish.	  Therefore,	  there	  are	  very	  
limited	  sources	  that	  actively	  define	  general	  reflectances	  for	  a	  large	  number	  
of	  materials,	  as	  that	  would	  simply	  not	  be	  possible.	  Especially	  the	  amount	  of	  
academic	  sources	  that	  states	  standard	  reflectances	  is	  almost	  non-­‐existing.	  	  
	  
The	  authors	  therefore	  individually	  evaluate	  the	  sources	  on	  which	  the	  
reflectance	  tool	  is	  based.	  Danish	  national	  standards	  such	  as	  SBI	  203	  
(Johansen	  &	  Christoffersen,	  2002),	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  and	  DBR10	  all	  states	  
some	  recommended	  reflectances	  typically	  used	  for	  floors,	  walls	  and	  
ceilings.	  These	  values	  will	  most	  likely	  be	  based	  on	  historical	  facts	  and	  
empirical	  investigations	  on	  typical	  interior	  materials	  and	  how	  they	  degrade	  
with	  time.	  These	  references	  are	  therefore	  highly	  reliable	  but	  will	  only	  
provide	  default	  values	  and	  not	  material-­‐specific	  values:	  for	  instance,	  these	  
default	  values	  does	  not	  consider	  if	  walls	  are	  painted	  white	  grey,	  but	  rather	  a	  
typical	  average	  value.	  Other	  places	  where	  lists	  of	  material	  reflectances	  can	  
be	  found	  are	  among	  the	  lighting	  simulation	  software,	  which	  has	  made	  their	  
own	  evaluation	  of	  different	  material’s	  reflectances.	  Velux	  Visualizer	  and	  
Revit	  both	  provide	  reflectances,	  which	  are	  very	  optimistic,	  compared	  to	  
other	  sources.	  	  
Of	  course	  the	  tool	  developed	  would	  be	  more	  reliable	  if	  values	  referenced	  to	  
be	  based	  on	  an	  academic	  research.	  It	  is	  also	  risky	  to	  take	  the	  values	  from	  
this	  tool	  and	  use	  them	  as	  non-­‐negotiable	  ‘definitive’	  reflectances,	  exactly	  
due	  to	  the	  fact	  that	  one	  cannot	  generalise	  all	  materials	  having	  one	  
reflectance.	  Every	  situation	  and	  specific	  material	  needs	  to	  evaluate	  
==PAGE:123==
TOOL DEVELOPMENT // 5 
	  
98
individually	  and	  this	  tool	  and	  the	  values	  that	  follow	  are	  meant	  as	  a	  guideline	  
that	  shows	  the	  most	  commonly	  assumed	  values.	  	  
	  
The	  tool	  as	  it	  is	  at	  the	  moment	  is	  not	  very	  comprehensive	  but	  has	  included	  
the	  most	  common	  building	  materials.	  One	  idea	  for	  further	  work	  could	  be	  to	  
investigate	  a	  larger	  pool	  of	  materials	  and	  include	  them	  in	  this	  tool.	  Making	  
this	  tool	  open	  source	  and	  available	  online,	  could	  also	  open	  up	  for	  the	  
possibility	  for	  lighting	  professionals	  to	  fill	  in	  their	  materials	  or	  comment	  on	  
existing	  materials.	  This	  way,	  if	  enough	  people	  participated	  the	  reliance	  of	  
the	  values	  would	  be	  strengthened	  significantly.	  Suddenly	  the	  material’s	  
reflectances	  would	  be	  based	  on	  a	  large	  number	  of	  professional’s	  personal	  
experience,	  which	  is	  of	  much	  higher	  value,	  compared	  to	  the	  handful	  of	  
sources	  used	  by	  the	  authors	  to	  develop	  this	  tool.	  	  
	  
The	  present	  tool	  can	  be	  used	  as	  it	  is	  and	  will	  most	  likely	  ease	  the	  working	  
process	  for	  a	  number	  of	  lighting	  professionals.	  However,	  the	  tool	  is	  not	  
complete	  and	  there	  are	  still	  things	  to	  improve.	  If	  combined	  with	  the	  CCS	  
room	  type	  classification	  like	  described	  in	  Section	  5.2,	  the	  architect	  could	  
provide	  surface	  reflectances	  together	  with	  the	  CCS	  class	  code.	  This	  way	  a	  
tool	  like	  this	  could	  potentially	  solve	  two	  problems:	  facilitate	  a	  proper	  
reflectance	  evaluation	  of	  the	  precise	  material,	  and	  avoid	  unnecessary	  
dialogue	  between	  architect	  and	  lighting	  designer	  by	  providing	  sufficient	  
information	  from	  the	  start.	  	  
5.4. LIGHT FIXTURE PROPERTIES STANDARD 
5.4.1. BACKGROUND 
Once	  a	  lighting	  design	  is	  finished	  the	  results	  need	  to	  be	  communicated,	  
including	  specifications	  of	  the	  fixtures	  chosen.	  In	  most	  cases	  no	  
manufacturer-­‐specific	  information	  is	  allowed	  when	  tendering	  a	  lighting	  
project	  why	  a	  very	  comprehensive	  description	  about	  the	  light	  fixtures	  is	  
needed.	  	  
	  
At	  present	  state	  there	  is	  no	  industry-­‐wide	  standardisation	  or	  common	  
ground	  on	  which	  information	  to	  include	  about	  light	  fixtures.	  This	  means,	  
information	  that	  is	  relevant	  for	  lighting	  designers	  to	  know	  about	  possible	  
products	  in	  their	  design	  is	  not	  always	  obtainable;	  or	  at	  least	  not	  as	  standard	  
information.	  This	  requires	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  waste	  time	  gathering	  
information	  from	  product	  manufacturers	  or	  looking	  through	  large	  amounts	  
of	  datasheets	  just	  to	  obtain	  some	  single	  values.	  This	  time	  could	  have	  been	  
spent	  more	  effectively	  elsewhere.	  	  
	  
Lack	  of	  standardisation	  leaves	  tracks	  on	  product	  manufacturer’s	  datasheets,	  
where	  one	  cannot	  find	  two	  product	  manufacturers	  with	  the	  same	  datasheet	  
template.	  Or	  in	  product	  manufacturer’s	  3D	  lighting	  objects	  where	  
information	  embedded	  is	  not	  always	  the	  same.	  The	  authors	  of	  this	  report	  
investigated	  10	  different	  Revit	  light	  fixture	  objects	  and	  10	  datasheets	  from	  
different	  product	  manufacturers	  without	  finding	  two	  exact	  similar.	  	  
One	  surveyed	  industry	  professional	  expressed	  enthusiasm	  in	  having	  an	  
industry-­‐wide	  standardisation	  of	  light	  fixture	  property	  content	  but	  stating	  
==PAGE:124==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  99 
that	  the	  main	  obstacle	  was	  to	  determine	  what	  lighting	  designers	  saw	  as	  the	  
most	  relevant	  information21.	  
	  
Therefore	  this	  section	  aims	  to	  gather	  the	  most	  relevant	  information	  for	  
lighting	  designers	  to	  obtain	  from	  lighting	  fixtures,	  which	  can	  result	  in	  a	  
proposal	  for	  product	  manufacturers	  to	  streamline	  their	  datasheets	  and	  3D	  
objects.	  This	  would	  potentially	  benefit	  lighting	  designers	  and	  make	  it	  easier	  
to	  compare	  different	  products	  simultaneously.	  Eventually	  product	  
manufacturers	  can	  benefit	  from	  this	  standardisation	  as	  well,	  especially	  if	  
common	  datasheets	  or	  3D	  objects	  templates	  were	  provided,	  as	  this	  would	  
mean	  time	  and	  resources	  spent	  elsewhere.	  	  
5.4.2. METHOD 
Gathering	  information	  relevant	  for	  lighting	  designers	  to	  know	  about	  their	  
fixtures	  both	  when	  designing	  the	  light	  and	  specifying	  the	  requirements	  has	  
been	  conducted	  while	  interviewing	  the	  industry	  professionals	  and	  resulted	  
in	  the	  ERs	  in	  subsection	  4.3.4	  and	  4.4.4.	  Alongside	  with	  this,	  a	  number	  of	  
lighting	  products	  from	  four	  different	  manufacturers	  will	  be	  investigated	  to	  
find	  common	  properties	  that	  all	  of	  them	  provides.	  The	  product	  
manufacturers	  (PRM)	  selected	  are	  some	  of	  the	  leading	  international	  PRMs	  
present	  in	  Denmark	  and	  can	  be	  seen	  on	  Table	  10.	  	  
	  
Table	  10	  -­‐	  Product	  manufacturers	  assessed	  to	  develop	  standard	  datasheet	  
PRODUCT MANUFACTURER 
COUNTRY OF ORIGIN 
Philips	  Lighting	  
The	  Netherlands	  
Louis	  Poulsen	  
Denmark	  
Glamox/Luxo	  
Norway	  
Zumtobel	  
Austria	  
	  
All	  these	  PRMs	  had	  their	  majority	  of	  products	  available	  as	  Revit	  3D	  objects	  
with	  embedded	  information	  and	  were	  compared.	  	  
	  
When	  common	  properties	  are	  found	  in	  PRM’s	  datasheets	  or	  3D	  objects	  they	  
will	  be	  compared	  to	  the	  statements	  of	  lighting	  designers,	  hence	  the	  
properties	  described	  in	  the	  ERs	  in	  subsection	  4.3.4	  and	  4.4.4.	  If	  relevant	  
information,	  this	  will	  proceed	  to	  the	  standardisation	  proposal.	  If	  some	  of	  
the	  PRMs	  have	  diverging	  or	  unique	  properties	  this	  will	  be	  taken	  into	  
account	  and	  be	  evaluated	  if	  really	  important	  information	  or	  ‘nice-­‐to-­‐have’	  
information.	  Figure	  29	  shows	  a	  diagrammatic	  overview	  of	  the	  selection	  
process.	  	  
	  
When	  essential	  information	  is	  gathered	  in	  a	  standard	  datasheet	  all	  these	  
properties	  will	  have	  assigned	  IFC	  corresponding	  properties	  to	  make	  the	  
datasheet	  have	  a	  wider	  application	  area	  and	  later	  use	  it	  in	  technical	  matters.	  
Where	  IFC	  does	  not	  have	  corresponding	  properties	  this	  will	  be	  assessed	  
and	  possibly	  used	  as	  argument	  for	  IFC	  to	  develop	  their	  database.	  	  	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
21Survey:	  Kasper	  Hammer,	  Louis	  Poulsen

Figure	  29	  -­‐	  Process	  map	  of	  selecting	  and	  merging	  relevant	  information	  from	  both	  datasheets	  and	  3D

5.4.3. RESULTS 
All	  accumulated	  essential	  information	  related	  to	  light	  fixtures	  and	  light	  
sources	  is	  gathered	  in	  two	  standard	  datasheets:	  One	  for	  ‘standard’	  
applications,	  meaning	  datasheets	  and	  other	  non-­‐interactive	  media,	  and	  one	  
proposal	  for	  a	  standard	  set	  of	  properties	  for	  Revit	  lighting	  fixtures.	  Both	  are	  
available	  in	  Appendix	  L	  and	  Appendix	  M.	  
Furthermore	  an	  IFC	  translator	  has	  been	  developed	  that	  translates	  Revit	  
properties	  into	  IFC	  user-­‐defined	  property	  sets.	  This	  tool	  is	  necessary	  if	  one	  
wishes	  to	  export	  a	  Revit	  lighting	  fixture	  to	  IFC	  and	  keep	  all	  standardised	  
properties.	  This	  .txt	  file	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  N.	  	  
5.4.4. DISCUSSION 
It	  is	  assumed	  that	  all	  actors	  involved	  will	  benefit	  from	  having	  a	  
standardised	  light	  fixture	  property	  list	  to	  follow.	  It	  should	  be	  pointed	  out,	  
that	  the	  list	  developed	  is	  minimum	  required	  information	  that	  both	  PRMs	  
and	  LDs	  should	  follow.	  The	  authors	  are	  fully	  aware	  that	  PRMs	  will	  have	  
additional	  information	  they	  want	  to	  supply	  and	  that	  different	  LDs	  have	  
information	  they	  weigh	  as	  equally	  important.	  It	  is	  a	  difficult	  task	  to	  
standardise	  what	  information	  is	  important.	  With	  having	  four	  industry	  
professionals	  and	  around	  10	  examples	  as	  a	  reference	  a	  large	  number	  of	  
essential	  information	  is	  identified.	  Of	  course	  the	  number	  of	  references	  could	  
be	  significantly	  increased,	  potentially	  exposing	  further	  properties	  that	  could	  
be	  essential.	  Ideally	  the	  industry	  actors	  themselves	  represented	  by	  an	  
official	  organization	  –	  IES	  (USA),	  CIE	  (EU)	  or	  DCL	  (DK)	  –	  should	  develop	  a	  
common	  standard.	  
	  
Based	  on	  the	  standard	  datasheet	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  create	  a	  standard	  Revit	  
lighting	  object.	  Revit	  objects	  are	  chosen	  because	  of	  Revit’s	  major	  impact	  on	  
BIM	  implementation	  in	  several	  countries,	  and	  are	  considered	  to	  be	  the	  most	  
used	  BIM	  supporting	  modelling	  tool	  (Hamil,	  2013).	  Louis	  Poulsen	  creates	  all	  
their	  products	  as	  Revit	  objects	  because	  it	  is	  their	  belief	  that	  this	  is	  what	  the	  
==PAGE:126==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  101 
industry	  wants22.	  However,	  creating	  Revit	  objects	  does	  not	  necessarily	  
mean	  this	  standard	  is	  exclusively	  used	  for	  Revit.	  By	  translating	  the	  Revit	  
properties	  into	  open-­‐source	  format	  IFC	  it	  makes	  the	  standard	  able	  to	  
interoperate	  between	  different	  software	  packages.	  It	  is	  therefore	  possible	  to	  
export	  Revit	  light	  fixture	  objects	  to	  IFC	  with	  all	  properties	  intact,	  as	  seen	  in	  
Appendix	  N.	  Some	  properties	  could	  not	  be	  described	  by	  IFC	  properties	  
simply	  because	  they	  are	  not	  created	  in	  the	  current	  database.	  A	  suggestion	  
for	  a	  further	  development	  of	  the	  IFC	  database	  is	  given	  in	  Appendix	  H.	  
	  
One	  of	  the	  core	  values	  of	  having	  this	  tool	  is	  pinpointing	  what	  information	  is	  
essential	  and	  what	  information	  is	  nice	  to	  have.	  So	  far	  it	  seems	  like	  PRMs	  
have	  tried	  to	  differentiate	  themselves	  from	  each	  other	  in	  the	  way	  they	  
create	  their	  product	  pages	  –	  the	  more	  information	  supplied	  the	  better.	  Also	  
in	  cases	  where	  unique	  information	  can	  be	  used	  as	  a	  selling	  point	  –	  for	  
instance	  a	  certain	  designer.	  However,	  for	  lighting	  professionals	  it	  is	  not	  
necessarily	  a	  good	  thing	  to	  have	  a	  large	  quantity	  of	  information	  provided.	  
All	  information	  could	  be	  accessible	  but	  the	  first	  layer	  should	  only	  contain	  
the	  most	  essential,	  which	  has	  been	  defined	  in	  this	  tool.	  Whether	  PRMs	  want	  
to	  follow	  this	  standard	  is	  unsure.	  It	  will	  still	  be	  of	  large	  value	  for	  
manufacturers	  to	  differentiate	  themselves	  and	  following	  a	  common	  
standard	  converts	  them	  from	  being	  unique	  to	  ‘just	  one	  of	  the	  others’.	  For	  all	  
PRMs	  to	  follow	  the	  standard	  requires	  national	  or	  international	  action	  as	  
described	  above.	  All	  PRMs	  leaded	  by	  the	  major	  firms	  should	  in	  
collaboration	  decide	  to	  take	  action	  and	  follow	  the	  standard.	  One	  just	  has	  to	  
convince	  the	  PRMs	  that	  they	  will	  benefit	  from	  following	  the	  standard	  and	  
make	  their	  products	  easily	  comparable	  with	  others.	  	  
5.5. SUMMARY 
Based	  on	  the	  tool	  development	  the	  following	  overall	  conclusions	  can	  be	  
made:	  
o Using	  a	  generic	  room	  classification	  system	  such	  as	  CCS	  it	  is	  possible	  
to	  identify	  the	  precise	  function	  of	  a	  space	  and	  thereby	  determine	  the	  
illumination	  requirements	  quickly.	  Linking	  CCS	  to	  a	  requirements	  
database	  could	  automatically	  generate	  a	  lighting	  requirement	  from	  
the	  CCS	  code	  provided	  thus	  supporting	  automation	  of	  IDM	  packages.	  	  	  
o By	  having	  a	  materials	  database	  with	  commonly	  used	  building	  
materials	  and	  their	  reflectances	  it	  is	  easier	  for	  architects	  to	  choose	  
exactly	  the	  material	  they	  prefer	  and	  provide	  an	  exact	  reflectance	  
value.	  That	  way,	  wrongly	  estimations	  or	  use	  of	  pessimistic	  default	  
reflectance	  values	  can	  be	  avoided	  and	  back-­‐and-­‐fourth	  
communication	  can	  be	  reduced.	  	  
o Creating	  standard	  datasheets	  of	  light	  fixture	  properties	  provides	  an	  
easy	  framework	  for	  lighting	  designers	  to	  work	  with	  and	  ensures	  that	  
product	  manufacturers	  provide	  the	  exact	  information	  needed	  about	  
their	  products.	  This	  standard	  set	  of	  properties	  can	  be	  adopted	  by	  
various	  programs	  but	  should	  still	  support	  interoperability	  by	  using	  
IFC	  properties.	  	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
22	  Survey:	  Kasper	  Hammer,	  Louis	  Poulsen

CASE STUDY: NIELS BOHR SCIENCE PARK // 6

6. 
CASE STUDY: NIELS BOHR SCIENCE PARK 
6.1. INTRODUCTION 
The	  following	  chapter	  will	  present	  a	  real-­‐life	  demonstration	  of	  the	  IDM	  
packages	  and	  tools	  developed	  in	  previous	  chapters.	  All	  demonstrations	  
presented	  in	  this	  chapter	  have	  been	  performed	  based	  on	  the	  Niels	  Bohr	  
Science	  Park	  (NBSP)	  in	  Copenhagen,	  which	  can	  be	  seen	  on	  Figure	  30.	  The	  
building	  is	  a	  new	  research	  facility	  designed	  by	  Christensen	  &	  Co	  Architects	  
(Christensen	  &	  Co,	  n.d.)	  and	  engineered	  by	  Ramboll.	  Construction	  of	  the	  
building	  has	  been	  on	  going	  since	  summer	  2014	  and	  expected	  to	  terminate	  
in	  the	  end	  of	  2016	  (University	  of	  Copenhagen,	  n.d.).	  The	  science	  park	  is	  
covering	  45,000	  m2	  of	  which	  are	  research	  facilities,	  lecture	  rooms,	  offices	  
and	  laboratories.	  It	  is	  a	  highly	  technical	  demanding	  building	  with	  large	  
focus	  on	  energy	  reduction	  as	  well	  as	  providing	  the	  newest	  research	  
technology.	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  30	  –	  Computer	  rendered	  picture	  showing	  the	  exterior	  of	  the	  main	  building.	  
The	  building	  complex	  is	  divided	  into	  two	  main	  buildings:	  BA1	  and	  BA2,	  
where	  BA2	  is	  the	  smallest	  building.	  This	  case	  demonstration	  will	  focus	  on	  
Building	  BA2	  as	  which	  is	  the	  smaller	  left	  building	  in	  Figure	  31.	  	  	  
	  
	  
Figure	  31	  -­‐	  Building	  BA2	  on	  the	  left	  and	  BA1	  on	  the	  right	  
==PAGE:130==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN

6.1.1. PROVIDED MATERIAL 
Since	  Ramboll	  were	  the	  head	  engineering	  company	  behind	  the	  NBSP	  BA2	  
building	  it	  has	  been	  very	  convenient	  to	  receive	  relevant	  material	  about	  this	  
building	  as	  seen	  on	  Table	  11.	  The	  whole	  building	  was	  designed	  in	  Revit	  with	  
a	  great	  focus	  on	  BIM.	  Tendering	  and	  quantitative	  measurements	  were	  all	  
performed	  based	  on	  a	  central	  3D	  model	  containing	  information.	  All	  received	  
3D	  models	  were	  at	  least	  LOD	  350	  so	  with	  more	  than	  significant	  
architectural	  detailing	  to	  perform	  lighting	  design	  processes.

6.1.2. CASE SCENARIOS 
Since	  this	  chapter	  concerns	  testing	  the	  developed	  IDM	  packages	  they	  will	  
have	  to	  be	  demonstrated	  individually,	  since	  they	  are	  situated	  in	  two	  
different	  life	  cycle	  stages.	  For	  further	  description	  of	  the	  two	  IDM	  packages	  
please	  refer	  to	  section	  4.3	  and	  4.4.	  	  	  
	  
For	  the	  sake	  of	  simplifying	  the	  case	  studies,	  a	  representative	  room	  in	  
building	  BA2	  will	  be	  selected	  and	  performed	  testing	  on.	  This	  room	  is	  an	  80	  
m2	  teaching	  room	  and	  located	  on	  the	  ground	  level.	  See	  3D	  location	  and	  plan	  
drawing	  on	  Figure	  32	  and	  Figure	  33.	  Even	  though	  only	  a	  single	  room	  is	  
selected,	  all	  steps	  in	  the	  two	  IDM	  packages	  will	  still	  be	  relevant	  and	  applied.	  
The	  activities	  in	  the	  IDMs	  are	  not	  size	  or	  complexity	  dependent.	  	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  32	  -­‐	  3D	  representation	  of	  the	  building	  with	  highlighted	  room	  where	  the	  case	  is	  carried	  out.

6.1.3. DOCUMENTATION 
A	  thorough	  description	  of	  all	  IDM	  activities	  related	  to	  the	  cases	  is	  provided	  
in	  this	  chapter.	  For	  each	  activity	  performed,	  there	  is	  a	  QR	  code	  linking	  to	  an	  
online	  video	  that	  displays	  the	  actual	  workflow.	  A	  QR	  scanner	  installed	  on	  a	  
smartphone	  or	  tablet	  is	  required	  to	  scan	  the	  code.	  Additionally	  a	  URL	  will	  
be	  provided.	  
	  
Screenshots	  and	  documentation	  of	  the	  workflows	  are	  available	  in	  Appendix	  
O.	  
	  
Some	  IDM	  activities	  will	  be	  rounded	  off	  with	  a	  table	  summarising	  
suggestions	  for	  improvements	  to	  make	  the	  working	  process	  or	  information	  
flow	  better.	  These	  comments	  are	  the	  author’s	  own	  suggestions	  based	  on	  
experiences	  made	  during	  the	  case	  study.	  	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:132==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  105 
6.2. CASE 1: IDM PACKAGE E 33 - 21 99 28 11  
Architectural	  lighting	  design	  in	  the	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage.	  At	  this	  
point,	  the	  lighting	  designer	  receives	  a	  lighting	  concept	  and	  chooses	  light	  
fixtures	  to	  suit	  the	  requirements	  in	  the	  concept.	  The	  choice	  and	  placement	  
of	  light	  fixtures	  has	  to	  comply	  with	  a	  number	  of	  requirements,	  such	  as	  
energy	  consumption	  and	  illumination	  levels.	  
6.2.1. ACTIVITY 1.1: IDENTIFY LIGHTING CRITERIA 
	  
	  
As	  a	  lighting	  designer	  one	  has	  to	  determine	  the	  requirements	  for	  lighting	  
before	  initiating	  the	  fixture	  selection	  and	  placement	  activities.	  In	  this	  case	  
study,	  the	  lighting	  criteria	  were	  provided	  by	  a	  lighting	  designer	  at	  Ramboll	  
who	  defined	  the	  requirements	  for	  lighting	  in	  an	  earlier	  life	  cycle	  stage.	  
Based	  on	  the	  lighting	  design	  brief,	  architectural	  vision	  and	  feasibility	  
documents,	  the	  following	  requirements	  for	  lighting	  were	  detected.	  	  
	  
The	  input	  requirements	  are	  based	  on	  the	  IRM	  1.1	  defined	  in	  subsection	  
4.3.4	  and	  Appendix	  E.	  An	  overview	  of	  possible	  improvements	  is	  visible	  in	  
Table	  12.	  
IRM 1.1.1 Space lighting 
Space	  function	  is	  provided	  through	  the	  3D	  model	  with	  assigned	  names	  and	  
numbers.	  The	  room	  number	  is	  01.0.I.086	  and	  it	  is	  believed	  that	  the	  
numbering	  system	  is	  Ramboll’s	  own;	  naming	  is	  plain	  text.	  Since	  the	  
analysed	  room	  is	  a	  teaching	  room	  this	  could	  be	  assigned	  the	  CCS	  class	  code	  
‘ADE’.	  	  
	  
All	  internal	  rooms	  in	  this	  building	  need	  artificial	  lighting.	  	  
	  
Illuminance	  requirements	  are	  described	  in	  the	  feasibility	  document	  but	  
references	  to	  the	  Danish	  standard	  DS700	  (Danish	  Standards,	  2005).	  Since	  
the	  feasibility	  document	  is	  from	  2012	  it	  is	  assumed	  that	  the	  requirements	  
followed	  the	  national	  standards	  at	  that	  time.	  If	  the	  project	  were	  made	  today	  
the	  lighting	  requirements	  would	  follow	  the	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  standard	  
(Danish	  Standards,	  2011),	  which	  is	  the	  national	  standard	  for	  buildings	  built	  
after	  2015.	  This	  case	  example	  has	  to	  represent	  a	  current	  time	  workflow,	  so	  
DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  requirements	  will	  be	  followed.	  In	  this	  standard	  the	  
illuminance	  requirements	  for	  a	  ‘teaching	  room’	  is	  300	  lux	  on	  the	  horizontal	  
work	  plane,	  if	  the	  room	  is	  used	  as	  a	  classroom	  for	  tutorials.	  This	  is	  assumed	  
to	  be	  the	  case	  here.	  Alternatively,	  one	  could	  use	  the	  CCS	  class	  code	  
illuminance	  requirement	  tool	  (as	  developed	  in	  Chapter	  5),	  which	  directly	  
show	  the	  same	  requirements	  of	  300	  lux	  for	  an	  ‘ADE’	  teaching	  room	  used	  for	  
tutorials.	  A	  work	  plane	  is	  set	  to	  be	  0.85	  m	  from	  the	  floor,	  which	  is	  a	  common	  
approximation	  and	  is	  a	  standard	  value	  in	  DS700.	  
==PAGE:133==
CASE 1: E 33 – 21 99 28 11 NIELS BOHR SCIENCE PARK // 6 
	  
106
IRM 1.1.2 Lighting system 
The	  energy	  frame	  for	  installed	  lighting	  is	  defined	  in	  the	  feasibility	  document	  
to	  be	  7.5	  W/m2	  of	  floor	  area	  at	  a	  lighting	  level	  of	  250	  lux.	  It	  is	  not	  precisely	  
defined	  what	  requirements	  are	  at	  300	  lux,	  however	  it	  is	  assumed	  to	  be	  the	  
same	  in	  this	  case.	  The	  goal	  is	  to	  design	  a	  lighting	  system	  below	  7.5	  W/m2.	  	  	  
IRM 1.1.3 Lighting fixtures 
It	  is	  aimed	  that	  light	  fixtures	  in	  the	  teaching	  room	  spread	  out	  the	  light	  
evenly	  hence	  directional	  source	  light	  fixtures	  is	  preferred.	  	  
	  
Light	  fixtures	  should	  be	  recessed	  in	  the	  ceiling,	  preferably	  in	  a	  ceiling	  
modular	  system,	  which	  is	  not	  yet	  designed	  at	  the	  point	  of	  feasibility	  
document	  release.	  	  
IRM 1.1.4 Light source 
It	  is	  specified	  in	  the	  feasibility	  documents	  that	  there	  is	  a	  requirement	  
towards	  the	  use	  of	  LED	  fixtures	  in	  the	  building	  since	  reduction	  of	  energy	  
consumption	  is	  of	  high	  priority.	  LED	  light	  source	  could	  be	  given	  CCS	  class	  
code	  EAC	  –	  ‘LED	  lamp’.	  	  
	  
General	  lighting	  and	  task	  lighting	  should	  be	  kept	  in	  white	  colours.	  There	  are	  
no	  specific	  requirements	  towards	  light	  colour	  temperature	  or	  colour	  
rendering	  index	  for	  this	  particular	  type	  of	  room.	  Only	  that	  it	  should	  be	  ‘high’	  
which	  is	  a	  very	  wide	  definition.	  Normally	  the	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  requires	  a	  CRI	  
>80	  for	  tutorial	  rooms.	  	  
	  
Table	  12	  -­‐	  IDM	  E	  33	  -­‐	  21	  99	  28	  11	  Activity	  1.1	  suggestions	  for	  improvements	  
ACTIVITY 1.1:  
SUGGESTIONS FOR IMPROVEMENTS 
Room	  naming	  
and	  illuminance	  
requirements.	  	  
Currently	  Ramboll	  uses	  their	  own	  room	  naming	  
system.	  By	  using	  CCS	  classification	  there	  would	  be	  no	  
need	  to	  double-­‐check	  a	  room	  schedule	  to	  determine	  
room	  functions	  and	  avoid	  misunderstandings	  of	  the	  
name.	  CCS	  could	  be	  linked	  to	  illuminance	  
requirements	  so	  that	  these	  values	  will	  automatically	  
be	  provided.	  Then	  lighting	  designers	  do	  not	  have	  to	  
guess	  the	  work	  function.	  	  
Light	  source	  
requirements	  
Light	  source	  type	  could	  be	  named	  using	  CCS	  class	  
codes.	  For	  that	  to	  work	  properly,	  more	  CCS	  codes	  for	  
more	  specific	  light	  sources	  should	  be	  developed.	  	  
6.2.2.  ACTIVITY 1.2: CREATE SIMULATION GEOMETRY 
	  
	  
A	  lighting	  simulation	  is	  performed	  in	  the	  software	  using	  a	  3D	  geometry	  
based	  on	  the	  architectural	  model.	  In	  this	  Ramboll	  case	  example	  the	  
architectural	  model	  provided	  was	  a	  full	  Revit	  3D	  model.	  This	  case	  thereby	  
==PAGE:134==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  107 
presents	  how	  a	  Revit	  model	  is	  prepared	  so	  that	  it	  can	  be	  used	  with	  the	  
lighting	  simulation	  software.	  
	  
The	  input	  requirements	  are	  based	  on	  the	  IRM	  1.2	  defined	  in	  subsection	  
4.3.4	  and	  available	  in	  Appendix	  E.	  A	  list	  of	  possible	  improvements	  is	  visible	  
in	  Table	  13.	  	  
	  
It	  should	  be	  pointed	  out	  that	  in	  case	  a	  3D	  model	  is	  not	  present,	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  would	  have	  to	  build	  the	  interior	  space	  manually	  from	  a	  2D	  plan	  
drawing,	  which	  is	  not	  covered	  in	  this	  case	  example.	  
IRM 1.2 Architectural model information 
All	  geometry	  was	  considered	  to	  be	  at	  least	  LOD	  200,	  showing	  all	  relevant	  
measurements.	  A	  suspended	  ceiling	  was	  drawn	  into	  the	  model	  showing	  a	  
room	  height	  of	  3	  m	  from	  floor	  to	  suspended	  ceiling.	  Columns	  were	  drawn	  as	  
well.	  	  
	  
All	  interior	  materials	  are	  provided.	  They	  are	  as	  following:	  
	  
o Walls:	  Gypsum	  board,	  white	  finish.	  
o Floors:	  Linoleum,	  grey	  finish.	  	  
o Ceiling:	  Gypsum	  board,	  white	  finish.	  	  
o Columns:	  Concrete,	  grey.	  	  
o Windows:	  Glass,	  70	  %	  transmittance.	  	  
ADD 1.2 Surface reflectance 
Since	  materials	  were	  provided	  they	  have	  to	  be	  translated	  to	  a	  reflectance	  
used	  in	  the	  lighting	  simulation	  software.	  Normally,	  the	  lighting	  designer	  will	  
choose	  default	  reflectances	  or	  use	  personal	  experience	  to	  evaluate	  
approximate	  values	  for	  the	  materials	  provided,	  hence	  guessing	  the	  exact	  
material	  the	  architect	  had	  in	  mind.	  In	  this	  case,	  the	  developed	  reflectance	  
tool	  is	  used	  which	  can	  make	  this	  process	  quicker.	  The	  architect	  could	  also	  
use	  it	  himself	  and	  then	  provide	  the	  following	  reflectances:	  
	  
o Walls:	  0.85	  (85	  %)	  
o Floors:	  0.15	  (15	  %)	  
o Ceiling:	  0.85	  (85	  %)	  
o Columns:	  0.4	  (40	  %)	  
o Windows:	  Glass,	  0.04	  (4%)	  
Workflow Step 1.2.1: Create space in Revit and export to .gbXML 
The	  workflow	  includes	  exporting	  spaces	  as	  .gbXML	  file	  format,	  which	  can	  be	  
imported	  in	  DIALux.	  A	  video	  showing	  the	  workflow	  is	  available	  through	  the	  
following	  options:	  	  
	  
http://youtu.be/ruy_URLlUPk

For	  a	  thorough	  description	  of	  this	  workflow,	  please	  see	  Appendix	  O.	  
Workflow Step 1.2.2: Import the model in DIALux and prepare for 
simulation 
DIALux	  has	  the	  ability	  to	  import	  .gbXML	  files.	  Import	  the	  previously	  saved	  
file	  and	  the	  geometry	  will	  be	  transferred	  from	  Revit.	  A	  video	  showing	  the	  
workflow	  is	  available	  through	  the	  following	  options:	  	  
	  
http://youtu.be/qqt1fvfXPrQ	  
	  
A	  thorough	  description	  of	  this	  workflow	  step	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  O.	  
	  
Table	  13	  -­‐	  IDM	  E	  33	  -­‐	  21	  99	  28	  11	  Activity	  1.2	  suggestions	  for	  improvements	  
ACTIVITY 1.2:  
SUGGESTIONS FOR IMPROVEMENTS 
Simplifying	  the	  
architectural	  
model	  
While	  already	  designing	  the	  3D	  model	  the	  architect	  
can	  create	  spaces	  and	  export	  relevant	  spaces	  as	  
gbXML.	  All	  spaces	  should	  have	  CCS	  class	  codes	  
assigned.	  This	  way,	  the	  architect	  can	  simply	  send	  
rooms	  to	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  perform	  
simulations	  without	  sending	  a	  complex	  3D	  model	  
that	  takes	  way	  too	  many	  man-­‐hours	  to	  prepare.	  	  
Revit	  space	  
export	  issues	  
Revit	  has	  to	  improve	  the	  interoperability	  with	  
DIALux	  and	  ensure	  that	  reflectances	  are	  transferred	  
properly.	  	  
Support	  export	  of	  windows	  and	  changing	  of	  surface	  
materials.	  At	  the	  moment	  the	  Revit	  space	  is	  exported	  
as	  vertical	  and	  horizontal	  continuous	  surfaces,	  
however	  if	  there	  are	  a	  change	  in	  materials	  the	  
surface	  will	  not	  be	  split	  in	  two.	  This	  can	  be	  a	  
problem,	  especially	  along	  windows	  where	  the	  
parapet	  is	  not	  exported	  properly	  since	  the	  window	  
and	  parapet	  is	  assumed	  to	  be	  one	  continuous	  
surface.	  	  
6.2.3. ACTIVITY 1.3: SELECT INITIAL LIGHT FIXTURES 
	  
	  
Real-­‐world	  light	  fixtures	  have	  to	  be	  selected	  and	  performed	  lighting	  
simulations	  with,	  unless	  an	  off-­‐the-­‐market	  unique	  fixture	  were	  developed	  
specifically	  for	  this	  project.	  Usually	  the	  light	  fixtures	  will	  be	  available	  for	  
==PAGE:136==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  109 
download	  as	  DIALux	  files.	  Product	  manufacturers	  have	  great	  interest	  in	  
having	  their	  products	  available	  in	  DIALux	  as	  this	  means	  people	  will	  choose	  
them	  in	  their	  design.	  
	  
The	  input	  requirements	  are	  based	  on	  the	  IRM	  1.3	  defined	  in	  subsection	  
4.3.4	  and	  Appendix	  E.	  
IRM 1.3 Photometric data 
The	  light	  fixture	  objects	  in	  DIALux	  contain	  photometric	  files	  used	  by	  the	  
program	  to	  calculate	  light	  distribution.	  In	  the	  rare	  case	  where	  an	  on-­‐the-­‐
market	  product	  is	  not	  available	  as	  a	  DIALux	  file,	  a	  product	  manufacturer	  
has	  to	  provide	  an	  .ies	  or	  .ldt	  photometric	  file.	  	  
Workflow Step 1.3.1: Select fixtures in DIALux 
The	  two	  videos	  below	  show	  the	  workflow	  of	  selecting	  fixtures	  in	  DIALux,	  
however	  they	  are	  combined	  with	  Activity	  1.4	  (place	  light	  fixtures	  in	  
simulation	  geometry)	  and	  Activity	  1.5	  (perform	  lighting	  simulation).	  There	  
are	  two	  videos,	  because	  for	  the	  sake	  of	  using	  this	  case	  study	  as	  an	  example	  
they	  will	  show	  iteration	  where	  the	  first	  fixture	  selection	  is	  not	  fulfilling	  the	  
requirements.	  Therefore	  the	  first	  video	  will	  show	  the	  wrong	  choice	  and	  the	  
second	  video	  will	  show	  the	  correct	  choice.	  They	  are	  accessible	  by	  using	  the	  
QR	  codes	  or	  URL	  addresses	  below:	  	      
First	  choice:	  
http://youtu.be/eUfZLc9SNoc	  
Second	  choice:	  
http://youtu.be/acX-­‐-­‐ShO3yM	  
	  
For	  a	  picture-­‐based	  walk-­‐through	  the	  workflow	  please	  see	  Appendix	  O.	  
	  
In	  this	  case	  study,	  a	  Philips	  recessed	  lighting	  fixture	  were	  chosen	  where	  all	  
photometric	  data	  was	  available.	  This	  light	  fixture	  was	  chosen	  based	  on	  its	  
linear	  shape,	  high	  lumen	  output	  and	  low	  wattage	  of	  24W	  due	  to	  its	  LED	  
light	  source.	  Information	  about	  this	  fixture	  is	  obtained	  on	  the	  product	  
manufacturers	  homepage	  and	  datasheets.	  It	  is	  a	  ‘standard’	  fixture	  and	  
therefore	  supposedly	  cheap.	  Normally	  a	  lighting	  designer	  would	  have	  a	  
large	  personal	  experience	  to	  choose	  light	  fixtures	  from	  to	  perform	  the	  first	  
iteration.

6.2.4. ACTIVITY 1.4: PLACE FIXTURES IN SIMULATION GEOMETRY 
	  
	  
After	  selection	  of	  fixtures	  they	  will	  have	  to	  be	  placed	  in	  the	  3D	  geometry	  
modelled	  in	  DIALux.	  Usually	  the	  light	  fixture	  products	  will	  have	  geometric	  
dimensions	  making	  it	  possible	  to	  evaluate	  the	  actual	  fitting	  on	  the	  surface.	  	  
	  
Due	  to	  the	  requirements	  in	  the	  feasibility	  documents	  and	  lighting	  concept	  
the	  light	  fixtures	  has	  to	  be	  placed	  in	  the	  ceiling.	  Uniformly	  distributed	  light	  
has	  been	  prioritised	  as	  well,	  why	  placing	  the	  fixtures	  in	  a	  uniform	  grid	  
would	  be	  preferable.	  	  
	  
A	  video	  of	  this	  workflow	  is	  available	  in	  Activity	  1.3.	  A	  picture-­‐based	  
walkthrough	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  O.	  
6.2.5. ACTIVITY 1.5: PERFORM LIGHTING SIMULATION 
	  
	  
Running	  a	  simulation	  is	  the	  next	  step	  after	  placing	  the	  fixtures.	  If	  everything	  
is	  set	  properly	  the	  last	  step	  is	  to	  push	  the	  start-­‐button.	  The	  gateway	  from	  
the	  IDM	  package	  is	  included	  in	  this	  activity.	  A	  video	  of	  this	  is	  available	  in	  
Activity	  1.3.	  A	  picture-­‐based	  walkthrough	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  O.	  
6.2.6. ACTIVITY 2.1: EXPORT LIGHT FIXTURES TO ARCH MODEL 
	  
	  
Once	  the	  lighting	  design	  is	  finished	  and	  satisfactory	  the	  selection	  and	  
placement	  of	  fixtures	  has	  to	  be	  provided	  to	  either	  the	  architect	  responsible	  
or	  technical	  drawer.	  Also	  technical	  data	  about	  the	  light	  fixtures	  needs	  to	  be	  
communicated	  for	  an	  electrical	  engineer	  or	  lighting	  designer	  in	  a	  later	  life	  
cycle	  stage	  to	  continue	  the	  lighting	  design	  detailing	  work.	  	  
	  
Following	  IDM	  package	  E-­‐33	  21	  99	  28	  11	  structure	  the	  output	  
requirements	  are	  based	  on	  the	  ORM	  2.1	  defined	  in	  subsection	  4.3.4	  and	  
available	  in	  Appendix	  E.	  Filled	  out	  output	  requirement	  model	  is	  available	  in	  
Appendix	  P.	  
	  
A	  list	  of	  possible	  improvements	  is	  visible	  in	  Table	  14.

The	  placement	  of	  fixtures	  look	  like	  on	  Figure	  34	  after	  exporting	  from	  
DIALux:	  	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  34	  -­‐	  Placement	  of	  fixtures	  in	  the	  architectural	  model	  
ORM 2.1.1 Space lighting 
Space	  lighting	  information	  requires	  a	  value	  of	  illumination	  levels	  in	  the	  
room	  or	  building.	  In	  this	  case,	  the	  illumination	  levels	  were	  calculated	  to	  
have	  a	  maintained	  illuminance	  of	  303	  lux	  on	  the	  working	  surface.	  	  
ORM 2.1.2 Lighting system 
The	  installed	  energy	  consumption	  for	  lighting	  in	  this	  room	  is	  4.28	  W/m2.	  	  
	  
A	  3D	  model	  from	  DIALux	  will	  be	  exported	  to	  the	  architect	  as	  a	  DWG	  file	  so	  
that	  placement	  and	  size	  of	  light	  fixtures	  is	  clear.	  The	  geometric	  information	  
is	  at	  least	  LOD200.	  See	  workflow	  steps	  on	  export	  of	  geometry	  from	  DIALux	  
for	  further	  information.	  	  
ORM 2.1.3 Lighting fixtures 
Each	  light	  fixture	  gets	  its	  own	  project	  reference	  tag,	  which	  in	  this	  case	  
would	  be	  project-­‐specific.	  The	  selected	  Philips	  recessed	  fixture	  is	  in	  this	  
case	  example	  assigned	  the	  label	  ‘UAC01’	  (CCS	  lighting	  fixture,	  type	  1).	  	  
	  
The	  manufacturer	  is	  Philips.	  Model	  name	  is	  Smartform	  LED	  BBS415.	  
Product	  manufacturer	  specific	  model	  number	  is	  LED24/840	  W15L120	  LIN-­‐
PC-­‐N.	  With	  these	  numbers	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  obtain	  additional	  information.	  	  
	  
Light	  fixture	  placing	  is	  the	  ceiling.	  Light	  fixture	  mounting	  is	  recessed.	  
	  
Light	  fixture	  wattage	  is	  24	  W	  each.	  	  
ORM 2.1.4 Light source 
Lamp	  type	  used	  in	  this	  lighting	  fixture	  is	  LED.	  The	  luminous	  flux	  is	  2000	  
lumen.	  These	  are	  the	  basic	  information	  extracted	  from	  the	  DIALux	  objects.	  	  
==PAGE:139==
CASE 1: E 33 – 21 99 28 11 NIELS BOHR SCIENCE PARK // 6 
	  
112
More	  specific	  information	  such	  as	  the	  following	  is	  only	  available	  if	  an	  
additional	  datasheet	  is	  obtained.	  Light	  colour	  appearance	  is	  white.	  Colour	  
temperature	  is	  4000	  kelvin.	  Colour	  rendering	  index	  is	  above	  80.	  	  
	  
Electrical	  information	  about	  the	  fixture	  includes	  voltage,	  which	  is	  220-­‐240	  
Volt.	  The	  light	  source	  is	  dimmable	  with	  DALI	  interface.	  	  
Workflow Step 2.1.1: Export light fixtures to arch model 
The	  finished	  placement	  of	  fixtures	  should	  be	  delivered	  to	  the	  architect	  or	  
technical	  drawer	  as	  part	  of	  the	  output	  requirement	  model.	  In	  this	  case	  
example	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  export	  the	  light	  fixture	  placement	  from	  DIALux	  to	  
DWG	  file	  format,	  which	  can	  be	  placed	  back	  in	  to	  the	  Revit	  architectural	  
model.	  	  A	  video	  showing	  this	  workflow	  is	  available	  by	  scanning	  the	  QR	  code	  
or	  using	  the	  URL	  address	  below:	  	  
	  
http://youtu.be/piz-­‐_vXarl8	  
	  
A	  walkthrough	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  O	  where	  one	  can	  see	  the	  final	  
placement	  of	  fixtures.	  	  
	  
Table	  14	  -­‐	  IDM	  E	  33	  -­‐	  21	  99	  28	  11	  Activity	  2.1	  suggestions	  for	  improvements	  
ACTIVITY 2.1:  
SUGGESTIONS FOR IMPROVEMENTS 
Presenting	  
illuminance	  
levels	  
Standardisation	  on	  how	  illuminance	  levels	  should	  be	  
communicated:	  whether	  a	  simple	  Em	  value	  is	  
sufficient	  or	  if	  either	  calculation	  grid-­‐values	  or	  iso-­‐
lux	  diagrams	  should	  be	  added.	  	  
Light	  fixture	  
reference	  
number	  
Project	  reference	  number	  for	  a	  lighting	  fixture	  is	  still	  
on	  a	  project-­‐specific	  state.	  It	  could	  be	  interesting	  to	  
experiment	  with	  universal	  identification	  labels	  that	  
tells	  about	  the	  type	  of	  fixture	  and	  in	  which	  room	  that	  
fixture	  is	  placed	  directly	  from	  the	  identification	  tag.	  	  
DIALux	  
information	  
Obtaining	  all	  relevant	  information	  in	  this	  ORM	  is	  not	  
directly	  available	  in	  DIALux	  so	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  look	  
up	  the	  product	  manufacturer	  datasheet.	  
Standardisation	  of	  information	  embedded	  in	  DIALux	  
would	  be	  a	  step	  in	  the	  right	  direction.	  	  
Revit	  and	  
DIALux	  
interoperability	  
It	  could	  be	  very	  useful	  if	  there	  happened	  to	  be	  an	  
interoperability	  feature	  between	  DIALux	  and	  Revit	  
that	  allowed	  DIALux	  to	  export	  light	  fixtures	  as	  Revit	  
objects	  instead	  of	  a	  DWG	  drawing	  file.	  This	  way	  one	  
could	  easily	  insert	  light	  fixture	  in	  Revit	  and	  obtain	  
relevant	  information	  regardless	  of	  a	  separate	  
datasheet.

6.3. CASE 2: IDM PACKAGE G 33 - 21 99 28 11 
This	  package	  concerns	  architectural	  lighting	  design	  in	  the	  coordinated	  
design	  stage.	  This	  is	  where	  the	  previous	  selection	  of	  fixtures	  has	  to	  be	  
further	  defined	  to	  suit	  control	  concepts	  and	  proper	  placement	  in	  the	  
architectural	  geometry.	  This	  is	  a	  process	  that	  required	  close	  collaboration	  
with	  an	  electrical	  engineer.	  Only	  the	  work	  of	  a	  lighting	  designer	  will	  be	  
demonstrated	  in	  this	  case	  study.	  
6.3.1. ACTIVITY 1.1: DEFINE CONTROL ZONES 
	  
	  
Before	  defining	  control	  zones	  the	  lighting	  designer	  has	  to	  determine	  where	  
controlling	  is	  necessary.	  This	  usually	  includes	  analysing	  the	  occupancy	  
patterns	  and	  availability	  of	  daylight,	  hence	  knowing	  the	  architectural	  space	  
geometry	  is	  important.	  	  
	  
The	  input	  requirements	  are	  based	  on	  the	  IRM	  1.1	  defined	  in	  subsection	  
4.4.4	  and	  Appendix	  G.	  A	  list	  of	  possible	  improvements	  is	  visible	  in	  Table	  15.	  
IRM 1.1 Architectural space 
Space	  function	  is	  provided	  through	  the	  3D	  Revit	  model	  with	  assigned	  
names	  and	  numbers.	  The	  room	  label	  tells	  that	  it	  is	  a	  ‘teaching	  room’.	  
Alternatively	  it	  could	  have	  been	  assigned	  the	  CCS	  class	  code	  ‘ADE’.	  The	  
geometry	  of	  the	  model	  is	  at	  least	  LOD	  300	  showing	  all	  relevant	  internal	  
geometry,	  such	  as	  ceiling	  height	  and	  fixed	  furniture.	  Also	  the	  height	  above	  
the	  suspended	  ceiling	  is	  provided	  being	  0.5	  m.	  	  	  
	  
Occupancy	  time	  per	  day	  is	  not	  provided	  directly	  from	  the	  feasibility	  
documents.	  However,	  the	  building	  is	  an	  office/study	  building	  with	  fixed	  
opening	  hours	  from	  8	  to	  17,	  Monday	  to	  Friday.	  The	  teaching	  room	  will	  be	  
occupied	  with	  30-­‐60	  students	  each	  day	  but	  a	  varying	  time	  slots	  and	  
definitely	  not	  occupied	  continuously	  throughout	  the	  opening	  hours.	  If	  it	  
were	  an	  office,	  it	  would	  be	  easier	  to	  assume	  a	  more	  fixed	  occupancy	  pattern.	  
This	  calls	  for	  a	  flexible	  light	  control	  system	  so	  that	  light	  is	  not	  turned	  on	  all	  
day	  long.	  	  
	  
Daylight	  factor	  is	  provided	  in	  the	  feasibility	  documents	  stating	  a	  daylight	  
factor	  of	  >2	  in	  the	  middle	  of	  the	  room,	  and	  >1	  in	  the	  rest.	  Hence	  it	  is	  
possible	  to	  control	  lighting	  due	  to	  daylight	  contribution.	  	  
	  
The	  final	  control	  characteristics	  for	  this	  room	  would	  then	  be:	  	  
	  
Function:	  	  
Teaching	  room	  	  
Occupancy:	  	   Mixed	  use	  during	  8-­‐17,	  Mon-­‐Fri	  
Daylight:	  
Daylight	  contribution,	  DF>2	  
==PAGE:141==
