ACTIVITY 1.1:  
SUGGESTIONS FOR IMPROVEMENTS 
Generic	  control	  
zone	  type	  
It	  would	  be	  a	  possibility	  to	  include	  information	  about	  
the	  control	  zone	  in	  a	  room	  type	  classification	  such	  as	  
CCS.	  For	  example,	  the	  code	  could	  tell	  the	  occupancy	  
pattern	  and	  if	  there	  is	  daylight	  contribution	  in	  the	  
room.	  	  
6.3.2. ACTIVITY 1.2: DEFINE CONTROLLING CONCEPT 
	  
	  
Defining	  the	  controlling	  concept	  is	  using	  the	  zone	  found	  in	  Activity	  1.1	  and	  
then	  assigning	  a	  schedule	  on	  how	  lighting	  in	  this	  room	  should	  be	  controlled.	  
With	  the	  knowledge	  about	  the	  preliminary	  design	  developed	  in	  IDM	  E-­‐33	  
21	  99	  28	  11	  the	  control	  concept	  can	  be	  tailored	  to	  fit	  that	  initial	  selection	  of	  
fixtures.	  	  
	  
The	  input	  requirements	  are	  based	  on	  the	  IRM	  1.2	  defined	  in	  subsection	  
4.4.4	  and	  available	  in	  Appendix	  G.	  
IRM 1.2 Preliminary lighting design 
From	  the	  previous	  IDM	  package	  a	  long	  list	  of	  properties	  were	  provided	  
telling	  about	  the	  selection	  and	  placement	  of	  light	  fixtures.	  This	  list	  is	  
obtainable	  in	  subsection	  6.2.6	  and	  Appendix	  P	  and	  not	  necessary	  to	  
duplicate	  here.	  	  
	  
However,	  the	  information	  especially	  necessary	  to	  know	  about	  the	  fixtures	  
in	  regard	  to	  controlling	  is	  its	  voltage	  and	  dimming	  signal.	  The	  Philips	  
BGS415	  light	  fixture	  selected	  use	  220-­‐240	  V	  and	  the	  LED	  light	  source	  is	  
dimmable	  with	  DALI	  interface.	  From	  the	  control	  zone	  information	  there	  is	  
information	  about	  availability	  of	  daylight	  and	  a	  need	  to	  support	  a	  flexible	  
user	  pattern.	  	  
	  
This	  ends	  up	  with	  the	  following	  requirements	  towards	  a	  lighting	  control	  in	  
the	  selected	  control	  zone:	  
	  
During	  working	  hours	  (8-­‐17):	  
o Manual	  push	  buttons	  to	  turn	  light	  on/off.	  Light	  turns	  on	  to	  the	  level	  
defined	  by	  the	  BMS/BAS	  system.	  	  
o Dimming	  uses	  DALI	  interface,	  centrally	  controlled	  by	  the	  BMS/BAS	  
system	  but	  with	  manual	  override	  possibility.	  	  	  
o Signals	  to	  the	  BMS/BAS	  system	  comes	  from	  daylight	  sensors	  so	  light	  
is	  dimmed	  according	  to	  daylight	  availability	  and	  with	  the	  goal	  of	  
maintaining	  a	  light	  level	  of	  300	  lx	  on	  workspace.	  	  
o PIR	  detectors	  sends	  signal	  to	  BMS/BAS	  to	  automatically	  turn	  of	  light	  
if	  people	  are	  not	  present.	  
==PAGE:142==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  115 
After	  working	  hours	  (17-­‐8):	  
o Manual	  push	  buttons	  to	  turn	  light	  on/off.	  Light	  turns	  on	  to	  a	  
predefined	  level,	  which	  is	  adequate	  to	  deliver	  cleaning	  light.	  	  	  
o Manual	  override	  is	  possible	  so	  light	  can	  be	  dimmed	  0-­‐100	  %	  if	  
necessary.	  	  
o PIR	  detectors	  turn	  off	  light	  automatically	  if	  people	  are	  not	  present.	  	  
	  
A	  diagram	  showing	  the	  control	  concept	  and	  interaction	  between	  the	  
controlling	  components	  can	  be	  seen	  on	  Figure	  35.	  
	  
	  
Figure	  35	  -­‐	  Control	  concept	  diagram	  for	  Case	  2	  
6.3.3. ACTIVITY 1.3: RE-EVALUATE LIGHT FIXTURES 
	  
	  
The	  initial	  light	  fixture	  selection	  has	  to	  support	  the	  developed	  controlling	  
concept.	  Additional	  technical	  data	  about	  the	  light	  fixture	  can	  be	  obtained	  
from	  the	  product	  manufacturer’s	  website,	  however	  in	  this	  case	  adequate	  
information	  is	  available.	  Nothing	  in	  the	  selection	  of	  Philips	  BGS415	  LED	  
light	  fixture	  prevents	  it	  from	  being	  controlled	  like	  defined	  in	  Activity	  1.2.	  
The	  initial	  fixture	  selection	  is	  therefore	  still	  relevant.	  	  
	  
Another	  factor	  that	  potentially	  could	  complicate	  the	  fixture	  selection	  is	  its	  
recession	  height	  and	  space	  above	  the	  ceiling.	  In	  this	  case	  the	  Philips	  fixture	  
has	  a	  height	  of	  75	  mm	  and	  an	  extra	  75	  mm	  for	  recession	  clearance.	  A	  total	  of	  
150	  mm	  from	  the	  bottom	  of	  the	  ceiling	  is	  acceptable,	  taken	  the	  500	  mm	  
space	  above	  the	  ceiling	  into	  account.	  	  
At	  this	  point	  there	  are	  no	  further	  issues	  that	  require	  re-­‐evaluation	  of	  fixture	  
placement,	  since	  the	  fixtures	  are	  not	  changed	  and	  there	  is	  no	  major	  change	  
in	  the	  architectural	  model.	  	  
==PAGE:143==

116
6.3.4. ACTIVITY 1.4: VERIFY LIGHTING CONDITIONS 
	  
	  
Since	  no	  changes	  have	  been	  made	  there	  is	  no	  need	  to	  perform	  a	  verifying	  
lighting	  simulation.	  	  
6.3.5. ACTIVITY 1.5: SURFACE COORDINATION 
	  
	  
The	  next	  large	  step	  is	  to	  coordinate	  the	  light	  fixture	  placement	  with	  other	  
engineering	  disciplines.	  In	  this	  case	  the	  fixtures	  are	  recessed	  in	  the	  ceiling	  
hence	  other	  components	  present	  in	  the	  ceiling	  could	  be	  an	  issue.	  Primarily	  
an	  electrical	  engineer	  that	  has	  the	  overview	  and	  interaction	  with	  other	  
disciplines	  performs	  this	  activity.	  Even	  though,	  this	  activity	  will	  be	  
performed	  based	  on	  other	  components	  visible	  in	  the	  architectural	  model	  for	  
the	  sake	  of	  this	  case	  study	  example.	  	  
	  
In	  this	  case,	  the	  ceiling	  in	  which	  lighting	  fixtures	  are	  placed	  has	  been	  
updated	  with	  new	  information.	  Six	  ventilation	  inlets	  are	  present	  in	  the	  
ceiling	  and	  the	  whole	  ceiling	  is	  built	  up	  by	  a	  modular	  structure	  that	  requires	  
the	  lighting	  fixtures	  to	  fit	  into	  those.	  All	  this	  new	  information	  indicates	  
clashes	  in	  the	  ceiling	  forcing	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  redo	  their	  work	  and	  go	  
back	  to	  Activity	  1.3	  hence	  the	  process	  map	  gateway.	  	  
6.3.6. ACTIVITY 1.3: RE-EVALUATE LIGHT FIXTURE SELECTION AND 
PLACEMENT (REPEATED) 
	  
	  
Due	  to	  clashes,	  the	  light	  fixture	  selection	  and	  placement	  has	  to	  be	  re-­‐
evaluated.	  In	  this	  case	  it	  is	  assumed	  that	  changing	  the	  placement	  of	  the	  
fixtures	  will	  solve	  the	  clash	  problem	  and	  still	  deliver	  adequate	  lighting	  
without	  having	  to	  re-­‐select	  fixtures.	  
	  
In	  order	  to	  place	  the	  fixtures	  correctly	  a	  detailed	  drawing	  of	  the	  ceiling	  has	  
to	  be	  presented.	  Due	  to	  interoperability	  reasons	  between	  Revit	  and	  DIALux	  
the	  easiest	  way	  is	  to	  provide	  a	  2D	  plan	  of	  the	  ceiling	  based	  on	  the	  
architectural	  3D	  model.	  Fortunately	  Revit	  makes	  it	  possible	  to	  export	  the	  
ceiling	  plan	  as	  a	  DWG	  drawing,	  which	  can	  be	  imported	  into	  DIALux.	  Then	  all	  
fixtures	  can	  be	  moved	  to	  appropriate	  places.	  	  
	  
A	  list	  of	  possible	  improvements	  for	  this	  activity	  is	  visible	  in	  Table	  16.	  
==PAGE:144==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  117 
Workflow Step 1.3.1: Export ceiling plan and change the lighting layout 
A	  workflow	  showing	  how	  to	  create	  a	  plan	  drawing	  and	  then	  import	  it	  into	  
the	  DIALux	  model	  is	  available	  through	  the	  following	  options.	  This	  workflow	  
is	  applicable	  for	  all	  2D	  surfaces	  in	  a	  Revit	  model.	  	  
	  
Two	  videos,	  showing	  the	  workflow	  of	  exporting	  a	  ceiling	  plan	  and	  
afterwards	  changing	  the	  fixture	  layout	  is	  available	  by	  scanning	  the	  QR	  codes

Table	  16	  -­‐	  IDM	  G	  33	  -­‐	  21	  99	  28	  11	  Activity	  1.3	  suggestions	  for	  improvements	  
ACTIVITY 1.3:  
SUGGESTIONS FOR IMPROVEMENTS 
DWG	  import	  in	  
DIALux	  
DIALux	  does	  not	  support	  AutoCAD	  hatches	  when	  
importing	  DWG	  files.	  This	  creates	  an	  extra	  step	  in	  the	  
process	  where	  one	  has	  to	  open	  the	  DWG	  file	  in	  
AutoCAD	  and	  remove	  hatches	  if	  present.	  This	  step	  
could	  easily	  be	  avoided	  if	  DIALux	  supported	  hatches.	  
Linking	  Revit	  
and	  DIALux	  
A	  more	  flowing	  interaction	  between	  Revit	  and	  
DIALux	  would	  be	  preferable.	  Exporting	  2D	  plans	  to	  
and	  from	  Revit	  is	  fine	  but	  still	  a	  manual	  process	  that	  
has	  to	  be	  repeated	  every	  time	  updates	  occur.	  It	  
would	  be	  very	  beneficial	  if	  one	  could	  link	  the	  Revit	  
3D	  model	  in	  DIALux	  or	  even	  just	  a	  selected	  part	  of	  
the	  larger	  3D	  model	  as	  external	  reference.	  
6.3.7. ACTIVITY 1.4: VERIFY LIGHTING CONDITIONS (REPEATED) 
	  
	  
Since	  the	  fixtures	  has	  been	  moved	  a	  new	  simulation	  have	  to	  be	  performed	  
in	  order	  to	  ensure	  lighting	  requirements	  are	  met.	  For	  this	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  needs	  to	  know	  if	  materials	  in	  the	  architectural	  model	  are	  changed	  
hence	  influencing	  the	  reflectances.	  	  
	  
The	  input	  requirements	  are	  based	  on	  the	  IRM	  1.4	  defined	  in	  subsection	  
4.4.4	  and	  available	  in	  Appendix	  G.	  
==PAGE:145==
CASE 2: G 33 – 21 99 28 11 NIELS BOHR SCIENCE PARK // 6 
	  
118
IRM 1.4 Simulation data 
This	  input	  requirement	  model	  includes	  information	  about	  ceiling,	  floor	  and	  
wall	  materials.	  The	  materials	  remain	  unchanged	  since	  the	  initial	  simulation	  
and	  therefore	  the	  reflectances	  will	  not	  be	  influenced.	  If	  materials	  are	  
changed	  then	  new	  reflectances	  has	  to	  be	  estimated.	  Again	  the	  developed	  
reflectance	  tool	  can	  be	  used	  for	  this	  purpose.	  	  
Workflow Step 1.4.1: Perform lighting simulation 
The	  workflow	  video	  showing	  how	  the	  simulation	  is	  being	  performed	  can	  be	  
obtained	  in	  Activity	  1.3.	  A	  walkthrough	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  O.	  
6.3.8. ACTIVITY 1.5 (repeated): SURFACE COORDINATION 
	  
	  
No	  relevant	  updates	  are	  detected	  and	  the	  process	  can	  proceed	  to	  the	  final	  
activity.	  	  
6.3.9. ACTIVITY 2.1: EXPORT LIGHT FIXTURES TO ARCH MODEL 
	  
	  
When	  the	  placement	  and	  selection	  is	  finalised	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  proceed	  and	  
communicate	  the	  results	  to	  relevant	  actors.	  The	  architect	  would	  need	  the	  
finalised	  placement	  of	  fixtures	  exported	  into	  their	  model	  and	  the	  engineer	  
responsible	  for	  tendering	  (usually	  the	  electrical	  engineer)	  need	  documents	  
that	  specifies	  the	  lighting	  solution.	  When	  specifying	  the	  lighting	  fixture	  it	  is	  
important	  to	  do	  it	  as	  detailed	  as	  possible	  without	  mentioning	  any	  
manufacturer	  specific	  information.	  Especially	  in	  public	  or	  EU	  funded	  
projects	  it	  is	  not	  allowed	  to	  refer	  to	  specific	  manufacturers.	  In	  theory	  it	  
should	  be	  possible	  to	  make	  a	  lighting	  design	  following	  the	  general	  
specifications	  that	  delivers	  the	  same	  light	  as	  the	  fixtures	  selected	  by	  the	  
lighting	  designer.	  	  
	  
Following	  IDM	  package	  G	  33	  -­‐	  21	  99	  28	  11	  structure	  the	  output	  
requirements	  are	  based	  on	  the	  ORM	  2.1	  defined	  in	  subsection	  4.4.4	  and	  
available	  in	  Appendix	  G.	  Filled	  out	  output	  requirement	  model	  is	  available	  in	  
Appendix	  Q.	  All	  specifications	  are	  information	  obtained	  from	  Philips.	  	  
	  
A	  list	  of	  possible	  improvements	  for	  this	  activity	  is	  visible	  in	  Table	  17	  
	  
The	  placement	  of	  fixtures	  look	  like	  on	  Figure	  36	  after	  exporting	  from	  
DIALux.

ORM 2.1.1 Space lighting 
Space	  lighting	  information	  requires	  a	  value	  of	  illumination	  levels	  in	  the	  
room	  or	  building.	  In	  this	  case,	  the	  illumination	  levels	  were	  calculated	  to	  
have	  a	  maintained	  illuminance	  of	  324	  lux	  on	  the	  working	  surface.	  	  
ORM 2.1.2 Lighting system 
The	  installed	  energy	  consumption	  for	  lighting	  in	  this	  room	  is	  4.28	  W/m2.	  	  
	  
A	  3D	  model	  that	  includes	  the	  precise	  placement	  and	  amount	  of	  fixtures	  will	  
be	  exported	  from	  DIALux.	  The	  geometric	  information	  is	  at	  least	  LOD200.	  
See	  later	  workflow	  steps	  on	  export	  of	  geometry	  from	  DIALux	  for	  further	  
information	  on	  this.	  	  
ORM 2.1.3 Lighting fixtures 
Each	  light	  fixture	  gets	  its	  own	  project	  reference	  tag,	  which	  in	  this	  case	  
would	  be	  project-­‐specific.	  The	  selected	  recessed	  fixture	  is	  in	  this	  case	  
example	  assigned	  the	  label	  ‘UAC01’	  (CCS	  Light	  fixture	  type	  1).	  	  
	  
The	  geometry	  of	  each	  fixture	  is	  followed	  in	  the	  architectural	  model	  that	  
clearly	  specifies	  the	  outer	  dimensions.	  The	  geometric	  model	  is	  at	  least	  LOD	  
300.	  	  
	  
Fixture	  type	  is	  directional	  since	  the	  light	  is	  evenly	  distributed	  from	  a	  light	  
emitting	  surface	  hence	  the	  rectangular	  shape.	  The	  light	  fixture	  placing	  and	  
mounting	  is	  recessed	  in	  the	  ceiling.	  	  
	  
Light	  fixture	  wattage	  is	  24	  W	  each.	  	  
	  
Beam	  width	  angle	  is	  80o	  but	  is	  actually	  not	  relevant	  for	  this	  fixture	  type,	  as	  
it	  is	  a	  directional	  fixture.	  	  
Ingress	  protection	  code	  (IP)	  is	  IP40,	  meaning	  it	  is	  protected	  against	  building	  
dust,	  screws	  and	  wires,	  but	  not	  protected	  against	  water.	  Vandal	  resistance	  
rating	  (IK)	  is	  IK02	  meaning	  it	  can	  withstand	  an	  impact	  of	  0.2	  joule.	  	  
==PAGE:147==
CASE 2: G 33 – 21 99 28 11 NIELS BOHR SCIENCE PARK // 6 
	  
120
Body	  material	  is	  zinc-­‐coated	  steel	  with	  white	  finish.	  	  	  
	  
Optical	  covering	  is	  a	  linear	  lens	  array	  optic	  in	  polycarbonate	  cover.	  	  
ORM 2.1.4 Light source 
Lamp	  type	  used	  in	  this	  lighting	  fixture	  is	  LED	  hence	  CCS	  Class	  code	  ‘EAC’.	  
The	  luminous	  flux	  is	  2000	  lumen.	  	  
	  
Light	  colour	  appearance	  is	  white	  with	  colour	  temperature	  of	  4000	  kelvin.	  
Colour	  rendering	  index	  is	  above	  80.	  	  
	  
The	  service	  life	  for	  this	  light	  source	  is	  specified	  as	  L80	  after	  50,000	  hours	  
meaning	  the	  initial	  light	  output	  will	  be	  reduced	  to	  80	  %	  after	  this	  time.	  	  
	  
The	  electrical	  ballast	  to	  control	  this	  light	  source	  is	  specified	  as	  a	  built-­‐in	  
driver.	  Voltage	  is	  220-­‐240	  V.	  Dimming	  is	  possible	  with	  DALI	  interface.	  	  	  
Workflow Step 2.1.1: Export light fixtures to arch model 
The	  finished	  placement	  of	  fixtures	  should	  be	  delivered	  to	  the	  architect	  or	  
technical	  drawer	  as	  part	  of	  the	  output	  requirement	  model.	  In	  this	  case	  
example	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  export	  the	  light	  fixture	  placement	  from	  DIALux	  to	  
DWG	  file	  format,	  which	  can	  be	  placed	  back	  in	  to	  the	  Revit	  architectural	  
model.	  	  
	  
A	  video	  showing	  this	  workflow	  is	  available	  by	  using	  the	  QR	  code	  or	  URL	  
address	  below:	  	  
	  
http://youtu.be/vbjAxyG-­‐ww4	  
	  
The	  workflow	  is	  available	  in	  Appendix	  O.	  
	  
Table	  17	  -­‐	  IDM	  G	  33	  -­‐	  21	  99	  28	  11	  Activity	  2.1	  suggestions	  for	  improvements	  
ACTIVITY 2.1:  
SUGGESTIONS FOR IMPROVEMENTS 
Export	  light	  
fixtures	  from	  
DIALux	  
A	  great	  need	  to	  export	  light	  fixtures	  from	  DIALux	  as	  
actual	  objects	  in	  Revit	  where	  a	  large	  amount	  of	  
information	  can	  be	  embedded.	  At	  the	  moment	  the	  
fixture	  placement	  is	  just	  a	  DWG	  reference	  with	  no	  
option	  to	  assign	  properties.	  If	  the	  3D	  model	  is	  to	  be	  
used	  for	  tendering,	  the	  architect	  is	  supposed	  to	  
insert	  custom	  light	  fixture	  objects	  using	  the	  DWG	  
layout	  as	  a	  underlay.	  Then	  properties	  can	  be	  
assigned	  to	  these	  Revit	  fixtures.

FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN

6.4. CASE STUDY DISCUSSION 
IDM Packages 
After	  testing	  out	  the	  IDM	  package	  processes	  on	  a	  real	  life	  case	  it	  is	  generally	  
observed	  that	  the	  process	  workflows	  work	  as	  expected.	  The	  lighting	  
concept	  delivered	  by	  the	  Ramboll	  engineers	  and	  used	  in	  Case	  1,	  were	  quite	  
loosely	  described.	  Information	  about	  what	  type	  of	  fixtures	  or	  where	  to	  
mount	  them	  was	  missing	  and	  assumptions	  had	  to	  be	  made.	  Information	  
could	  be	  missing	  due	  to	  two	  reasons:	  either	  the	  architects/lighting	  designer	  
had	  no	  preferences,	  or	  it	  was	  simply	  not	  decided	  at	  the	  time	  the	  documents	  
were	  written.	  If	  it	  is	  the	  last,	  it	  clearly	  points	  out	  that	  there	  is	  a	  need	  to	  
standardise	  information	  exchange	  and	  when	  certain	  information	  should	  be	  
decided.	  The	  fixtures	  ended	  up	  being	  linear	  recessed	  ceiling	  fixtures.	  
Looking	  at	  the	  final	  project	  drawings	  from	  the	  architects	  it	  seems	  like	  they	  
eventually	  went	  with	  the	  same	  solution.	  	  	  
Apart	  from	  this	  assumption	  there	  are	  no	  large	  interruptions	  or	  insecurities	  
during	  the	  first	  case.	  Once	  the	  selection	  of	  fixtures	  is	  in	  place	  the	  
architectural	  geometry	  has	  to	  be	  prepared.	  This	  is	  a	  simple	  process	  due	  to	  
space	  export	  from	  Revit	  to	  DIALux,	  which	  works	  fine.	  Placing	  the	  fixtures	  is	  
easy	  because	  one	  can	  use	  a	  standard	  grid-­‐feature	  in	  DIALux.	  The	  total	  case	  
took	  little	  over	  30	  minutes	  to	  carry	  out.	  Of	  course	  this	  will	  in	  other	  cases	  
depend	  on	  the	  complexity	  and	  scale	  of	  the	  task	  and	  number	  of	  iterations	  
required.	  	  
	  
Case	  2	  is	  a	  bit	  more	  unclear	  especially	  activity	  1.5,	  which	  is	  normally	  not	  
intended	  for	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  perform.	  In	  this	  case	  study	  it	  is	  
performed	  by	  the	  authors	  of	  this	  report,	  but	  with	  limited	  information	  about	  
the	  ceiling	  structure.	  Information	  about	  the	  ceiling	  is	  entirely	  based	  upon	  
the	  received	  architectural	  material.	  In	  reality	  the	  electrical	  engineer	  or	  
whoever	  performs	  the	  surface	  coordination	  would	  have	  much	  more	  
knowledge	  about	  this.	  Therefore,	  the	  ceiling	  coordination	  would	  in	  practice	  
be	  much	  more	  complex	  than	  how	  it	  is	  displayed	  in	  this	  case.	  How	  many	  
man-­‐hours	  used	  to	  coordinate	  this	  ceiling	  is	  unknown	  but	  definitely	  a	  lot	  
more.	  	  
	  
In	  general	  both	  cases	  has	  been	  performed	  based	  on	  received	  information	  
that	  includes	  a	  final	  architectural	  model.	  It	  has	  been	  difficult	  to	  decide	  the	  
difference	  in	  information	  level	  between	  the	  received	  models	  and	  how	  these	  
models	  would	  look	  like	  at	  the	  respective	  life	  cycle	  stages	  of	  the	  IDM	  
packages.	  Would	  information	  about	  materials	  or	  ceiling	  height	  even	  be	  
available	  in	  the	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage?	  Or	  would	  placement	  of	  
ventilation	  inlet	  be	  decided	  in	  the	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  at	  the	  time	  of	  
activity	  1.5?	  One	  could	  argue,	  that	  testing	  of	  information	  exchange	  is	  not	  
representative	  for	  this	  case	  study,	  as	  it	  is	  easy	  to	  perform	  a	  design	  when	  all	  
information	  is	  obtainable.	  However,	  the	  core	  purpose	  of	  testing	  the	  IDM	  
packages	  is	  not	  to	  decide	  what	  to	  do	  if	  information	  is	  not	  obtainable	  –	  but	  
rather	  demonstrate	  how	  the	  IDM	  packages	  are	  performed	  when	  all	  
information	  is	  available.	  Besides,	  it	  is	  a	  prerequisite	  for	  the	  IDM	  packages	  to	  
initiate	  that	  all	  IRMs	  are	  fulfilled.	  	  
==PAGE:149==
CASE STUDY // 6 
	  
122
Use of developed tools 
All	  three	  tools	  developed	  in	  Chapter	  5	  have	  been	  used	  at	  some	  point.	  In	  
general	  they	  all	  work	  like	  expected,	  thus	  fulfilling	  the	  hypothesis	  that	  they	  
can	  be	  helpful	  in	  lighting	  design	  processes.	  	  
	  
When	  naming	  spaces	  using	  CCS	  classification	  one	  can	  quickly	  identify	  the	  
function	  of	  the	  room.	  By	  having	  the	  CCS	  room	  tag	  it	  is	  easy	  to	  look	  up	  the	  
illuminance	  requirements	  in	  the	  separate	  tool	  that	  gives	  information	  based	  
on	  national	  standards.	  This	  reduces	  time	  otherwise	  used	  to	  identify	  a	  room,	  
and	  then	  evaluate	  which	  room	  name	  in	  DS/EN	  12464-­‐1	  to	  use	  and	  finally	  
find	  the	  illumination	  requirements.	  The	  potential	  to	  make	  this	  process	  even	  
more	  automatic	  is	  large,	  as	  this	  database	  could	  be	  linked	  to	  Revit	  (or	  
another	  BIM-­‐supporting	  software)	  that	  could	  automatically	  recall	  this	  lux-­‐
value	  when	  a	  CCS	  class	  code	  is	  assigned.	  	  
	  
Reflection	  tool	  is	  used	  when	  translating	  materials	  found	  in	  the	  architectural	  
model	  to	  reflectances	  used	  in	  the	  lighting	  simulation	  software.	  This	  is	  again	  
simplifying	  the	  process	  as	  the	  tool	  gathers	  information	  from	  a	  wide	  range	  of	  
sources	  and	  announces	  the	  average	  value.	  Instead	  of	  looking	  through	  all	  
these	  sources	  manually	  the	  tool	  provides	  it	  with	  one	  input.	  This	  tool	  has	  a	  
large	  potential	  as	  this	  could	  easily	  be	  made	  more	  open-­‐source-­‐minded	  thus	  
allowing	  other	  lighting	  professionals	  to	  give	  their	  input	  on	  materials.	  When	  
using	  this	  tool	  on	  the	  materials	  chosen,	  it	  also	  gives	  a	  higher	  reflectance	  
than	  if	  default	  wall	  values	  were	  chosen.	  One	  could	  argue	  that	  by	  choosing	  
more	  realistic	  values	  the	  simulation	  is	  optimistic	  and	  will	  not	  allow	  
deviations.	  If	  default	  values	  were	  chosen	  they	  would	  be	  pessimistic	  but	  at	  
the	  other	  hand	  allow	  for	  unexpected	  changes.	  It	  is	  however	  still	  the	  belief	  of	  
the	  authors	  that	  choosing	  realistic	  scenarios	  will	  always	  be	  the	  most	  
representative.	  	  
	  
Lastly	  the	  standard	  datasheet	  has	  been	  chosen	  to	  define	  properties	  in	  the	  
last	  ORM	  2.1	  in	  Case	  2.	  All	  information	  were	  obtainable	  on	  the	  product	  
manufacturer’s	  website	  and	  a	  Revit	  lighting	  object	  were	  edited	  to	  include	  all	  
information	  from	  the	  standard	  datasheet.	  That	  way	  it	  will	  be	  easier	  to	  
crosscheck	  Revit	  objects	  with	  the	  requirements	  from	  a	  lighting	  designer.	  	  
Interoperability 
Interoperability	  between	  Revit	  and	  DIALux	  works	  surprisingly	  well.	  The	  
ability	  to	  simplify	  the	  model	  and	  export	  spaces	  using	  space	  boundaries	  
makes	  it	  possible	  to	  make	  simulations	  on	  single	  rooms.	  However,	  there	  are	  
small	  issues	  with	  for	  instance	  the	  assigned	  reflectances	  in	  Revit	  not	  being	  
transferred	  properly.	  Also	  there	  could	  be	  some	  implications	  with	  more	  
complex	  geometry	  and	  fixed	  objects	  in	  a	  space	  not	  being	  exported	  good	  
enough.	  	  
	  
Interoperability	  between	  DIALux	  and	  Revit	  works	  fine	  but	  there	  are	  some	  
issues	  when	  it	  comes	  to	  exporting	  the	  lighting	  objects	  from	  DIALux.	  What	  
DIALux	  provides,	  is	  a	  geometric	  model	  that	  shows	  the	  placement	  of	  the	  light	  
fixtures	  and	  accurate	  dimensions	  in	  the	  form	  of	  3D	  DWG	  model.	  The	  objects	  
==PAGE:150==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  123 
are	  purely	  geometric	  and	  contain	  no	  properties.	  All	  information	  about	  the	  
fixtures	  has	  to	  be	  provided	  as	  an	  attached	  document,	  which	  is	  not	  a	  very	  
automatic	  or	  BIM-­‐oriented	  process.	  If	  DIALux	  were	  able	  to	  export	  Revit	  
objects	  or	  as	  simple	  .3DS	  objects	  with	  embedded	  information,	  it	  would	  be	  
much	  easier	  to	  transfer	  information	  between	  those	  two	  programs.	  	  
	  
This	  is	  also	  the	  main	  reason	  why	  this	  case	  study	  ends	  with	  exporting	  the	  
light	  fixtures	  as	  geometry	  representatives	  with	  attached	  datasheet	  
containing	  requirements.	  If	  actual	  light	  objects	  should	  be	  inserted	  in	  Revit,	  
they	  would	  either	  need	  to	  include	  product	  manufacturer	  specific	  
information,	  which	  is	  not	  allowed	  in	  ORM2.1.	  Or	  one	  would	  have	  to	  create	  a	  
custom	  no-­‐named	  light	  fixture	  with	  the	  same	  geometric	  representation	  as	  
the	  one	  exported	  from	  DIALux,	  which	  would	  be	  unnecessary	  time	  
consuming.	  A	  geometric	  representation	  and	  technical	  requirements	  are	  
therefore	  considered	  sufficient	  for	  the	  tendering	  process	  at	  this	  stage:	  the	  
contractors	  know	  the	  geometry	  and	  technical	  requirements	  to	  bid	  on.	  
	  
In	  Appendix	  N	  it	  is	  demonstrated	  how	  a	  Revit	  light	  fixture	  is	  exported	  
containing	  all	  relevant	  IFC	  properties	  thus	  supporting	  interoperability	  
between	  software	  platforms.	  	  
6.5. SUMMARY 
Based	  on	  the	  case	  study	  of	  Niels	  Bohr	  Science	  Park	  the	  following	  overall	  
conclusions	  can	  be	  made:	  
o Performing	  a	  preliminary	  lighting	  simulation	  is	  quite	  
straightforward	  if	  all	  relevant	  information	  is	  present.	  Selecting	  
lighting	  fixtures	  will	  be	  the	  most	  iterative	  part	  as	  many	  factors	  plays	  
a	  role.	  	  
o Some	  information	  was	  not	  clear	  and	  had	  to	  be	  assumed.	  This	  
includes	  the	  space	  function	  and	  surface	  material	  reflectances.	  Using	  
the	  developed	  tools	  from	  chapter	  5	  helped	  gathering	  this	  
information.	  	  
o A	  surface	  coordination	  is	  a	  complex	  process	  that	  requires	  knowledge	  
about	  components	  installed	  on	  the	  surface	  –	  in	  this	  case	  study	  a	  
ceiling.	  The	  architectural	  drawings	  only	  showed	  ventilation	  outlets	  
why	  this	  was	  the	  only	  clashes	  appearing.	  	  
o It	  was	  possible	  to	  gather	  all	  information	  about	  the	  selected	  light	  
fixture	  as	  stated	  in	  ORM	  2.1	  and	  the	  standard	  datasheet	  proposal	  as	  
developed	  in	  chapter	  5.

DISCUSSION // 7 
	  
124
7. 
DISCUSSION 
Working	  with	  this	  project	  revealed	  how	  lighting	  design	  processes	  can	  be	  
described	  and	  defined	  by	  using	  IDM	  packages.	  These	  packages	  consist	  of	  
several	  parts	  and	  this	  study	  especially	  gave	  the	  impression	  that	  process	  
maps	  and	  exchange	  requirements	  are	  the	  most	  essential	  and	  complex	  parts	  
of	  the	  IDM	  package.	  They	  will	  mainly	  be	  covered	  in	  the	  following	  discussion.	  	  
	  
Process	  maps	  are	  used	  to	  describe	  a	  project	  discipline’s	  business	  process	  
using	  a	  generic	  notation	  system.	  The	  process	  map	  covers	  a	  specific	  life	  cycle	  
stage.	  In	  this	  study	  the	  two	  PMs	  covered	  architectural	  lighting	  design	  in	  
outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  and	  coordinated	  design	  life	  cycle	  stages	  
respectively.	  The	  idea	  with	  mapping	  business	  processes	  in	  an	  easy-­‐to-­‐
understand	  and	  industry-­‐acknowledged	  notation	  clearly	  communicates	  
what	  activities	  are	  needed	  to	  complete	  a	  process	  and	  the	  flow	  of	  
information	  exchanged	  between	  activities.	  
	  
What	  this	  study	  clarified	  was	  the	  challenge	  it	  was	  to	  map	  down	  a	  rather	  
complex	  business	  process,	  which	  lighting	  design	  usually	  is,	  into	  a	  simple,	  
linear	  structure.	  Some	  industry	  experts	  expressed	  uncertainty	  of	  having	  all	  
of	  their	  work	  compressed	  into	  a	  simple	  sequence	  of	  activities.	  Especially	  in	  
spite	  of	  a	  lighting	  designer’s	  nature	  of	  creative	  working	  process.	  Based	  on	  
the	  industry	  professional’s	  opinions	  and	  the	  author’s	  own	  discoveries,	  it	  
seems	  more	  difficult	  to	  map	  down	  creative	  working	  processes	  rather	  than	  
more	  linear,	  repetitive	  processes.	  For	  instance,	  developing	  a	  lighting	  
concept	  or	  selecting	  which	  fixtures	  to	  suit	  this	  concept	  is	  more	  difficult	  to	  
map	  down,	  than	  simulating	  lighting	  conditions	  in	  a	  space	  and	  export	  the	  
results.	  The	  latter	  is	  a	  more	  straightforward	  process	  and	  influenced	  by	  
standard	  procedures.	  Some	  lighting	  application	  projects	  would	  also	  be	  more	  
‘standard-­‐oriented’	  than	  other.	  For	  example	  office	  lighting	  design	  would	  be	  
more	  straightforward	  than	  doing	  lighting	  design	  in	  a	  recreational	  space	  
with	  different	  functions.	  Even	  though	  creative	  working	  processes	  might	  be	  
more	  difficult	  to	  map	  down	  it	  is	  still	  possible	  and	  highly	  relevant.	  
	  
So	  how	  can	  lighting	  designers	  benefit	  from	  using	  PMs?	  First,	  PMs	  display	  
the	  working	  processes	  clearly	  and	  can	  potentially	  help	  identify	  wasted	  work	  
or	  working	  procedures	  that	  can	  be	  optimised.	  This	  study’s	  development	  of	  
IDM	  packages	  and	  later	  testing	  in	  the	  case	  study	  exposed	  issues	  with	  some	  
of	  the	  activities.	  Especially	  the	  ceiling	  coordination	  activity	  and	  the	  large	  
iteration	  related	  to	  that.	  In	  this	  particular	  example	  it	  seems	  unnecessary	  
that	  a	  lighting	  designer	  delivers	  information	  to	  an	  electrical	  engineer	  with	  
the	  risk	  of	  getting	  a	  yes/no	  answer	  back	  and	  perform	  the	  process	  all	  over.	  
By	  having	  simultaneous	  communication	  with	  the	  surface	  coordination	  actor	  
or	  even	  design	  directly	  in	  a	  central	  3D	  model,	  this	  iteration	  can	  eventually	  
be	  reduced	  remarkably.	  IDMs	  can	  thereby	  help	  identify	  a	  problem	  where	  
BIM	  could	  be	  the	  potential	  solution;	  i.e.	  letting	  the	  lighting	  designer	  being	  
updated	  through	  a	  central	  BIM	  model	  and	  always	  aware	  of	  where	  to	  place	  
the	  fixtures.	  	  
	  
==PAGE:154==
DISCUSSION // 7 
	  125 
Information	  exchange	  standardisation	  is	  another	  IDM	  package	  aspect	  which	  
lighting	  designers	  can	  benefit	  from.	  By	  mapping	  down	  activities	  in	  the	  PM	  
and	  identifying	  relevant	  information	  exchanges	  and	  when	  they	  occur,	  the	  
information	  flow	  can	  be	  more	  streamlined	  and	  effective.	  In	  theory	  this	  limit	  
time	  lost	  while	  waiting	  for	  information	  or	  being	  unsure	  from	  whom	  to	  get	  it.	  	  
	  
Several	  of	  the	  surveyed	  industry	  experts	  expressed	  a	  need	  for	  standardising	  
information	  exchanged	  between	  actors.	  Surveyed	  experts	  from	  the	  industry	  
included	  lighting	  designers,	  an	  electrical	  engineer	  and	  a	  product	  
manufacturer,	  and	  they	  all	  agreed	  that	  the	  lighting	  industry	  could	  
potentially	  benefit	  from	  this.	  Lighting	  designers	  know	  what	  information	  
they	  get	  and	  what	  information	  to	  provide.	  By	  using	  common	  languages	  such	  
as	  CCS,	  LOD	  and	  IFC	  when	  exchanging	  information,	  no	  misunderstandings	  
about	  what	  data	  or	  object	  properties	  are	  required	  will	  occur.	  Using	  an	  open-­‐
source	  framework	  for	  data	  exchange	  also	  supports	  interoperability	  
between	  platforms,	  which	  is	  essential	  when	  information	  are	  used	  by	  many	  
actors	  and	  in	  different	  companies	  or	  organisations.	  
	  
During	  the	  IDM	  package	  development	  some	  information	  flows	  were	  
identified	  as	  being	  ineffective	  or	  un-­‐structured.	  This	  led	  the	  authors	  to	  
optimise	  the	  workflow	  and	  find	  possibilities	  to	  streamline	  information	  
exchange.	  As	  a	  result	  of	  this,	  three	  tools	  were	  developed	  that	  attempt	  to	  
solve	  separate	  problems.	  Figure	  37	  illustrates	  the	  areas	  of	  impact	  where	  
these	  tools	  would	  streamline	  information	  exchange.	  	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
Figure	  37	  -­‐	  Areas	  of	  miscommunication	  and	  inconsistent	  information	  exchange	  (left)	  that	  
implementation	  of	  the	  three	  tools	  (right)	  tries	  to	  streamline	  
First	  tool	  solves	  information	  consistency	  when	  providing	  room	  functions	  
and	  then	  assign	  related	  illumination	  requirements	  so	  that	  the	  lighting	  
designer	  can	  quickly	  initiate	  his	  work.	  The	  second	  tool	  aims	  towards	  
eliminating	  the	  inaccurate	  materials	  information	  exchange	  between	  
==PAGE:155==
DISCUSSION // 7 
	  
126
architect	  and	  lighting	  designer,	  that	  results	  in	  the	  lighting	  designer	  guessing	  
the	  materials	  and	  assigning	  assumed	  reflectance	  values.	  Third	  tool	  is	  
standardising	  information	  provided	  from	  product	  manufacturers	  to	  suit	  the	  
exact	  needs	  of	  lighting	  designers.	  	  
	  
All	  these	  tools	  were	  developed	  in	  belief	  that	  they	  would	  simplify	  processes	  
and	  make	  lighting	  designer’s	  work	  more	  efficient.	  This	  hypothesis	  was	  
further	  supported	  in	  the	  case	  study,	  which	  showed	  that	  having	  all	  the	  
correct	  information	  in	  place	  and	  ready	  when	  needed	  makes	  it	  possible	  to	  
create	  a	  lighting	  design	  relatively	  quickly.	  The	  time	  gained	  by	  avoiding	  
unnecessary	  dialogues	  back	  and	  forth	  with	  other	  actors	  can	  then	  be	  
reallocated	  for	  other	  purposes.	  Either	  used	  on	  optimising	  the	  design	  even	  
further	  or	  spent	  on	  more	  counselling	  for	  the	  client.	  	  
	  
Based	  on	  feedback	  from	  industry	  experts	  during	  this	  study	  it	  has	  been	  the	  
author’s	  impression	  that	  process	  mapping	  has	  been	  less	  positively	  met	  than	  
standardisation	  of	  information	  exchange.	  It	  is	  considered,	  that	  
implementation	  of	  IDM	  packages	  in	  the	  lighting	  industry	  will	  face	  a	  large	  
challenge,	  if	  process	  maps	  are	  met	  with	  criticism	  and	  accused	  of	  trying	  to	  
simplify	  and	  streamline	  their	  iterative	  and	  circular	  work.	  Some	  lighting	  
designers	  might	  feel	  their	  creativity	  being	  restricted	  thus	  distancing	  the	  
lighting	  designers	  even	  further	  away	  from	  the	  present	  use	  of	  digital	  
working	  methods.	  This	  would	  be	  disturbing,	  as	  this	  will	  only	  create	  a	  larger	  
gap	  between	  lighting	  designers	  and	  other	  engineering	  disciplines	  
(especially	  electrical	  engineers),	  which	  is	  best	  off	  avoided	  if	  integrated	  
design	  and	  interdisciplinary	  is	  the	  future	  of	  AEC	  projects.	  	  
	  
Therefore	  it	  is	  essential	  to	  communicate	  the	  IDM	  process	  map	  correctly	  to	  
creative	  actors,	  hereunder	  lighting	  designers.	  The	  point	  of	  focus	  has	  to	  lie	  
on	  the	  process	  map’s	  ability	  to	  display	  the	  overall	  road	  from	  A	  to	  B	  and	  not	  
necessarily	  dictate	  the	  work	  performed	  in	  between	  activities	  or	  the	  actual	  
activities	  itself.	  One	  has	  to	  see	  the	  process	  as	  a	  whole	  where	  as	  many	  
iterations	  are	  allowed	  but	  the	  overall	  sequential	  flow	  should	  be	  followed.	  
IDMs	  must	  not	  evolve	  to	  be	  used	  as	  a	  dictation	  tool	  with	  associated	  
responsibility;	  e.g.	  that	  process	  actors	  can	  be	  responsible	  and	  meet	  
consequences	  if	  not	  following	  the	  process	  map	  sequence	  even	  though	  
deliverables	  are	  met.	  However,	  by	  having	  a	  grade	  of	  flexibility	  within	  the	  
process	  map	  but	  still	  obeying	  the	  deliverables	  it	  is	  more	  than	  possible	  to	  
communicate	  the	  use	  of	  IDM	  packages	  in	  the	  lighting	  design	  discipline.	  	  
	  
Another	  point	  where	  the	  lighting	  industry	  could	  benefit	  from	  IDMs	  would	  
be	  to	  automatize	  some	  of	  the	  activities	  within	  a	  lighting	  design	  process.	  It	  is	  
a	  potentially	  direct	  enhancement	  of	  mapping	  down	  the	  activities	  in	  a	  
sequential	  order	  that	  an	  intelligent	  interface	  could	  run	  this	  sequence	  
automatically.	  Using	  this	  study’s	  developed	  tools	  support	  this	  idea	  even	  
further,	  by	  reducing	  situations	  with	  human	  evaluation	  and	  interaction	  and	  
replacing	  them	  with	  information	  generating	  and	  conversion	  tools.	  Some	  
activities	  in	  the	  process	  maps	  still	  require	  human	  interaction	  and	  
evaluation,	  such	  as	  selecting	  and	  placing	  fixtures.	  Letting	  computer	  
==PAGE:156==
DISCUSSION // 7 
	  127 
software	  automatically	  run	  iterations	  and	  calculate	  the	  most	  optimal	  
lighting	  layout	  from	  the	  IRMs	  specified	  in	  this	  study	  could	  possibly	  
automatize	  standard	  lighting	  design	  processes	  without	  a	  particular	  design	  
objective.	  	  
	  
Another	  large	  benefit	  of	  mapping	  working	  processes	  is	  to	  use	  IDMs	  as	  a	  
management	  tool.	  For	  the	  project	  manager	  it	  clarifies	  the	  activities	  
performed	  in	  the	  process	  and	  by	  time	  and	  resource	  estimating	  the	  single	  
activities	  it	  will	  be	  possible	  to	  plan	  out	  a	  project	  more	  accurately.	  Also	  by	  
dividing	  the	  process	  into	  activities	  with	  different	  exchange	  requirements	  it	  
is	  possible	  to	  use	  location	  based	  scheduling	  and	  thereby	  potentially	  
increase	  productivity	  and	  efficiency	  in	  the	  project.	  By	  making	  the	  activities	  
and	  specific	  deliverables	  more	  transparent	  it	  could	  also	  be	  a	  possibility	  to	  
optimise	  overall	  project	  milestone	  deliverables.	  The	  overall	  project	  
deliverable’s	  level	  of	  detail	  could	  be	  more	  specified	  by	  breaking	  down	  
deliverables	  in	  smaller	  disciplines	  rather	  than	  using	  level	  of	  detail	  on	  
project	  level.	  For	  example,	  a	  lighting	  layout	  could	  be	  specified	  with	  a	  higher	  
level	  of	  detail	  than	  what	  is	  sufficient	  for	  electrical	  circuits	  at	  the	  same	  
milestone,	  why	  the	  electrical	  engineers	  does	  not	  have	  comply	  with	  higher	  
detail	  than	  necessary.	  By	  defining	  specific	  level	  of	  detail	  for	  each	  deliverable	  
instead	  of	  overall	  process-­‐based	  it	  is	  therefore	  possible	  to	  make	  resource	  
allocation	  more	  efficient	  by	  allocating	  resources	  exactly	  where	  needed.	  	  
	  
Use	  of	  BIM	  in	  lighting	  design	  is	  currently	  very	  limited	  as	  lighting	  designers	  
tend	  to	  work	  on	  other	  platforms	  than	  where	  the	  usual	  central	  model	  is	  
placed.	  However,	  with	  the	  great	  potential	  of	  having	  a	  central	  model	  with	  all	  
relevant	  information	  embedded	  it	  is	  only	  a	  matter	  of	  time	  or	  generation	  
renewal	  before	  BIM	  is	  a	  natural	  part	  of	  a	  lighting	  designer’s	  work	  just	  like	  it	  
is	  with	  other	  building	  installation	  disciplines.	  Of	  course	  the	  practical	  design	  
part	  where	  the	  lighting	  designer	  tests	  out	  solutions	  by	  creating	  physical	  
mock-­‐ups	  or	  hand	  drawings	  is	  still	  an	  essential	  part	  and	  cannot	  be	  replaced	  
with	  BIM.	  But	  the	  age	  where	  lighting	  fixtures	  where	  represented	  by	  2D	  
drawings	  and	  followed	  by	  a	  large	  set	  of	  datasheets	  are	  gone.	  3D	  objects	  will	  
in	  the	  future	  contain	  enough	  information	  for	  the	  lighting	  designer	  to	  work	  
with	  them	  –	  hopefully	  standardised	  by	  using	  the	  IDM	  packages	  developed	  in	  
this	  study.

CONCLUSION
==PAGE:158==
==PAGE:159==
CONCLUSION // 8 
	  
	  
128
8. 
CONCLUSION 
The	  objective	  with	  this	  study	  was	  to	  answer	  the	  following	  problem:	  
	  
How	  can	  architectural	  lighting	  design	  discipline	  in	  outline	  conceptual	  and	  
coordinated	  design	  stages	  benefit	  from	  using	  IDM	  packages	  to	  streamline	  and	  
simplify	  working	  processes	  and	  information	  exchange?	  	  
By	  developing	  IDM	  packages,	  is	  it	  possible	  to	  optimise	  current	  workflows	  and	  
support	  implementation	  of	  BIM?	  
	  
The	  method	  towards	  developing	  IDM	  packages	  was	  to	  carry	  out	  a	  thorough	  
literature	  review	  and	  interviews	  of	  selected	  industry	  experts	  about	  current	  
working	  methods	  and	  information	  exchanges.	  It	  was	  strived	  to	  create	  
simple	  IDM	  packages	  using	  the	  buildingSMART	  format	  that	  would	  describe	  
the	  relevant	  business	  scenario	  where	  the	  IDM	  package	  is	  placed,	  illustrate	  
the	  working	  process	  clearly,	  and	  clarify	  affiliated	  information	  exchange	  
related	  to	  lighting	  designers’	  working	  processes.	  
	  
By	  analysing	  the	  working	  processes	  and	  information	  exchanges	  it	  was	  
possible	  to	  develop	  two	  IDM	  packages	  describing	  lighting	  design	  discipline	  
in	  outline	  conceptual	  design	  stage	  and	  coordinated	  design	  stage	  
respectively.	  	  
	  
Although	  the	  industry	  experts	  had	  different	  working	  approaches	  it	  quickly	  
became	  evident	  that	  they	  basically	  needed	  and	  delivered	  the	  same	  
information.	  The	  IDM	  process	  maps	  simplify	  the	  lighting	  design	  processes	  
to	  the	  most	  essential	  activities	  and	  displays	  the	  overall	  flow	  direction	  of	  the	  
process.	  Some	  of	  the	  lighting	  designers’	  most	  creative	  working	  approaches,	  
showed	  to	  be	  more	  difficult	  to	  standardise	  and	  simplify	  to	  a	  linear	  process,	  
than	  more	  straightforward	  processes	  with	  standard	  procedures	  such	  as	  
lighting	  simulations.	  	  
	  
Through	  the	  IDM	  packages,	  the	  necessary	  information	  exchange	  to	  initiate	  
activities	  or	  deliver	  from	  activities	  is	  identified.	  This	  identification	  is	  useful	  
for	  standardising	  information	  flow	  but	  also	  to	  suggest	  improvements	  that	  
can	  optimise	  how	  information	  is	  obtained.	  Developing	  three	  different	  tools	  
solves	  current	  issues	  where	  information	  is	  either	  inconsistent	  or	  where	  
inefficient	  communications	  occurs.	  The	  three	  developed	  tools	  streamline	  
information	  about	  space	  function	  and	  affiliated	  illumination	  requirements,	  
surface	  materials	  and	  their	  reflectances,	  and	  light	  fixture	  properties	  
provided	  by	  product	  manufacturers.	  	  
	  
The	  IDM	  packages	  were	  tested	  on	  a	  real-­‐life	  AEC	  project	  where	  both	  
architectural	  lighting	  design	  processes	  were	  demonstrated.	  The	  case	  study	  
showed	  that	  the	  packages	  are	  adaptable	  on	  a	  real	  world	  case	  and	  a	  
complete	  lighting	  design	  from	  concept	  to	  finished	  ceiling	  plan	  is	  possible	  to	  
accomplish	  by	  following	  the	  IDM	  package	  activities.	  	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:160==
FUTURE OF IDM IN BIM AND LIGHTING DESIGN	  
	  129 
The	  three	  tools	  are	  applicable	  and	  simplify	  the	  access	  to	  information	  and	  
streamline	  the	  information	  format	  to	  support	  software	  platform	  or	  
organisational	  independency.	  	  
	  
It	  encourages	  to	  transparency	  and	  consistency	  in	  the	  lighting	  design	  
industry	  by	  mapping	  activities	  in	  a	  process	  map	  and	  standardise	  
information	  exchanged	  between	  them.	  The	  IDM	  package	  development	  and	  
case	  study	  revealed	  that	  lighting	  designers’	  working	  processes	  can	  most	  
likely	  be	  improved	  by	  having	  a	  central	  BIM	  model,	  which	  will	  ease	  the	  work	  
of	  lighting	  designers	  and	  make	  their	  activities	  more	  efficient.	  Having	  a	  
central	  BIM	  model	  and	  IDM	  methodology	  makes	  it	  possible	  for	  other	  
engineering	  disciplines	  to	  communicate	  and	  interact	  with	  the	  lighting	  
design	  process	  as	  well	  as	  giving	  a	  project	  manager	  a	  basis	  to	  plan	  out	  
resource	  allocation	  and	  time	  management.	  	  
	  
By	  developing	  the	  two	  IDM	  packages	  and	  describing	  the	  lighting	  design	  
disciplines	  in	  the	  two	  respective	  life	  cycle	  stages,	  it	  identifies	  necessary	  
work	  flow	  and	  information	  exchange,	  which	  will	  eventually	  make	  it	  easier	  
to	  implement	  the	  use	  of	  BIM	  in	  lighting	  designers’	  work	  and	  help	  utilise	  the	  
potential	  of	  BIM.

BIBLIOGRAPHY

==PAGE:163==
GLOSSARY 
	  
	  
130
9. 
GLOSSARY 
	  
	  
.3ds	  
File	  format	  natively	  used	  by	  Autodesk	  3DS	  Max.	  It	  is	  used	  as	  
one	  of	  the	  most	  frequent	  file	  formats	  to	  display	  and	  exchange	  
3D	  geometry	  between	  different	  3D	  modelling	  software.	  The	  
file	  format	  only	  includes	  essential	  geometry,	  texture	  and	  
lighting	  data.	  	  	  	  
buildingSMART	  
International	  organisation	  that	  focuses	  on	  information	  
exchange	  between	  different	  platforms	  used	  in	  the	  AEC	  
industry.	  buildingSMART	  developed	  the	  IFC	  neutral	  file	  
format.	  Was	  formed	  in	  1994	  as	  International	  Alliance	  for	  
Interoperability.	  	  
DIALux	  
German	  freeware	  light	  simulation	  software,	  widely	  
acknowledged	  by	  the	  lighting	  industry	  for	  its	  simplicity	  and	  
user-­‐friendly	  interface.	  
DWG	  
Binary	  file	  format	  for	  several	  CAD	  packages	  storing	  design	  
data	  in	  both	  2D	  and	  3D.	  Native	  file	  format	  for	  AutoCAD	  
developed	  by	  Autodesk.	  	  	  
.ies	  
File	  extension	  of	  IESNA	  Photometric	  file.	  Contains	  data	  about	  
3D	  light	  distribution	  and	  intensities.	  	  
.ifc	  
Industry	  Foundation	  Classes	  (IFC)	  native	  file	  format.	  	  
.ldt	  
File	  extension	  for	  EULUMDAT	  Photometric	  file.	  Industry	  
standard	  photometric	  data	  file	  in	  Europe.	  It	  contains	  data	  
about	  light	  distribution	  and	  intensities	  in	  3D	  space.	  	  
Lumen	  
SI	  unit	  for	  luminous	  flux.	  Measure	  of	  the	  amount	  of	  visible	  light	  
emitted	  by	  a	  light	  source.	  	  
.gbXML	  
Open	  schema	  used	  to	  transfer	  building	  data	  stored	  in	  BIM	  
models	  and	  affiliated	  software.	  Usually	  used	  when	  transferring	  
data	  from	  the	  modelling	  tool	  into	  other	  simulation	  software.	  
‘gb’	  stands	  for	  Green	  Building.	  	  
Relux	  
Swiss-­‐made	  freeware	  light	  simulation	  software	  very	  similar	  to	  
DIALux.	  However,	  less	  frequently	  used	  due	  to	  its	  smaller	  
catalogue	  of	  available	  light	  fixtures.	  	  
Revit	  
Short	  for	  Autodesk	  Revit.	  Revit	  is	  the	  most	  used	  BIM	  software	  
in	  the	  AEC	  industry	  and	  allows	  both	  architects	  and	  engineers	  
to	  design	  in	  the	  same	  central	  model.	  	  	  
.rvt	  
Revit	  native	  file	  format	  
.stf	  
STF	  file	  extension	  developed	  by	  Microsoft.	  It	  initiates	  actions	  
based	  on	  plain	  text	  formatting.	  A	  plugin	  for	  Revit	  can	  export	  
models	  to	  STF	  file,	  which	  can	  be	  read	  by	  lighting	  simulation	  
software	  DIALux.	  	  
Velux	  Visualizer	  
Simple	  tool	  for	  daylight	  simulations	  based	  on	  a	  3D	  geometry,	  
materials	  and	  geographical	  location.	  Devloped	  by	  the	  Velux	  
Group.
